Divine Attributes

Why Sensory Attributes Cannot Be Attributed to God
The author refutes the analogy from the five senses to God, asserting divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sensory attributes like smell, taste, and touch cannot be attributed to God, and why God is exalted above both them and their opposites.

Core Idea
God transcends both sensory attributes and their opposites.

This passage tackles an important objection: if God is living, shouldn't He possess the five senses and speech? The author shows that such an analogy breaks down because conditions required for these attributes in creation (like bodily contact) do not apply to God. Furthermore, what is perfection in the created world may be deficiency in the Divine. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its flow. Then read the translation. Notice how the author structures the argument: statement, objection, reply, principle.
The passage contains a dialectical exchange between the author and an implied opponent.
2
Identify the opponent's analogy
The opponent argues that since God is living, He must possess the five senses and speech, just as living beings in the witnessed world do. Find the sentence that presents this view.
Look for 'وذلك أن الحي يصح منه الاتصاف بالحواس الخمس'.
3
Understand the rebuttal
The author rejects the analogy by stating that we should not say God has the opposites if He lacks them; rather, God is exalted above both. Then the author turns the opponent's own condition (contact of bodies) against them, showing that similar conditions (structure, contact) are required for hearing, sight, and speech in the witnessed world.
This refutes the analogy by pointing out that the conditions are not transferable.
4
Grasp the general principle
The author ends with a key principle: what is perfection in the witnessed world may be deficiency in the unseen. This undermines the assumption that sensory attributes are perfections that God must have.
This principle is important for many arguments about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك أن الحي يصح منه الاتصاف بالحواس الخمس، ثم الثلاث منها كالشم والذوق واللمس يصح في الشاهد ويصح وصفه بها والاتصاف بها، كما يصح منه الاتصاف بالسمع والبصر والكلام، ثم لم يجز طرد ذلك كله في الغائب، ولا يقال: إنه لو لم يتصف بها كان موصوفا بضدها، بل يجب أن يقال: يتعالى الحق عنها وعن أضدادها، كذلك قولنا في السمع والبصر والكلام واعتذاركم أن اتصال الأجرام بتلك الحواس شرط في الشاهد، كذلك اعتذارنا أن البنية واتصال الأجرام واصطكاكها شرط في الشاهد، فلا يجوز أن يوصف الحي بها ولا بضدها جميعًا، وكثيرًا ما يكون من الصفات كمالا في الشاهد ونقصانا في الغائب، فبقيت الحجة الأولى عرية عن البرهان.
And that is because the living being can be described by the five senses; then three of them, like smell, taste, and touch, are valid in the witnessed world (shāhid) and it is valid to describe him by them and to be described by them, just as it is valid for him to be described by hearing, sight, and speech. Then it is not permissible to apply all of that to the unseen (ghāʼib). And it is not said: If He were not described by them, He would be described by their opposites. Rather, it must be said: The Truth is exalted above them and above their opposites. Likewise our statement regarding hearing, sight, and speech: your excuse that the contact of bodies with those senses is a condition in the witnessed world, likewise our excuse that structure (al-bunya), contact of bodies, and their collision is a condition in the witnessed world. So it is not permissible to describe the Living (al-Ḥayy) by them nor by their opposites all together. And often attributes are perfection in the witnessed world but deficiency in the unseen. Thus the first argument remains devoid of proof.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the argument that because God is living, He must possess the five senses and speech, based on analogy from the witnessed world (shāhid) to the unseen (ghāʼib). The author begins by stating that a living being can be described by the five senses, but three of them (smell, taste, touch) are valid in the witnessed world, as are hearing, sight, and speech. However, the opponent cannot simply transfer all these attributes to God. The author argues that we should not say God would be described by their opposites if He lacked them; rather, God is exalted above both the attributes and their opposites. The author then turns the opponent's own objection against them: if the opponent excuses themselves by saying that contact of bodies with the senses is a condition in the witnessed world (and thus not applicable to God), the author replies that conditions like bodily structure, contact, and collision are also required for hearing, sight, and speech in the witnessed world. Therefore, it is not permissible to describe God by these attributes or their opposites. The author adds a general principle: what is perfection in the witnessed world may be deficiency in the unseen. Hence the opponent's first argument remains unproven.

Key Terms
الحواس الخمس
الشاهد
الغائب
الضد
التعالي
الكمال
النقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because God is living, He must possess the five senses and speech, just as living beings do in the witnessed world.
Correction: God is transcendent and exalted above both sensory attributes and their opposites; the conditions required for these attributes in creation (like bodily structure) do not apply to God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should we say about God in relation to the attributes of smell, taste, and touch?
A) He possesses them because He is living.
B) He is described by their opposites.
C) He is exalted above them and their opposites.
D) He possesses them in a manner befitting His majesty.

Key Takeaway
Sensory attributes cannot be attributed to God because He is exalted above them and their opposites, and what is perfection in creation may be deficiency in the Divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God’s hearing, do you unconsciously imagine something like a human ear? How does this passage challenge you to purify your concept of God? What specific practice will you adopt to remind yourself that God is beyond all sensory attributes?
?
The passage says what is perfection in creation can be deficiency in the Creator. Name one quality you admire in yourself (e.g., patience, strength) and reflect on how it would be a limitation if attributed to God. How does that realization deepen your awe of Him?
?
If God is beyond both having and lacking the five senses, what does that mean for your prayer life? Are you content to worship a God you cannot imagine with your senses? Commit this week to spending five minutes meditating on one of God’s names that emphasizes His transcendence (like al-ʿAlī, al-ʿAẓīm) without visualizing anything.

God's Command Is Creative, Not Verbal
Author refutes the view of God's command as speech and defends it as creative act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's command and prohibition are not literal speech but effective creative acts, and how the author supports this with rational argument and Qur'anic examples.

Core Idea
God's command is creative action, not human speech.

The author responds to the mutakallimūn who insist that God's command must be a literal speech attribute. He accepts that God is a king obeyed, but argues that His command is an effective act (fi‘lī) that creates necessary knowledge in the commanded. This passage defends a view of divine action that is direct and creative, not mediated through verbal language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The mutakallimūn assert that God's command is a speech attribute. Find where the author acknowledges this and then refines it.
2
Understand the author's alternative
The author says God's command is 'effective not verbal.' Notice how he defines it as creating necessary knowledge in the commanded.
This is a shift from speech to action.
3
Follow the Qur'anic examples
The author gives three examples: the command to heaven and earth, the travel in the land, and the creative 'Be.' See how each illustrates that God's 'speech' is really His directing of creation.
These examples are key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم: إن الرب تعالى ملك مطاع ذو أمر ونهي فمسلم، لكنكم معاشر المتكلمين أنتم منازعون في إثبات الكلام له هو أمر ونهي على وجه يتصف به إما قياما بذاته وإما قياما بغيره فإنا نقول: هو ملك مطاع وله أمر ونهي لا على طريق قولي بل على طريق فعلي، وهو تعريف المأمور خبرا أن الفعل المأمور به واجب الإقدام عليه بأن يخلق له معرفة ضرورية أن الأمر كذلك إما أن يتكلم بكلام يخلقه في محل، أو يتكلم أزلا وأبدًا بكلام يسمع في حال ولا يسمع في حال فهو من أمحل المحال، وهذا لأن تصريفه تعالى جواهر الخلائق بالفعل على وجه ينقاد له طوعًا وكرها لما خلقه له وقدره فيه نازلا منزلة القول، ألستم تقرون في كتابكم: {ثُمَّ اسْتَوَى إِلَى السَّمَاءِ وَهِيَ دُخَانٌ فَقَالَ لَهَا وَلِلأَرْضِ ائْتِيَا طَوْعًا أَوْ كَرْهًا قَالَتَا أَتَيْنَا طَائِعِينَ} [فصلت: 11] ثم ذلك ليس خطابا قوليا بل تصريفا وتسخيرا، بحيث لو عبر عن تلك الحالة من التصريف والانقياد كان ذلك أمراً بالإتيان وجوابا بالطاعة لا بالعصيان، وعلى ذلك المنهاج سيرة العباد في البلاد وتيسيره السير فيها حالة لو عير عنها بالقول لكان خطابًا لهم: {سِيرُوا فِيهَا لَيَالِيَ وَأَيَّامًا آمِنِينَ} [سبأ: 18]. وكذلك كل موجود أبدعه الباري تعالى بلا زمان ولا مادة ولا آلة؛ بحيث لو عبر عن حال الإيجاد والإبداع كان ذلك أمراً بالتكون كما قال: {إِنَّمَا قَوْلُنَا لِشَيْءٍ إِذَا أَرَدْنَاهُ أَنْ نَقُولَ لَهُ كُنْ فَيَكُونُ} [النحل: 40]، وإلا فالخطاب القولي مستحيل أن يتوجه إلى العدم، وهذا في المبدأ كذلك نقول في حال الكمال؛ أعني كمال حال الإنسان من النبوة والرسالة، أو كمال حال الملائكة من الروح والواسطة، فهو يصلون إلى حال في النفوس الشريفة والمناسبات اللطيفة بين الأرواح المجسدة المشخصة وبين الروحانيات البسيطة المجردة؛ بحيث لو عبر عن تلك الحالة كان ذلك وحيا وتنزيلا وخطابًا وسؤالًا وجوابًا وتقريبا وإيجابا.
As for your statement: The Lord, exalted is He, is a king obeyed, possessing command and prohibition, it is accepted. However, you group of mutakallimūn are contesting the affirmation of speech (kalām) for Him which is command and prohibition in a way that He is described by it, either as subsisting in His essence or subsisting in something else. For we say: He is a king obeyed, and He has command and prohibition not in a verbal manner (qawlī) but in an effective manner (fi‘lī). And it is a definition of the commanded (al-ma’mūr) by which it is known informatively that the commanded act is obligatory to undertake, by His creating for him a necessary knowledge that the command is thus. Either He speaks with speech that He creates in a locus, or He speaks eternally and forever with speech that is heard in one state and not heard in another – that is among the most impossible of impossibilities. This is because His, exalted is He, directing (taṣrīf) the substances of creatures by deed, in a way that they submit to Him willingly and unwillingly, for what He created and decreed for them, takes the place of speech. Do you not acknowledge in your book: {Then He turned to the heaven while it was smoke and said to it and to the earth: 'Come willingly or unwillingly.' They said: 'We come willingly.'} [Fussilat 41:11] Then that is not a verbal address but a directing and subduing, such that if that state of directing and submission were expressed, it would be a command to come and a response of obedience, not of disobedience. And similarly, the practice of servants in the lands and His facilitating their travel in them – a state such that if it were expressed by speech, it would be an address to them: {Travel through them nights and days, secure.} [Saba’ 34:18]. Likewise every existent that the Creator originates without time, matter, or instrument, such that if the state of origination (ījād) and creation (ibdā‘) were expressed, it would be a command to come into being, as He said: {Our only word to a thing when We intend it is that We say to it 'Be' and it is.} [An-Nahl 16:40]. Otherwise, verbal address is impossible to be directed to non-existence. And this is in the beginning. Similarly we say in the state of perfection; that is, the perfection of the human state from prophethood (nubuwwa) and messengership (risāla), or the perfection of the angels from spirit (rūḥ) and medium (wāsiṭa). They reach a state, in noble souls and subtle relationships between embodied particular spirits and simple abstract spiritualities, such that if that state were expressed, it would be revelation (waḥy), sending down (tanzīl), address (khitāb), question (su’āl), answer (jawāb), drawing near (taqrīb), and obligation (ījāb).
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the mutakallimūn who claim that God's command and prohibition must be a speech attribute subsisting in His essence or in some other locus. The author first concedes that God is a king obeyed with command and prohibition, but then insists that this command is not verbal (qawlī) but effective (fi‘lī). The author explains that God's 'command' is actually His creating in the commanded a necessary knowledge that a certain act is obligatory. The author then dismisses the two possibilities of God's speech: either He creates speech in a locus (which would make it created) or He speaks eternally yet is heard only sometimes (which is absurd). Rather, God's directing of the substances of creation by His will is equivalent to speech. The author provides Qur’anic examples: when God said to the heaven and earth 'Come,' it was not a verbal address but a creative act of subduing. Similarly, when He 'said' to the earth 'Travel through it,' it was a facilitation of movement. The author extends this to all creation: God's creative word 'Be' is not a literal utterance but a description of His instantaneous bringing into existence. Finally, the author applies this to the perfection of prophets and angels: their reception of revelation is a subtle spiritual state that, if expressed, would be called address, question, answer, etc., but it is not literal speech.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
قولي
فعلي
كلام
معرفة ضرورية
تصريف
تسخير
وحي
تنزيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that God's command must be a literal spoken word like human speech.
Correction: God's command is not verbal but an effective act that creates necessary knowledge and directs creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does God make His command known to the commanded?
A) By creating speech that He hears directly.
B) By creating in him a necessary knowledge that the act is obligatory.
C) By sending an angel to speak to him verbally.
D) By inscribing the command in the heart.

Key Takeaway
God's command is His effective act of creating necessary knowledge and directing creation, not a literal speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'Be and it is,' do you truly believe that God's command is instant and not like your own speech? Or does your heart imagine God speaking with a voice? What does that reveal about your concept of divine power?
?
If God's command to heaven and earth was a subduing action, not words, how does that change your understanding of how God speaks to you through circumstances? Are you listening to His effective commands in your life?
?
In what ways do you still expect God to 'speak' to you in human-like words, and how might that expectation be limiting your trust in His effective guidance?

The Opponents' Argument: God's Speech Must Be Like Human Speech
Author presents the beginning of an objection against divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the objection that God's speech must be of the same genus as human speech, and the two subcategories (linguistic and psychological) used to force a dilemma.

Core Idea
The opponents force a dilemma: if God speaks, His speech must be like human speech—either spoken or inner.

In this lesson, we encounter an objection raised by a group (likely the Asharites or others) against the divine attribute of speech. They argue that if God truly has speech, it must fall into the same categories as human speech: either linguistic (uttered by tongue) or psychological (inner speech). This sets up a dilemma that the author will later refute. Let's examine the logical structure of this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical progression: a division into two main branches, then a sub-division.
The passage is incomplete; it ends before the conclusion.
2
Identify the two main possibilities
The opponents posit two possibilities: either God's speech is like human speech or not. If it is like human speech, then it must be either linguistic or psychological.
These are the categories used in human language.
3
Understand the dilemma
The opponents aim to show that both options are impossible for God, hence God cannot have speech. The missing conclusion is that God's speech cannot be linguistic (because God has no tongue) nor psychological (because that would be a created accident).
This lesson only presents the structure; the refutation is in a subsequent passage.
4
Reflect on the key terms
Note the terms 'jins' (genus), 'kalām lisānī' (linguistic speech), and 'nuṭq nafsānī' (psychological speech). These are crucial for understanding the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا أيضا: لو قدر للباري تعالى كلام فلم يخل من أحد الأمرين؛ إما أن كان من جنس كلام البشر أو لم يكن، فإن كان لم يخل أيضًا من أحد الأمرين إما أن كان من جنس الكلام اللساني، أو من جنس النطق النفساني، ويستحيل أن يكون كلامه...
And they also said: If speech (kalām) were possible for the Creator, exalted is He, it would not be free from one of two matters: either it is of the genus (jins) of human speech or it is not. If it is, then it would also not be free from one of two matters: either it is of the genus of linguistic speech (kalām lisānī) or of the genus of psychological speech (nuṭq nafsānī). And it is impossible that His speech be...
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the opponents (likely the Asharites or other school) who argue that if God has speech, it must be either like human speech or not like it. If it is like human speech, then it must be either linguistic (spoken with tongue) or psychological (inner speech). The text cuts off before the author provides the response, but the beginning of the argument is given. The opponents presumably go on to argue that both are impossible for God, hence God does not have speech. The author will later refute this. The passage ends mid-sentence with 'ويستحيل أن يكون كلامه' (And it is impossible that His speech be...). This lesson captures the opening of this objection.

Key Terms
كلام
جنس
كلام لساني
نطق نفساني
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that all speech must fall into the categories of human speech (linguistic or psychological).
Correction: God's speech is eternal and not composed of letters or sounds; it does not fit human categories. The opponents' error is to impose human categories on the divine.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two possibilities if God's speech is of the genus of human speech?
A) It is either created or eternal.
B) It is either linguistic or psychological.
C) It is either audible or inaudible.
D) It is either command or prohibition.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' argument forces a dilemma: if God has speech, it must be either linguistic or psychological, both of which they deem impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's speech, do you unconsciously imagine it as sounding like your own words or thoughts? Will you stop and realize the limitation of that comparison?
?
The opponents force a human-constructed dilemma onto divine speech. In what areas of your faith have you unknowingly boxed Allah's attributes into human categories? Commit to identifying one such area today.
?
If the author will soon refute this argument by showing that divine speech belongs to a different genus, what does that tell you about the danger of relying solely on your own logic when it comes to Allah? How can you guard against that in your daily reflection?

Proving Divine Speech from the Nature of Command
Mutakallimun present their method of proving divine speech from laws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's method of proving divine speech as an attribute distinct from knowledge, power, and will using the existence of legal obligations.

Core Idea
Divine speech is proven by the distinctive nature of command itself.

The mutakallimun developed a rigorous method for establishing the divine attributes by observing patterns in the created world. Just as they proved knowledge from rulings, power from actions, and will from specification, they proved divine speech from the phenomenon of obligation (taklif). In this passage, they present this method and then carefully distinguish divine command from other attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overarching method
Read how the mutakallimun infer attributes from witnessed evidences: knowledge from rulings, power from actions, will from specification, and speech from obligations.
The same pattern is applied consistently.
2
See how obligations prove divine command
Legal obligations require a commander, and command is of the genus of speech, not action. The Quranic verse separates creation and command.
Command is an intelligible reality beyond other attributes.
3
Examine the three distinctions
The author explains how command differs from knowledge (knowledge relates to all, command only to what is commanded), from power (power relates to all possibles equally), and from will (will relates to one's own act, command to another's act).
Each contrast shows why command is a unique attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المتكلمون : الطريقة المختارة عندنا في إثبات الصفات الاستدلال بالدلائل التي نجدها في الشاهد، فإنا بالأحكام استدللنا على العلم، ويوقوع الأفعال على القدرة، وبالاختصاص ببعض الجائزات على الإرادة كذلك نستدل بالتكاليف على العباد ذوي العقول والاستطاعة على أن الباري تعالى آمر ناه، وله الأمر والنهي والوعد على إتيان المأمور به والوعيد على فعل المنهي عنه، وهذا لا يتصور من جنس الفعل، وإنما يتحقق من جنس القول فيستدل بالوجوه التي في الصنائع والأفعال على أنه تعالى خالق وبالوجوه التي في الشرائع والأحكام على أنه تعالى آمر ( أَلَا لَهُ الخَلْقُ وَالْأَمْرُ تَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ رَبُّ الْعَالَمِينَ ﴾ [الأعراف : ٢٥٤]. ووجه الاستدلال من الشرائع والأحكام أن التكليف اقتضاء وطلب المأمور والاقتضاء قضية معقولة وراء العلم والقدرة والإرادة، أما وجه المباينة بينها وبين العلم أن العلم يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو به وليس في تعلقه اقتضاء المعلوم، بل هو تبيين وانكشاف ومن خاصيته أن يتعلق بالواجب والجائز والمستحيل، والاقتضاء والأمر لا يتعلق بالواجب والمستحيل، وأما وجه مباينته للقدرة أن القدرة صالحة للإيجاد فقط، وإنما تتعلق بالممكن ويستوي في تعلقه كل ممكن في ذاته ، والاقتضاء والأمر والنهي لا يتعلق بكل ممكن ليحصل وجوده. وأما وجه مباينته الإرادة أن الإرادة صالحة للتخصيص ببعض الجائزات فقط وإنما تتعلق بالمتجدد من جملة الممكن، ويستوي في تعلقه كل متجدد متخصص ، والاقتضاء والطلب يختلف من جهة تجدده، وقد بينا أن من الأشياء ما يراد ولا يؤمر به ومنها ما يؤمر به ولا يراد، بل الإرادة من حيث الحقيقة لا تتعلق إلا بفعل المريد، والأمر والاقتضاء لا يتعلق من حيث الحقيقة إلا بفعل الغير فكيف
The mutakallimun said: The chosen method with us in establishing the attributes is inference by the evidences that we find in the witnessed. For we infer by the rulings (ahkam) to [the attribute of] knowledge, and by the occurrence of actions to power, and by specification of some possible things to will. Likewise we infer by the obligations (takalif) upon rational and capable servants that the Exalted Creator is a commander and a prohibitor, and that He has command and prohibition, and promise upon performing commanded acts and threat upon committing prohibited acts. And this is not conceivable to be of the genus of action, but rather it is actualized from the genus of speech. So we infer by the aspects in the crafts and actions that He, exalted, is a Creator, and by the aspects in the laws and rulings that He, exalted, is a Commander. (Indeed, His is the creation and the command; blessed is Allah, Lord of the worlds.) [Al-A'raf:54]. And the aspect of inference from the laws and rulings is that obligation (taklif) is a requiring and a requesting of the commanded thing, and the requiring is an intelligible reality beyond knowledge, power, and will. As for the aspect of its distinction from knowledge: knowledge relates to the known as it is, and in its relation there is no requiring of the known, rather it is elucidation and disclosure, and among its properties is that it relates to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, while requiring and command do not relate to the necessary and the impossible. As for the aspect of its distinction from power: power is suitable only for bringing into existence, and it relates only to the possible, and in its relation every possible is equal in itself, while requiring, command, and prohibition do not relate to every possible to bring about its existence. As for the aspect of its distinction from will: will is suitable only for specification of some possible things, and it relates only to that which renews from among the possible, and in its relation every renewing specified thing is equal, while requiring and requesting differ in terms of its renewal. And we have already explained that among things are what is willed but not commanded, and among them are what is commanded but not willed. Rather, will in reality relates only to the act of the willer, while command and requiring in reality relate only to the act of another. So how...
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's preferred method for proving the divine attributes, focusing on the attribute of speech (kalam). They argue that just as we infer knowledge from the existence of well-ordered rulings, power from the occurrence of actions, and will from the specification of possibilities, we also infer that God is an issuer of commands and prohibitions from the existence of legal obligations (takalif) imposed upon rational and capable beings. These obligations are not of the genus of action but of speech. The author then cites the Quranic verse (Al-A'raf:54) that separates creation (al-khalq) from command (al-amr). He then explains the distinction between divine command and other attributes: (1) Command is distinct from knowledge because knowledge merely discloses the known as it is, whereas command requires and requests; knowledge can relate to necessary and impossible things, whereas command cannot. (2) Command is distinct from power because power is only about bringing possibilities into existence equally, whereas command and prohibition are selective and not applied to all possibilities. (3) Command is distinct from will because will relates only to one's own act and is for specification, whereas command relates to the act of another; moreover, what is willed is not always commanded and vice versa. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument continues to show that divine speech is a unique attribute beyond these others.

Key Terms
المتكلمون
الصفات
الأحكام
الاستدلال
التكليف
الأمر والنهي
الاقتضاء
العلم
القدرة
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might confuse divine command with divine will or power.
Correction: The passage shows that command is distinct: it relates to another's act, it is selective, and it does not apply to necessary or impossible things, unlike knowledge, power, and will.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, what evidence do they use to prove that God is a commander (amir)?
A) The occurrence of natural phenomena.
B) The existence of legal obligations on rational beings.
C) The innate knowledge of good and evil.
D) The creation of the world.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun demonstrate that divine speech is a distinct attribute essential for explaining the phenomenon of obligation, as command cannot be reduced to knowledge, power, or will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a command from Allah, do you treat it as a mere suggestion or as a unique demand that cannot be reduced to your own desires? How does this distinction change your response?
?
The passage shows that command relates to another's act, not the self. In what areas of your life have you been treating God's commands as if they were your own will? What would it take to submit fully?
?
If you had previously confused God's will with His command, how might that have led you to excuse disobedience? Knowing now that they are distinct, what step will you take today to align your actions with His command over your own preferences?

The Distinctiveness of Divine Command
The author distinguishes divine command from knowledge, power, and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine command (amr) is a distinct attribute from knowledge, power, and will, and how it is inferred from the existence of laws and obligations.

Core Idea
Divine command is not reducible to other attributes; it is a distinct kind of speech.

This passage tackles a subtle but crucial theological question: is God's command just another aspect of His knowledge, power, or will? The author argues that it is not. He begins by noting that promise and threat are forms of speech, not actions, and then shows that the very idea of obligation (taklīf) implies a rational demand that goes beyond what we attribute to knowledge, power, or will. As you read, pay attention to the careful distinctions he draws.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once without stopping. Let the argument flow naturally.
The passage includes a Quranic verse (Al-A'raf 7:54) as evidence.
2
Identify the three distinctions
The author distinguishes command from knowledge, power, and will. Find each argument and notice how he contrasts the properties of each attribute with the properties of command.
He uses the term 'demand' (iqtiḍā') to characterize command.
3
Understand the final conclusion
After showing that command is distinct from the three, the author concludes that obligations signify a reality beyond them, which he calls speech (qawl/kalām) and which revelation calls command (amr) and address (khiṭāb).
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that command is just a form of will. The author argues they differ because will relates to the agent's own act, while command relates to another's act.
This is a key point for kalam theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوعد على إتيان المأمور به والوعيد على فعل المنهي عنه، وهذا لا يتصور من جنس الفعل، وإنما يتحقق من جنس القول فيستدل بالوجوه التي في الصنائع والأفعال على أنه تعالى خالق وبالوجوه التي في الشرائع والأحكام على أنه تعالى آمر ( أَلَا لَهُ الخَلْقُ وَالْأَمْرُ تَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ رَبُّ الْعَالَمِينَ ) ووجه الاستدلال من الشرائع والأحكام أن التكليف اقتضاء وطلب المأمور والاقتضاء قضية معقولة وراء العلم والقدرة والإرادة، أما وجه المباينة بينها وبين العلم أن العلم يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو به وليس في تعلقه اقتضاء المعلوم، بل هو تبيين وانكشاف ومن خاصيته أن يتعلق بالواجب والجائز والمستحيل، والاقتضاء والأمر لا يتعلق بالواجب والمستحيل، وأما وجه مباينته للقدرة أن القدرة صالحة للإيجاد فقط، وإنما تتعلق بالممكن ويستوي في تعلقه كل ممكن في ذاته، والاقتضاء والأمر والنهي لا يتعلق بكل ممكن ليحصل وجوده. وأما وجه مباينته الإرادة أن الإرادة صالحة للتخصيص ببعض الجائزات فقط وإنما تتعلق بالمتجدد من جملة الممكن، ويستوي في تعلقه كل متجدد متخصص، والاقتضاء والطلب يختلف من جهة تجدده، وقد بينا أن من الأشياء ما يراد ولا يؤمر به ومنها ما يؤمر به ولا يراد، بل الإرادة من حيث الحقيقة لا تتعلق إلا بفعل المريد، والأمر والاقتضاء لا يتعلق من حيث الحقيقة إلا بفعل الغير فكيف تتحدان حقيقة وخاصية، فثبت أن مدلول التكاليف من حيث الحدود والأحكام قضية وراء العلم والقدرة والإرادة، وذلك ما عبرنا عنه بالقول والكلام، وعبر التنزيل عنه بالأمر والخطاب.
And the promise (al-wa'd) for performing the commanded and the threat (al-wa'id) for committing the forbidden. This is not conceivable as a kind of action, but rather is realized as a kind of speech. So one infers from the aspects found in acts and deeds that He, exalted, is Creator, and from the aspects found in laws and rulings that He, exalted, is Commander. (Indeed, His is the creation and the command; blessed is Allah, Lord of the worlds) (Al-A'raf 54). The way of inference from laws and rulings is that obligation (taklīf) is a demand (iqtiḍā') and request for the commanded thing, and the demand is a rational reality beyond knowledge (al-ʿilm), power (al-qudra), and will (al-irāda). As for the aspect of its distinction from knowledge: knowledge relates to the known as it is, and its relation does not involve a demand for the known; rather it is manifestation and disclosure, and its property is that it relates to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible. But demand and command do not relate to the necessary and the impossible. As for its distinction from power: power is fit only for bringing into existence, and it relates only to the possible, and every possible is equal in its relation. But demand, command, and prohibition do not relate to every possible so as to bring about its existence. As for its distinction from will: will is fit only for specification of some possibles, and it relates only to contingent things among the possible, and every contingent specified thing is equal in its relation. But demand and request differ with respect to their contingency. And we have shown that some things are willed but not commanded, and some are commanded but not willed. Rather, will in reality relates only to the act of the willing agent, whereas command and demand in reality relate only to the act of another. So how can they be identical in reality and property? Thus it is established that the signification of obligations (takālīf) in terms of limits and rulings is a reality beyond knowledge, power, and will, and that is what we have expressed as speech (al-qawl) and discourse (al-kalām), and the revelation expressed it as command (al-amr) and address (al-khiṭāb).
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine command (amr) is a distinct attribute from knowledge, power, and will. The author begins by noting that promise and threat are not actions but speech. He then infers that from the created order we know God as Creator, and from laws we know Him as Commander (citing Quran 7:54). He explains that obligation (taklīf) is a rational reality of demand beyond those three attributes. He distinguishes demand from knowledge: knowledge merely discloses the known without demanding it, and relates to all categories (necessary, possible, impossible), whereas demand excludes necessary and impossible. From power: power is for bringing about any possible, equally, but demand specifies only some possibilities. From will: will relates to the agent's own act, whereas command relates to another's act; furthermore, some things are willed but not commanded, and vice versa. Therefore command cannot be identical to will. The author concludes that the reality of obligations is a distinct entity, which he calls speech/kalām, and revelation calls it command and address.

التكليف (al-taklīf)
الاقتضاء (al-iqtiḍā')
العلم (al-ʿilm)
القدرة (al-qudra)
الإرادة (al-irāda)
القول (al-qawl)
الكلام (al-kalām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine command is simply an expression of God's will, reducible to the attribute of will.
Correction: The author shows that will relates to the agent's own act, whereas command relates to another's act. Moreover, some things are willed but not commanded (e.g., natural events), and some commanded but not willed (e.g., obligations that are not fulfilled). Therefore command is distinct from will.

Checkpoint

A) Will relates only to the possible, command only to the necessary.
B) Will relates to the agent's own act, while command relates to another's act.
C) Will is eternal, command is temporal.
D) Will is known by reason, command only by revelation.

Key Takeaway
Divine command (amr) is a distinct attribute, a kind of speech (kalām), separate from knowledge, power, and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If divine command is distinct from God's will, then His command to you is not simply a reflection of what He has willed for you—it is a direct address. When you find a command hard to obey, are you treating it as an optional suggestion rather than a personal summons from Allah to you? What would change if you heard it as His speech to you right now?
?
The author argues that command relates only to the act of another, not the agent. Allah's commands are for your benefit, not His. How does that reframe your resistance to them? Will you continue to see His commands as burdens, or will you embrace them as gifts tailored for your growth?
?
Consider a command from Allah that you have been neglecting. Is there a part of you that excuses yourself by thinking, 'If Allah really wanted me to do it, He would make it easy for me'? How does the distinction between command and will challenge that excuse? What is one concrete step you will take today to respond to His command as a direct address from your Lord?

Three Voluntary Movements and Divine Command
The author argues that three voluntary movements indicate divine judgment and command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three types of human voluntary movements (mental, verbal, physical) indicate Allah's judgment and command.

Core Idea
Every voluntary act reveals Allah's command because it carries moral weight.

In this passage, the author builds a second argument for the divine attribute of command. He examines human voluntary actions—thinking, speaking, and doing—and shows that each involves moral categories that require a divine judgment. This judgment cannot be an action itself but must be a command or prohibition. The argument connects human moral experience to divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Learn the three types of voluntary movement
Read the passage and identify the three types: mental, verbal, and physical. Notice that each involves moral distinctions (truth/falsehood, good/evil, etc.).
2
Understand that each movement has a divine judgment
The author asserts that Allah has a judgment (ḥukm) for every voluntary movement. This judgment is either an act or a statement, but it cannot be an act because every act is preceded by judgment.
3
See the analogy with necessary movements
Just as involuntary movements indicate God's knowledge, will, and power, voluntary movements indicate His judgment, command, and decree.
4
Consider the objection and response
The author addresses an objection that God's management by action is like obligation by speech, but he distinguishes voluntary from involuntary actions.
The text is cut off before the full response is given.

ومن وجه آخر نقول : الحركات الاختيارية التي اختصت بتصرفات الإنسان ثلاث ؛ حركة فكرية وهي من تصرفات عقله وفكره وحركة قولية وهي من تصرفات نفسه ولسانه وحركة فعلية وهي من تصرفات بدنه والقوى البدنية، وما من حركة من هذه الحركات إلا والله فيها حكم بالأمر والنهي، فإن الفكرية تشتمل على حجة وشبهة وصواب وخطأ وحق وباطل والقولية تشتمل على صدق وكذب، وإقرار وإنكار وحكمة وسفه والفعلية تشتمل على خير وشر، وطاعة ومعصية، وصلاح وفساد، وقد قام الدليل على أن الباري تعالى حكم عدل، وله حكم وقضية في كل حركة من هذه الحركات، وذلك الحكم إما أن يكون فعلا من الأفعال، وإما أن يكون قولا من الأقوال، واستحال أن يكون فعلا يفعله، فإن كل فعل فهو مسبوق بحكمه، بل ذلك الفعل دليل على حكمه، وحكمه مدلول فعله. وكما أن الحركات الضرورية دلت باختصاصها ببعض الجائزات دون البعض على علمه وإرادته وقدرته، كذلك الحركات الاختيارية دلت باختصاصها ببعض الأحكام دون البعض على حكمه وأمره وقضيته، وقولكم: إن التصريف بالفعل نازل منزلة التكليف بالقول مسلم فيما يرجع إلى أفعال غير اختيارية للإنسان لكن الأفعال الاختيارية لما استدعت ممن له الخلق والأمر حكما واقتضاء
From another aspect, we say: The voluntary movements that pertain to human actions are three: a mental movement (ḥaraka fikriyya), which is from the acts of his intellect and thought; a verbal movement (ḥaraka qawliyya), which is from the acts of his soul and tongue; and a physical movement (ḥaraka fiʿliyya), which is from the acts of his body and physical powers. There is no movement among these movements except that Allah has a judgment (ḥukm) concerning it by command (al-amr) and prohibition (al-nahy). For the mental movement includes proof and doubt, correctness and error, truth and falsehood; the verbal movement includes truth and falsehood, affirmation and denial, wisdom and foolishness; and the physical movement includes good and evil, obedience and disobedience, righteousness and corruption. The proof has been established that the Creator, exalted, is a just judge, and He has a judgment and decree (qaḍiyya) in every movement of these movements. That judgment is either an act among acts or a statement among statements. It is impossible that it be an act that He does, for every act is preceded by His judgment; rather, that act is evidence for His judgment, and His judgment is the signification of His act. Just as necessary movements indicated by their specification to some possibles over others His knowledge, will, and power, likewise voluntary movements indicated by their specification to some judgments over others His judgment, command, and decree. And your saying: 'The management by action is analogous to obligation by speech' is conceded regarding involuntary actions of man, but voluntary actions, since they require from Him to whom belongs creation and command a judgment and a demand...
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a second proof for the distinct attribute of divine command, this time based on the three types of human voluntary movements. The author classifies voluntary movements into mental, verbal, and physical. Each has moral dimensions (truth/falsehood, good/evil, etc.). He argues that God, as a just judge, has a judgment concerning each movement. This judgment cannot be an act, because every act is preceded by a judgment; rather, it must be a statement (i.e., command or prohibition). He then draws an analogy: just as necessary (involuntary) movements indicate God's knowledge, will, and power by their specification to certain possibles, voluntary movements indicate God's judgment, command, and decree by their specification to certain moral categories. The author responds to an objection that God's management by action is like obligation by speech, conceding it for involuntary actions but insisting that voluntary actions require a distinct judgment and demand. The text is cut off before the conclusion is completed.

Every Act of God Indicates His Judgment
The author argues that every act of God indicates His judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how every act of the Creator indicates His judgment (hukm) of command, prohibition, or permission.

Core Idea
God's actions are not silent—they speak His judgment.

This passage presents a key argument in Islamic theology: that every action of God carries a normative judgment. The author uses the act itself as evidence for God's attributes and then for His ruling on human actions. By analyzing the act's precision, occurrence, specification, and moral ambiguity, we can infer that God has a specific judgment for that act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise argumentative style.
2
Identify the four aspects of the act
The passage breaks down a divine act into four aspects: precision (إحكام), occurrence (وقوع), specification (اختصاص), and being between permissibility, prohibition, and permission (تردد بين الجواز والحظر والإباحة).
Each aspect leads to a different attribute.
3
Understand the inference to judgment
The last aspect—the act's moral ambiguity—directly indicates that God has a judgment (hukm) about it. That judgment can be a command, a prohibition, or a permission.
This is the core point the author wants to prove.
4
Notice the conclusion
The author ends by saying the opponent has conceded the issue. Reflect on why that conclusion follows from the argument.
The argument shows that denying judgment for acts is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطلبًا فكل فعل وتصريف من الخالق يدل على حكمه واقتضائه، وذلك بعينه هو غرضنا ومرادنا. فنقول : ذلك الفعل من وجه إحكامه دل على علم الفاعل، ومن وجه وقوعه دل على قدرته، ومن وجه اختصاصه دل على إرادته ومن وجه تردده بين الجواز والحظر والإباحة دل على أن للباري تعالى فيها حكمًا وقضية، ثم ذلك الحكم قد يكون أمراً وقد يكون نهيًا وقد يكون إباحة، فقد سلمتم المسألة من حيث منعتموها.
And seeking: every action and disposal from the Creator indicates His judgment and requirement, and that itself is our goal and intention. So we say: that action, from the aspect of its precision, indicates the knowledge of the Agent; from the aspect of its occurrence, indicates His power; from the aspect of its specification, indicates His will; and from the aspect of its being between permissibility, prohibition, and permission, indicates that the Exalted Creator has a judgment and decree concerning it. Then that judgment sometimes is a command, sometimes a prohibition, and sometimes a permission. So you have conceded the issue from where you denied it.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's actions indicate His judgment (hukm) regarding human actions. The author argues that every divine act has aspects that reveal God's attributes: the perfection of the act shows His knowledge, its occurrence shows His power, its specific nature shows His will, and its being between possible categories (permissible, prohibited, allowable) shows that God has a ruling about it. This ruling can be a command (amr), a prohibition (nahy), or a permission (ibaha). The author concludes that the opponent has unwittingly conceded the point they were denying.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm - judgment)
أمر (amr - command)
نهي (nahy - prohibition)
إباحة (ibaha - permission)
إرادة (irada - will)
قدرة (qudra - power)
علم (ilm - knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's actions are morally neutral and do not indicate any ruling.
Correction: This passage shows that the very nature of a divine act—its being subject to categories of permissibility, prohibition, and permission—indicates that God has a judgment about it.

Checkpoint

A) His knowledge
B) His power
C) His will
D) His judgment

Key Takeaway
The four aspects of any divine act point to God's knowledge, power, will, and judgment, making every act a source of divine law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What if the events of your life—both pleasant and painful—are divine messages telling you exactly what to do? Are you listening for the hukm in each moment, or are you treating them as random? Take one specific event this week and ask: is it a command, prohibition, or permission? Then respond accordingly.

Proof That Divine Speech Necessitates Messengers
The author links divine speech to the possibility of sending messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of divine speech, command, and prohibition proves the possibility of sending messengers.

Core Idea
Divine speech and command are the foundation for prophethood.

This lesson explores the logical connection between God's attribute of speech and the institution of prophethood. The author argues that if God can send a messenger, that messenger must convey commands and prohibitions, which are the very content of divine speech. Thus, denying God's speech would undermine the possibility of prophecy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to understand the flow of the argument.
Focus on the conditional statements.
2
Identify the logical link
Notice how the author connects divine speech to the possibility of sending messengers. The proof of speech is built upon the permissibility of sending messengers.
3
Differentiate command from will
The author argues that command (amr) is not merely will (irada); it includes a requirement and judgment. See how the passage refutes reducing command to will.
This is a key distinction against opponents.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that what the opponents call 'will' is actually what we call speech, command, and prohibition. This establishes the necessity of divine speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : إثبات الكلام والأمر والنهي يبني على جواز انبعاث الرسل، فإن من أحال كونه متكلما يلزمه أن ينكر كونه مرسلا، ومن جوز على الله تعالى أن يبعث رسولا فذلك الرسول يبلغ رسالات الله لا محالة، ورسالاته أوامره ونواهيه، وحملها على فعل يفعله في محل بحيث يفهم من ذلك الفعل أنه يريد من المكلف فعلا ويكره فعلا تسليم المسألة، فإن تلك الإرادة التي تتعلق بفعل الغير حتى يفعله إرادة تضمنت اقتضاء وحكماً، وإلا كانت تمنيًا وتشهيّا، وذلك الذي يسمى أمرا ونهيًا، وسميتموه إرادة وكراهية، فإذا مدلول ذلك الفعل الذي أشاروا إليه هو الذي نسميه الشرع كلاما وأمرًا ونهيًا.
So we say: establishing speech, command, and prohibition is built upon the permissibility of sending messengers. For whoever considers it impossible for Him to be speaking necessarily denies that He is sending a messenger. And whoever allows that God may send a messenger, that messenger inevitably conveys God's messages, and His messages are His commands and prohibitions. And interpreting them as an action He performs in a locus such that it is understood from that action that He desires the responsible person to do something and dislikes an action—this is a concession of the issue. For that will which relates to the action of another so that he does it is a will that includes requirement and judgment, otherwise it would be wishful thinking and desire. And that is what is called command and prohibition, though you have called it will and dislike. So the signification of that action which they indicated is what we call by the Law (shar') speech, command, and prohibition.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument connecting divine speech (kalam) to prophethood. The author states that the proof of God's speech, command, and prohibition is based on the possibility of sending messengers. If one denies that God can speak, he must also deny that God can send a messenger, because a messenger conveys speech. Conversely, if one accepts that God can send a messenger, that messenger must convey commands and prohibitions. These commands cannot be reduced to mere will or dislike, because mere will does not entail obligation; rather, they are acts that indicate divine requirement and judgment. The author concludes that what the opponents call 'will and dislike' is in fact the very thing we call speech, command, and prohibition.

Key Terms
كلام
أمر
نهي
رسل
إرادة
كراهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine command is merely a metaphor for God's will.
Correction: The author shows that command entails a distinct judgment (requirement and obligation) beyond mere will, which would only be wishful thinking.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what follows if one allows that God can send a messenger?
A) The messenger must be a human
B) The messenger conveys commands and prohibitions
C) God must have a physical body
D) Prophethood is impossible

Key Takeaway
The institution of prophethood logically requires that God possesses speech, command, and prohibition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated God's commands as mere suggestions or ideals, while in your heart you knew they were binding obligations? What would it mean for you to truly acknowledge divine speech as real, authoritative, and demanding a response?
?
When you read the Quran and encounter a command like 'establish prayer' or 'give zakah', do you feel the weight of divine requirement, or do you subconsciously reduce it to a general guideline? What change in your daily life would show that you believe these commands carry genuine judgment?
?
If you were to stand before God tomorrow and be asked about the messengers He sent, could you honestly say you accepted their message as authoritative and acted upon it? What specific command from the Quran are you currently neglecting, and what step will you take today to obey it?

The Rational Soul and Divine Command and Prohibition
Philosophical proof for divine command and prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that the existence of rational souls with free choice necessitates divine command and prohibition, as well as promise and threat.

Core Idea
The rational soul's capacity for choice demands divine judgment in this life and the next.

This passage presents a rational proof for the reality of divine commands and promises. The author argues that since rational souls can choose between good and evil, a wise creator must issue judgments corresponding to their choices — commands and prohibitions in this world, and promises and threats in the afterlife. Denying this would undermine God's sovereignty and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Focus on the distinction between the two judgments.
Notice the key terms: rational soul (nafs natiqa), command (amr), prohibition (nahy), promise (wa'd), threat (wa'id).
2
Identify the two judgments
The author presents a first judgment for the soul while embodied (command and prohibition) and a second judgment after death (promise and threat). Find where these are defined.
These correspond to laws in this life and recompense in the next.
3
Understand the consequence of denial
The author warns that denying these judgments leads to denying God's kingship, making all actions compulsory, and negating reward and punishment, which invalidates divine wisdom.
This shows the logical necessity of these judgments.
4
Reflect on the argument's structure
Outline the argument: rational souls with free choice exist -> they choose well or poorly -> a wise creator must issue judgments accordingly -> if not, then compulsion and no afterlife, contradicting wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And according to the methodology of philosophical judgments: when creation and making reach the point where balanced mixtures are prepared to receive the rational soul which thinks with deliberation and acts by choice, and among the select ones with this soul there are those who perceive truth and rightness by the better choice and avoidance of the baser, and among them there are those who are sluggish and lazy until that preparedness is ruined and they use it in the side of falsehood and error, and the choice of the baser and avoidance of the better must be from the wise a judgment upon this rational soul while it is clothed in this bodily body, and a judgment upon it when it separates from it. So that first judgment is what we mean by command and prohibition, and this second judgment is what we mean by promise and threat. And whoever denies this judgment has denied that He is a king possessing His kingdom; so He would have creation but no command, and on the side of the creature it would be all compulsion and no choice. Then it would necessitate that rational souls come into being and decay, having no return, no perfection, no recompense for their actions, and no reward. And that invalidates the judgment of wisdom. And we see from animal souls those which do not communicate with each other except by one of the four senses: touch, taste, smell, and sight; and among them those which communicate by these and also by hearing. Then among them are those which have mere sound without melody, and among them those which have with their sound a melody; then among the possessors of melodies are those which are of articulated letters, and among them those which are not, until the matter and condition reaches the formation of words from letters and the ordering of letters in words, so that becomes an indication of what is in the rational souls. Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents a philosophical proof for divine command and prohibition. The author argues that rational souls (nufus natiqa) have the capacity for choice between good and evil. The Wise Creator (al-hakim) must issue a judgment (hukm) for the soul while embodied (command and prohibition) and a judgment after separation (promise and threat). Denying this leads to denying God's sovereignty, making all actions compulsory, and negating reward and punishment, which invalidates divine wisdom. The author then draws an analogy from animal communication, which progresses from basic senses to sounds to melodies to articulated letters, showing that communication is a graded phenomenon culminating in human speech that indicates the contents of rational souls. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is that just as animal communication has levels, so does divine communication (speech, command, prohibition) have a reality beyond mere will.

Key Terms
نفس ناطقة (nafs natiqa - rational soul)
أمر (amr - command)
نهي (nahy - prohibition)
وعد (wa'd - promise)
وعيد (wa'id - threat)
حكمة (hikma - wisdom)
حكم (hukm - judgment)
اختيار (ikhtiyar - choice)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine commands and prohibitions are merely metaphorical or not real divine acts; they are only guidelines.
Correction: The author argues that command and prohibition are real judgments (hukm) issued by the Wise Creator upon the rational soul, based on its capacity for free choice. Denying their reality leads to logical absurdities that undermine God's wisdom and authority.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the first judgment (al-hukm al-awwal) that the wise (al-hakim) issues upon the rational soul while embodied?
A) Promise and threat
B) Command and prohibition
C) Knowledge and power
D) Reward and punishment

Key Takeaway
The wise Creator must issue command and prohibition for the embodied soul, and promise and threat for the separated soul, based on the soul's free choices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says some people are 'sluggish and lazy' until their preparedness for truth is ruined. Where in your life are you making excuses or delaying obedience to Allah's commands? What is one command you have been avoiding, and what will you do this week to act upon it?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is 'a King possessing His kingdom' and has the absolute right to command you, would you still resist any of His prohibitions? Which prohibition do you find difficult to accept, and how does this passage challenge that resistance?
?
Your choices today—whether you choose truth or falsehood—will be judged by the Wise and will result in either promise or threat. Is there a choice you are making right now that would be safer not to make if you remembered this? What change must you make before you meet your Lord?

Gradation in Animal Communication as Evidence for Divine Speech
Analogy from animal communication to affirm divine speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the gradation of animal communication, from simple senses to articulated speech, points to the reality of divine speech and communication.

Core Idea
Animal communication shows a hierarchy culminating in human speech, indicating the soul's content — a reflection of divine communication.

The author uses an empirical observation: animals communicate through progressively complex means, from touch to articulated letters. This gradation demonstrates that communication is a real attribute, not a metaphor. By analogy, divine communication (command, prohibition, etc.) must also be real and not merely metaphorical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on animal communication
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the different levels of communication described.
The passage is a comparison between animal and human communication.
2
List the levels of communication
Identify each level: (1) senses: touch, taste, smell, sight; (2) hearing added; (3) sound without melody; (4) sound with melody; (5) articulated letters; (6) words composed of letters.
This is a progression from simple to complex.
3
Connect to rational souls
The author says that words and letters indicate what is in the rational soul. This shows that communication can express inner states.
Humans have the highest form of communication.
4
Reflect on the analogy
Consider how this gradation supports the reality of divine speech. Just as animal communication is real and graded, divine speech (command, prohibition) must be a real attribute, not a metaphor.
The author is refuting those who deny divine speech as a real attribute.

The Two Judgments on the Rational Soul
Defines the two judgments on the rational soul: command/prohibition and promise/threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two divine judgments on the rational soul—command/prohibition in this life and promise/threat in the next—and why denying them leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
The rational soul is subject to two judgments that uphold divine wisdom and human accountability.

This passage from Shahrastani's Nihayat al-Aqdam sets out the foundational distinction between God's judgments on the soul while it is embodied (commands and prohibitions) and after it departs (promises and threats). The author argues that denying these judgments collapses the entire edifice of divine wisdom, reducing creation to compulsion and eliminating any basis for an afterlife. As you read, notice how the argument relies on the necessary connection between divine sovereignty and the two judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's own structure and rhythm come through before interpreting.
2
Identify the two judgments
The passage presents two distinct judgments on the rational soul. Find the definition of each and note the context (embodied vs. separate).
The first judgment is command and prohibition; the second is promise and threat.
3
Analyze the denial consequence
The author explains what follows from denying these judgments. Trace the logical chain: denial implies creation without command, then compulsion, then no afterlife.
Notice the phrase 'then it necessarily follows' which signals a logical consequence.
4
Reflect on the necessity
Ask yourself why the author considers this denial to 'invalidate the requirement of wisdom'. How does the argument establish the necessity of both judgments?
This connects to the broader theme of divine justice and human accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من المختارين بهذه النفس على روية الحق والصواب بالاختيار الأفضل والاجتناب عن الأرذل، ومنهم من يتوانى ويكسل حتى يبطل ذلك الاستعداد ويستعمله في جانب الباطل والخطأ، واختيار الأرذل والاجتناب عن الأفضل وجب أن يكون من عند الحكيم حكم على هذه النفس الناطقة وهي ملتبسة في هذا البدن الجسماني، وحكم عليها وهي مفارقة له فذلك الحكم الأول هو الذي نعني به بأنه أمر ونهي، وهذا الحكم الثاني هو الذي نعني به أنه وعد ووعيد، ومن نفى هذا الأمر والنهي والحكم فقد نفى كونه ملكًا مالكًا لملكه، فيكون له خلق ولا يكون له أمر، ويكون في جانب المخلوق كله جبرًا ولا يكون اختيارًا، ثم يلزم أن تكون النفوس الناطقة كائنة فاسدة لا معاد لها ولا كمال ولا جزاء على أفعالها ولا ثواب، وذلك يبطل قضية الحكمة.
From those who choose by this soul the vision of truth and correctness by choosing the better and avoiding the baser, and among them are those who slacken and become lazy until that preparedness becomes void and he uses it in the side of falsehood and error, and choosing the baser and avoiding the better; it must be that from the Wise is a judgment upon this rational soul while it is clothed in this bodily body, and a judgment upon it while it is separate from it. So that first judgment is what we mean by command and prohibition, and this second judgment is what we mean by promise and threat. And whoever denies this command, prohibition, and judgment has denied that He is a king possessing His kingdom, so He has creation but not command, and on the side of the creature all is compulsion and not choice. Then it necessarily follows that the rational souls are temporal and corruptible, with no return, no perfection, no recompense for their actions, and no reward. And that invalidates the requirement of wisdom.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage defines two divine judgments on the rational soul: one while it is embodied (command and prohibition) and one after separation from the body (promise and threat). The author argues that denying these judgments leads to the conclusion that God has creation but no command, implying compulsion and no free choice, and ultimately nullifies the possibility of an afterlife and divine wisdom.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة (rational soul)
أمر ونهي (command and prohibition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying divine command and reward does not affect divine wisdom or the afterlife.
Correction: The author shows that denying these judgments necessarily leads to compulsion, no afterlife, and the invalidation of wisdom itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows from denying the two judgments on the rational soul?
A) The soul becomes independent in its actions.
B) God's creation is without command and all is compulsion, leading to no afterlife.
C) The soul is purely spiritual and above any judgment.
D) The rational soul is only judged after death, not before.

Key Takeaway
Denying the two judgments on the rational soul undermines divine sovereignty and the possibility of an afterlife, contradicting divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you genuinely live with the awareness that every choice you make is under divine command and prohibition, or do you sometimes act as if your actions have no ultimate consequence? What is one specific area where you need to align your choices with this truth?
?
The passage warns that denying command and prohibition leads to the absurd conclusion of no afterlife. Have you ever lived as if this life is all that matters, neglecting the reality of promise and threat? How can you remind yourself of the eternal stakes today?
?
You are accountable for choosing the better over the baser. What is one baser habit or decision you have been tolerating, and will you commit to turning away from it right now, knowing that your rational soul is being judged both now and in the hereafter?

The Hierarchy of Souls
Describes ascending ranks of soul communication from animals to humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ascending hierarchy of souls in terms of communication, from animal senses to human rational speech.

Core Idea
Each higher soul rank is a different genus, not just a better version.

Before we can speak about divine speech, we need a clear picture of how created souls communicate. The author begins by describing a ladder of increasingly sophisticated forms of expression – from basic senses to melodic sounds to articulated human speech. This hierarchy sets up the analogy that will lead us to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage (first part)
Read the Arabic text from 'ونحن نرى' to 'النفس الناطقة الإنسانية'. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the progressive stages.
The author lists several ranks: four senses, then hearing, then sound without melody, then melody, then letters, then words.
2
Identify the ranks
List the specific ranks the author gives: (1) senses (touch, taste, smell, sight), (2) hearing plus those, (3) sound without melody, (4) sound with melody, (5) discrete letters, (6) articulated words. Notice each rank builds on the previous.
Not every rank is of the same genus – each is a perfection over the previous.
3
Understand the key claim
The author states: 'ليس كل مرتبة من هذه المراتب من جنس المرتبة التي قبلها لكنها كمالات النفوس بعد كمالات'. This means each rank is not simply a better version of the lower one; it is a different kind of perfection.
This is crucial for the later inference about God's speech.
4
Connect to the rational soul
The hierarchy culminates in the human rational soul. Notice how the author emphasizes that the rational soul's rank is above the earlier ranks, setting the stage for a further jump to angelic souls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نرى من النفوس الحيوانية ما لا يتفاهم بعضها من بعض إلا بإحدى من الحواس الأربع؛ وهي اللمس والذوق والشم والبصر، ومنها ما يتفاهم بها وبالسمع أيضا. ثم منها ما يكون له مجرد الصوت من غير لحن، ومنها ما يكون له مع صوته لحن، ثم من أصحاب الألحان ما يكون من ذوات الحروف المقطعة، ومنها ما لا يكون إلى أن يبلغ الأمر والحال إلى توصيع الكلمات من الحروف، وترتيب الحروف في الكلمات، فيكون ذلك دلالة على ما في النفوس الناطقة، وليس كل مرتبة من هذه المراتب من جنس المرتبة التي قبلها لكنها كمالات النفوس بعد كمالات إلى أن تبلغ إلى النفس الناطقة الإنسانية، فيحدس منها أن مرتبتها لما كانت فوق مرتبة سائر النفوس دل ذلك على أن مرتبة النفوس الروحانية والأرواح الملكية فوق مرتبة هذه النفوس في التفاهم. وكما لا تكون المرتبة التي للناطقة من جنس المرتبة التي للطير والبهائم، كذلك لا تكون المرتبة التي للملائكة الروحانية من جنس المرتبة التي لنا، بل تكون أشرف وألطف غير أنا بهذا الجنس استدللنا على ذلك النوع، كما استدللنا بنوع من الحدس في الأمور الغائبة على نوع من الحدس في أمور الغيب إلى أن يصل الكمال إلى حد من الخليقة فيقف الترتيب في المراتب، فيستدل بذلك على أن له الخلق والأمر فوق الأوامر النطقية الإنسانية والفكر العقلية، وأنه بوحدته فكر في معنى كل كلام ونطق وخبر واستخبار، وأمر ونهي، ووعد ووعيد، وإن سمع كلامه فوق كل سمع هو مفطور للحرف والصوت، كما أن سمعا يسمع الحروف والصوت فوق سمع هو مجبول بمجرد الصوت، فنحن إذا لم نستدل بجنس على جنس بل بجنس على ما هو فوق الجنس؟ فبطل استرواحهم إلى التقسيم الذي ذكروه.
And we see of the animal souls that some do not understand each other except by one of the four senses: touch, taste, smell, and sight; and some understand by these and by hearing as well. Then among them are those that have mere sound without melody, and among them are those that have with their sound a melody, then among the possessors of melodies are those that have discrete letters, and among them are those that do not, until the matter and state reaches the articulation of words from letters and the arrangement of letters in words, so that becomes an indication of what is in the rational souls. And not every rank of these ranks is of the genus of the rank before it, but they are perfections of souls after perfections until it reaches the human rational soul. Then one infers from it that since its rank is above the rank of other souls, that indicates that the rank of spiritual souls and angelic spirits is above the rank of these souls in understanding. And just as the rank of the rational soul is not of the genus of the rank of birds and beasts, likewise the rank of the spiritual angels is not of the genus of our rank, but is nobler and subtler. However, by this genus we inferred that type, just as we inferred by a type of intuition in absent matters a type of intuition in matters of the unseen, until perfection reaches a limit of creation and the arrangement in ranks stops; then one infers by that that to Him belongs creation and command above human verbal commands and intellectual thought, and that He by His unity thought the meaning of every word, speech, news, inquiry, command, prohibition, promise, and threat; and that hearing His speech is above every hearing that is created for hearing the letter and sound, just as hearing that hears letters and sounds is above hearing that is disposed to mere sound. So if we do not infer by genus from genus but by genus from what is above genus? So their resting upon the division they mentioned is invalidated.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the hierarchy of souls: animal souls communicate through senses, then through sound and melody, then through articulated speech in rational souls. Each rank is of a different genus, ascending to the human rational soul. From the superiority of the rational soul over other souls, we infer that angelic souls are even higher. Then by analogy, we infer that God's speech and command are above all human faculties. The author concludes that the opponents' division (based on genus-to-genus inference) fails because we infer from a lower genus to a higher one, not within the same genus.

Key Terms
النفوس الحيوانية
النفس الناطقة
الحدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the hierarchy is continuous, each rank being just a better version of the lower.
Correction: The author explicitly says each rank is not of the same genus as the one below; they are different perfections.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do some animal souls communicate besides the four senses?
A) By telepathy
B) By hearing
C) By rational thought
D) By divine inspiration

Key Takeaway
Communication among souls ascends through distinct genera from mere senses to articulate human language, each rank a new perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you communicating from the rational soul with intention and meaning, or merely repeating sounds like the lower ranks? How can you deliberately elevate your speech to reflect the distinct perfection God gave you?
?
The author emphasizes that each rank is a different genus, not just an improvement. Do you truly see yourself as essentially different from animals, or do you live as if you are only a smarter animal? How does this truth change your responsibility toward your Creator and your own soul?
?
If your rational soul is a distinct genus, what are you feeding it? Are you nourishing it with meaning and truth, or starving it with empty chatter? Commit today to spending ten minutes in silence, reflecting on one verse from the Quran that speaks to your soul.

Inferring Divine Speech from the Hierarchy
Uses hierarchy of souls to infer that divine speech is above all and refutes genus-to-genus objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author infers that God's speech is above all created ranks and refutes the objection that inference must be within the same genus.

Core Idea
We infer higher from lower across different genera, just as we infer angels from humans.

The author has established a hierarchy of soul communication. Now he uses that hierarchy to argue about divine speech. He shows that just as the rational soul is above animal souls, so angelic souls are above human souls, and God's speech is above all. He then answers an objection: that inference must be from genus to genus. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the Arabic passage
Read from 'فيحدس منها' to the end of the passage. Look for the analogical reasoning: rational soul is to animal souls as angelic souls are to us, and as God's speech is to all.
2
Trace the three-fold analogy
Identify three comparisons: (1) rational soul > animal souls, (2) angelic spirits > human rational souls, (3) God's creation and command > human speech and thought. Each step is a jump to a higher genus.
3
Understand the refutation of the opponents
The opponents argue that inference should be from genus to genus. The author replies that we are not inferring from genus to genus but from a lower genus to what is above genus. He gives the example of intuition about unseen matters.
Read the last sentences carefully.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by declaring the opponents' division invalid. Understand why: because they assumed inference must stay within genus, but the author shows it can cross genera upward.

Source Text
