Inferring God's Speech from the Hierarchy of Souls
The author argues from the hierarchy of souls to the nature of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hierarchy of souls from the lowest to the human rational soul leads to an inference about the transcendence of divine speech.

Core Idea
The ascent through soul ranks shows God's speech is above all human speech.

This passage presents a sophisticated analogical argument. By observing the hierarchy of souls — from vegetative to animal to human rational soul — we can infer that higher ranks are not just greater but of a different kind. The author uses this to argue for the uniqueness and transcendence of divine speech, refuting those who would compare it to human speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hierarchy of souls
Notice how the author describes the progression from lower ranks to the human rational soul. Each rank is a perfection of the previous.
The key term 'النفس الناطقة' appears here.
2
Follow the analogical inference
The author uses an analogy: just as human rational soul is above animals, so angelic souls are above humans, and divine command is above all. Trace this reasoning.
3
Understand the nature of divine speech
The passage explains that God's speech encompasses all types of utterances and that hearing it is superior to physical hearing.
Note the contrast between divine speech and human speech.
4
See the refutation of the Mu'tazila
The author concludes that if we cannot infer by genus to genus, then the Mu'tazila's division fails. Grasp why this argument invalidates their position.
The phrase 'بطل استرواحهم' signals the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مرتبة من هذه المراتب من جنس المرتبة التي قبلها لكنها كمالات النفوس بعد كمالات إلى أن تبلغ إلى النفس الناطقة الإنسانية، فيحدس منها أن مرتبتها لما كانت فوق مرتبة سائر النفوس دل ذلك على أن مرتبة النفوس الروحانية والأرواح الملكية فوق مرتبة هذه النفوس في التفاهم. وكما لا تكون المرتبة التي للناطقة من جنس المرتبة التي للطير والبهائم، كذلك لا تكون المرتبة التي للملائكة الروحانية من جنس المرتبة التي لنا، بل تكون أشرف وألطف غير أنا بهذا الجنس استدللنا على ذلك النوع، كما استدللنا بنوع من الحدس في الأمور الغائبة على نوع من الحدس في أمور الغيب إلى أن يصل الكمال إلى حد من الخليقة فيقف الترتيب في المراتب، فيستدل بذلك على أن أمر له الخلق والأمر فوق الأوامر النطقية الإنسانية والفكر العقلية، وأنه بوحدته فكر في معنى كل كلام ونطق وخبر واستخبار، وأمر ونهي، ووعد ووعيد، وإن سمعا كلامه فوق كل سمع هو مفطور للحرف يسمع والصوت، كما أن سمعا يسمع الحروف والصوت فوق سمع هو مجبول بمجرد الصوت، فنحن إذا لم نستدل بجنس على جنس بل بجنس على ما هو فوق الجنس؟ فبطل استرواحهم إلى التقسيم الذي ذكروه.
A rank of these ranks is of the same kind as the rank before it, but it is perfections of souls after perfections until it reaches the human rational soul. Then it is inferred from it that since its rank is above the rank of other souls, that indicates that the rank of spiritual souls and angelic spirits is above the rank of these souls in understanding. And just as the rank of the rational soul is not of the same kind as the rank of birds and beasts, so too the rank of spiritual angels is not of the same kind as our rank; rather they are nobler and subtler, but we by this kind have inferred that type, just as we inferred by a kind of intuition (al-ḥads) in absent things upon a kind of intuition in matters of the unseen, until perfection reaches a limit of creation and the arrangement in ranks stops, thereby inferring that His affair is the creation and the command is above human verbal commands and intellectual thought, and that He by His oneness thought in the meaning of every speech, utterance, news, inquiry, command, prohibition, promise, and threat, and that the hearing of His speech is above every hearing that is constituted for hearing the letter and sound, just as a hearing that hears letters and sounds is above a hearing that is constituted for mere sound. So if we have not inferred by a kind upon a kind, but by a kind upon what is above the kind? Thus their comfort in the division they mentioned is invalid.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is an analogical argument for the transcendence of divine speech. The author first establishes a hierarchy among souls: from vegetative to animal to human rational soul, each rank being superior and of a different kind. Then he argues that just as the human rational soul is above animal souls, so angelic spirits are above human souls. Continuing this ascent, he infers that God's command (amr) is above all human speech and thought. He emphasizes that the divine command encompasses all types of speech (news, inquiry, command, etc.) and that the hearing of God's speech is superior to physical hearing of sounds. The conclusion is that if the Mu'tazila try to analogize divine speech to human speech using a genus-species division, they fail because divine speech is above genera. Thus their division is invalid.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
المراتب
الكلام
السمع
الحدس
الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument anthropomorphizes God's speech by comparing it to human speech within the same genus.
Correction: The author uses analogy to show that divine speech is of a completely different and higher kind, not comparable to human speech.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the author draw from the hierarchy of souls about the divine command?
A) It is of the same kind as human command.
B) It is above human speech and thought.
C) It is merely symbolic.
D) It is limited to angels.

Key Takeaway
The hierarchy of souls proves that divine speech is not of the same kind as human speech and is therefore transcendent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage says God, by His oneness, encompasses the meaning of every speech. Does your heart truly believe that the Quran is not just a book but the very expression of His oneness? If so, how does that change your relationship with its verses?
?
You have been taught that God's speech is unlike any sound or letter. Yet when you recite the Quran, do you still imagine a voice or a sound? Can you let go of that image and worship Him as He truly is, beyond all comparisons?

The Author's Challenge: Analogizing Divine Speech to Other Attributes
The author challenges opponents to analogize speech to other attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues for the eternity of divine speech by analogizing it to other attributes like knowledge and power.

Core Idea
If you accept eternal attributes like knowledge, why not eternal speech?

In this passage, the author addresses the Mu'tazila directly, challenging them to apply the same logic to speech that they apply to other divine attributes. He points out the inconsistency in accepting eternal knowledge, power, and will, but denying eternal speech. The aim is to show that the dispute reduces to the general issue of attributes, not something unique about speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the challenge
Read the author's opening rhetorical questions. Notice how he lists attributes (intelligent, powerful, willing) and asks why speech is not treated the same way.
2
Identify the analogy
The author draws an analogy between the way God gives intellect, inception, and system, and the way He gives speech. Understand the parallel.
3
Consider the conclusion
The author concludes that the dispute should be about attributes in general, not about speech uniquely. Reflect on the logic.
This is a key move in the Ash'ari argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول ألستم تقولون : إن واجب الوجود من حيث يعطي العقل في المعقول عاقل ، ومن حيث يعطي البدء في المبد قادر، ومن حيث يعطي النظام على أتم وجوه الكمال مريد؟ فهلا قلتم : إنه من حيث يعطي النطق الروحاني للمفارقات والنطق النفساني لذوات القوالب آمر متكلم، ثم هلا حملتم كلامه وأمره على ما حملتم علمه وقدرته وإرادته فيصير التنازع واحدا بيننا، ويرجع الأمر إلى مسألة الصفات، فما الذي عدا مما بدا ؟ وكيف اشتملت مسألة الكلام على محال لم تشتمل عليه مسألة العلم والقدرة عندكم؟ قالت المعتزلة : نحن نوافقكم على أن الباري تعالى متكلم، لكن حقيقة المتكلم من فعل الكلام فهو فاعل الكلام في محل ؛ بحيث يسمع ويعلم أنه كلامه ضرورة ؛ لأنه لو كان المتكلم من قام به الكلام كما ذهبتم إليه وجب أن يكون كلامه إما قديما وإما حادثا، وإن كان قديما ففيه إثبات القديمين ويرجع القول إلى مسألة الصفات ومما يختص بهذه المسألة من الاستحالة أنه لو كان قديما وهو أمر ونهي لزم أن يكون كلامًا مع نفسه من غير مأمور ولا منهي ومن المحال الذي لا يتمارى فيه أن القول ب إِنَّا أَرْسَلْنَا نُوحًا إِلَى قَوْمِهِ ولا نوح ولا قومه إخبار عما ليس كما هو، فهو مع استحالته كذب، ومع كذبه محال. وقوله : ( اخلع نعليك ) لموسى ولا موسى ولا طور، ولا الوادي المقدس طوى خطاب المعدوم ، والمعدوم كيف يخاطب، وكذلك جميع ما في القرآن من الأوامر والنواهي والأخبار، فوجب أن يكون الكلام يحدث عند حدوث المخاطب
Then we say: Do you not say that the Necessary Existent, inasmuch as He gives intellect to the intelligible, is intelligent, and inasmuch as He gives inception to the originated, is powerful, and inasmuch as He gives system in the most perfect manner, is willing? Then why do you not say that He, inasmuch as He gives spiritual speech to the separated beings and psychic speech to those with bodies, is a commanding speaker? Then why do you not treat His speech and command as you treat His knowledge, power, and will, so that the dispute becomes one between us and the matter returns to the question of attributes? So what has changed from what was apparent? And how did the question of speech include impossibilities that the question of knowledge and power did not include according to you? The Mu'tazila said: We agree with you that the Creator, exalted, is a speaker, but the reality of 'speaker' is one who performs speech, so He is the agent of speech in a locus such that it is heard and known that it is His speech by necessity; because if the speaker were one in whom speech inheres as you hold, then His speech must be either eternal or originated. If it is eternal, then it implies affirming two eternals and the matter returns to the question of attributes. And what is specific to this question of impossibility is that if it is eternal and it is a command and prohibition, then it would be speech to Himself without anyone commanded or prohibited. And it is impossible, about which there is no doubt, that the statement 'Indeed, We sent Noah to his people' when there is no Noah and no people is an informing of what is not as it is; so it is, given its impossibility, a lie, and given its lie, impossible. And His saying: 'Remove your sandals' to Moses when there is no Moses, no mountain, and no holy valley of Tuwa is an address to a non-existent. How can a non-existent be addressed? And likewise all that is in the Qur'an of commands, prohibitions, and reports. Therefore speech must occur at the occurrence of the addressee.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage begins with the author posing a challenge to the Mu'tazila: If you accept that God is knowing, powerful, and willing through attributes that are not identical to His essence but are real, why do you not accept that He is a speaker in the same way, i.e., with an eternal attribute of speech? The author argues that the dispute should then be about attributes in general, not about speech uniquely. Then the Mu'tazila respond by defining 'speaker' (mutakallim) as one who creates speech in a locus (like the air), not as one in whom speech inheres. They argue that if speech inhered in God, it would be either eternal or originated. If eternal, it would imply two eternal beings (since the speech would be an eternal entity alongside God). Moreover, an eternal command would be meaningless without an addressee, and the Qur'anic verses would be false because they refer to events that had not yet occurred. Therefore, speech must be created at the time of addressing the addressee.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
متكلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazila denied that God is a speaker altogether.
Correction: The passage shows that the Mu'tazila agreed God is a speaker (mutakallim), but they defined it differently (as Creator of speech, not as one in whom speech inheres).

Checkpoint
What does the author ask the Mu'tazila to treat speech like?
A) Like a created object
B) Like knowledge, power, and will
C) Like a physical sound
D) Like human speech

Key Takeaway
The author argues that divine speech should be analogized to other attributes, so the dispute is about attributes in general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When the author asks, 'What has changed from what was apparent?' — ask yourself: In what area of my faith have I accepted God's attributes easily but resisted His speech being eternal? What is the root of that resistance in my heart? Will I now repent of that inconsistency and submit to the full implications of the Quran as His speech?
?
If I treat God's knowledge, power, and will as eternal attributes, why do I hesitate to treat His speech the same? Does this hesitation reveal a hidden assumption that the Quran is merely created? Am I willing to let go of that assumption, even if it challenges my previous understanding?
?
The author challenges you to be consistent: 'Then why do you not treat His speech and command as you treat His knowledge, power, and will?' Can you look at your own belief and say you are consistent? If not, what step will you take today to align your heart with what the revelation demands?

The Mu'tazilite Objection: Defining 'Speaker' and Rejecting Eternal Speech
The Mu'tazila object to eternal speech by redefining 'speaker' and citing absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite definition of 'speaker' and their arguments against eternal divine speech.

Core Idea
God is a speaker because He creates speech, not because speech inheres in Him.

The Mu'tazila respond to the author's challenge by offering a different definition of 'speaker' (mutakallim). For them, a speaker is one who creates speech in a locus, not one in whom speech inheres. They then argue that if speech were eternal, it would lead to absurdities such as commands to non-existents and false reports about the future. This lesson examines their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite response
Read from 'The Mu'tazila said' onwards. Note how they first agree that God is a speaker, then redefine it.
This is a critical definitional move.
2
Understand the two options
They say if speech inhered in God, it would be either eternal or originated. Consider why eternal speech is problematic.
3
Examine the absurdities
They list specific absurdities: two eternals, commanding non-existents, false reports about Noah, and addressing non-existent Moses.
These are powerful theological objections.
4
Draw the conclusion
The Mu'tazila conclude that speech must be created at the time of addressing.
This is the key conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول ألستم تقولون : إن واجب الوجود من حيث يعطي العقل في المعقول عاقل ، ومن حيث يعطي البدء في المبد قادر، ومن حيث يعطي النظام على أتم وجوه الكمال مريد؟ فهلا قلتم : إنه من حيث يعطي النطق الروحاني للمفارقات والنطق النفساني لذوات القوالب آمر متكلم، ثم هلا حملتم كلامه وأمره على ما حملتم علمه وقدرته وإرادته فيصير التنازع واحدا بيننا، ويرجع الأمر إلى مسألة الصفات، فما الذي عدا مما بدا ؟ وكيف اشتملت مسألة الكلام على محال لم تشتمل عليه مسألة العلم والقدرة عندكم؟ قالت المعتزلة : نحن نوافقكم على أن الباري تعالى متكلم، لكن حقيقة المتكلم من فعل الكلام فهو فاعل الكلام في محل ؛ بحيث يسمع ويعلم أنه كلامه ضرورة ؛ لأنه لو كان المتكلم من قام به الكلام كما ذهبتم إليه وجب أن يكون كلامه إما قديما وإما حادثا، وإن كان قديما ففيه إثبات القديمين ويرجع القول إلى مسألة الصفات ومما يختص بهذه المسألة من الاستحالة أنه لو كان قديما وهو أمر ونهي لزم أن يكون كلامًا مع نفسه من غير مأمور ولا منهي ومن المحال الذي لا يتمارى فيه أن القول ب إِنَّا أَرْسَلْنَا نُوحًا إِلَى قَوْمِهِ ولا نوح ولا قومه إخبار عما ليس كما هو، فهو مع استحالته كذب، ومع كذبه محال. وقوله : ( اخلع نعليك ) لموسى ولا موسى ولا طور، ولا الوادي المقدس طوى خطاب المعدوم ، والمعدوم كيف يخاطب، وكذلك جميع ما في القرآن من الأوامر والنواهي والأخبار، فوجب أن يكون الكلام يحدث عند حدوث المخاطب
Then we say: Do you not say that the Necessary Existent, inasmuch as He gives intellect to the intelligible, is intelligent, and inasmuch as He gives inception to the originated, is powerful, and inasmuch as He gives system in the most perfect manner, is willing? Then why do you not say that He, inasmuch as He gives spiritual speech to the separated beings and psychic speech to those with bodies, is a commanding speaker? Then why do you not treat His speech and command as you treat His knowledge, power, and will, so that the dispute becomes one between us and the matter returns to the question of attributes? The Challenge of Consistency and the Definition of 'Mutakallim'
Juwayni challenges the Mu'tazila; they define 'mutakallim' as creator of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila definition of 'mutakallim' (speaker) as one who creates speech in a locus, in contrast to the Ash'ari view, and why they see this definition as necessary for avoiding absurdities.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila agree God is a speaker, but redefine 'speaker' as creator of speech, not its locus.

This lesson begins with Juwayni's challenge to the Mu'tazila: if they accept that God is rational, powerful, and willing based on certain aspects, why not also accept that He is a commanding speaker based on His giving speech? The Mu'tazila then present their definition of 'mutakallim' as one who creates speech in a locus, setting the stage for their subsequent argument that speech cannot be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read Juwayni's Challenge
Read the opening rhetorical question carefully. It urges the Mu'tazila to be consistent in how they derive attributes from God's effects.
Notice the parallel: just as God is called 'rational' from giving intellect, 'powerful' from giving power, etc., so He should be called 'speaker' from giving speech.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila Response
The Mu'tazila agree God is a speaker, but they give a specific definition of 'mutakallim': one who performs speech (creates it) in a locus, so that it is heard and recognized as His speech.
This definition is crucial for their later argument against speech subsisting in God's essence.
3
Contrast with Ash'ari View
Consider how this differs from the Ash'ari view that speech is an attribute subsisting in God's essence. The Mu'tazila reject that view because it leads to absurdities, as you will see in the next lesson.
The definition here is the foundation for the entire debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول ألستم تقولون : إن واجب الوجود من حيث يعطي العقل في المعقول عاقل ، ومن حيث يعطي البدء في المبدأ قادر، ومن حيث يعطي النظام على أتم وجوه الكمال مريد؟ فهلا قلتم : إنه من حيث يعطي النطق الروحاني للمفارقات والنطق النفساني لذوات القوالب آمر متكلم، ثم هلا حملتم كلامه وأمره على ما حملتم علمه وقدرته وإرادته فيصير التنازع واحدا بيننا، ويرجع الأمر إلى مسألة الصفات، فما الذي عدا مما بدا ؟ وكيف اشتملت مسألة الكلام على محال لم تشتمل عليه مسألة العلم والقدرة عندكم؟ قالت المعتزلة : نحن نوافقكم على أن الباري تعالى متكلم، لكن حقيقة المتكلم من فعل الكلام فهو فاعل الكلام في محل ؛ بحيث يسمع ويعلم أنه كلامه ضرورة ؛ لأنه لو كان المتكلم من قام به الكلام كما ذهبتم إليه وجب أن يكون كلامه إما قديما وإما حادثا، وإن كان قديما ففيه إثبات القديمين ويرجع القول إلى مسألة الصفات ومما يختص بهذه المسألة من الاستحالة أنه لو كان قديما وهو أمر ونهي لزم أن يكون كلامًا مع نفسه من غير مأمور ولا منهي ومن المحال الذي لا يتمارى فيه أن القول ب إنا أرسلنا نوحا إلى قومه ولا نوح ولا قومه إخبار عما ليس كما هو، فهو مع استحالته كذب، ومع كذبه محال. وقوله : اخلع نعليك لموسى ولا موسى ولا طور، ولا الوادي المقدس طوى خطاب المعدوم ، والمعدوم كيف يخاطب، وكذلك جميع ما في القرآن من الأوامر والنواهي والأخبار، فوجب أن يكون الكلام يحدث عند حدوث المخاطب في الوقت الذي يصل الخطاب إليه، فيكون الكلام حادثا، ثم إما أن يحدث في ذاته كما صارت إليه الكرامية فيكون محلا للحوادث وذلك باطل، وإما أن يحدث لا في محل أو في محل ولا بد من محل فإنه حرف، والحرف تقطيع صوت، والصوت في الجسم متصور، فتعين أنه في جسم.
Then we say: Do you not say that the Necessary Existent is, by virtue of that which the intellect gives in the intelligible, rational; and by virtue of that which the beginning gives in the beginning, powerful; and by virtue of that which the system gives in the most perfect modes of perfection, willing? Then why do you not say that He is, by virtue of that which gives spiritual speech to the separate intellects and psychic speech to those with bodies, a commanding speaker? Then why do you not carry His speech and command on what you carried His knowledge, power, and will, so that the dispute becomes one between us, and the matter returns to the issue of attributes? So what has occurred that was not before? And how did the issue of speech include impossibilities that the issue of knowledge and power did not include according to you? The Mu'tazila said: We agree with you that the Creator is a speaker, but the essence of the speaker is one who performs speech, so He is the agent of speech in a locus, such that it is heard and it is known that it is His speech necessarily; because if the speaker were one in whom speech subsists, as you have held, then His speech must be either eternal or originated. If it is eternal, then there is affirmation of two eternals, and the matter returns to the issue of attributes; and what is specific to this issue of impossibility is that if it were eternal and it is a command and prohibition, then it would necessarily be speech with Himself without any commanded or prohibited person. And it is an impossibility about which there is no doubt that the statement 'We sent Noah to his people' when there is no Noah and no his people is an informing of what is not as it is; so it is, along with its impossibility, a lie, and with its lie, impossible. And His statement 'Take off your sandals' to Moses when there is no Moses, no mountain, and no holy valley of Tuwa is an address to the non-existent, and how can the non-existent be addressed? And likewise all the commands, prohibitions, and reports in the Quran. So it is necessary that speech be originated at the occurrence of the addressee in the time when the address reaches him, so speech is originated. Then either it originates in His essence, as the Karramiyya held, so it would be a locus for originated events, and that is false; or it originates not in a locus or in a locus, and a locus is necessary, for it is a letter, and a letter is a cutting of sound, and sound is conceivable in a body, so it is determined that it is in a body.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents an initial challenge and the Mu'tazila response regarding the nature of divine speech. The opening lines pose a rhetorical question to the Mu'tazila: if they accept that God is rational, powerful, and willing based on certain aspects, why do they not accept that He is a commanding speaker based on His giving spiritual and psychic speech? The challenge urges consistency in treating speech like other attributes. The Mu'tazila then respond directly. They agree that God is a speaker (mutakallim), but they define 'speaker' as one who creates speech in a locus (mahall), so that the speech is heard and known to be His. They argue that if speech subsisted in God's essence (as the Ash'ari hold), it would have to be either eternal or originated. If eternal, it would entail two eternals (the essence and the speech) and absurdities: eternal commands and prohibitions with no one to command or prohibit, and eternal reports about events that have not occurred, which would be false and impossible. They cite Quranic verses: 'We sent Noah to his people' when neither Noah nor his people existed yet, and 'Take off your sandals' to Moses before Moses existed. Such eternal speech would be an address to the non-existent, which is impossible. Therefore, speech must be originated (hadith) at the time of addressing the creature. Then they consider the locus of this originated speech: it cannot originate in God's essence (as the Karramiyya claim) because that would make Him a locus of originated events, which is false. It must originate either not in a locus or in a locus; but since speech consists of letters and sounds, which require a body, it must be in a body (jism). This conclusion supports the Mu'tazila view that God creates speech in a physical substrate, such as the angel or the prophet.

Key Terms
متكلم
كلام
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila deny that God is a speaker altogether.
Correction: The Mu'tazila affirm that God is a speaker (mutakallim), but they define speaker as one who creates speech, not one in whom speech subsists.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila in this passage, what does it mean for God to be a 'mutakallim'?
A) He has an eternal attribute of speech.
B) He creates speech in a locus so that it is heard.
C) He is identical to His speech.
D) He speaks without any intermediary.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila agree God is a speaker, but they insist that 'speaker' means creator of speech in a locus, not possessor of an eternal speech attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is your belief about God's speech merely intellectual, or do you live as though the Qur'an is the very word of God addressing you personally? What would change if you truly believed it was His eternal speech?
?
Juwayni calls for consistency: if you accept God's knowledge, power, and will from His effects, why not His speech? Where in your own faith is there inconsistency between what you profess and how you act? Will you identify one area today and commit to aligning it?
?
The Mu'tazila redefined 'speaker' to avoid attributing an eternal speech to God. Have you ever redefined a doctrine to make it more comfortable or acceptable, rather than submitting to its full implications? What would obedience cost you if you stopped redefining it?

The Mu'tazila Argument for Created Speech
Mu'tazila argue against eternal speech and conclude it is created in a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that God's speech cannot be eternal because it would entail impossible commands and reports, and why they conclude speech must be created in a body.

Core Idea
Eternal speech leads to absurdities like commanding non-existent people, so speech must be originated.

In this lesson, we examine the core of the Mu'tazila argument: if speech subsisted in God's essence, it would have to be eternal or originated. They argue that eternal speech is impossible because it would include commands to people who do not yet exist and reports about events that have not happened, which are false and absurd. Therefore, speech must be originated. They then consider where it originates, rejecting the Karramiyya view that it originates in God's essence, and concluding that it must be in a body, such as an angel or the prophet.

Guided Path
1
Understanding the Dilemma
Read the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazila set up a dilemma: if speech subsists in God, it must be either eternal or originated. They will argue both are impossible.
Focus on the logical structure: if P then Q or R; then Q is false, so R must be true.
2
The Absurdity of Eternal Speech
The Mu'tazila point out that eternal speech containing commands ('Do this') or reports ('We sent Noah') would be addressing non-existent beings or reporting events that haven't occurred, which is impossible and false.
They cite Quranic verses to illustrate: 'We sent Noah to his people' would be a lie if no Noah or people existed.
3
The Locus of Created Speech
Once they establish speech is originated, they consider its location. It cannot be in God's essence (as Karramiyya say) because that would make God a locus of events. It must be in a body, because letters and sounds require a physical substrate.
This conclusion supports the Mu'tazila view that God creates speech in the angel or prophet as a physical locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then we say: Do you not say that the Necessary Existent is, by virtue of that which the intellect gives in the intelligible, rational; and by virtue of that which the beginning gives in the beginning, powerful; and by virtue of that which the system gives in the most perfect modes of perfection, willing? Then why do you not say that He is, by virtue of that which gives spiritual speech to the separate intellects and psychic speech to those with bodies, a commanding speaker? Then why do you not carry His speech and command on what you carried His knowledge, power, and will, so that the dispute becomes one between us, and the matter returns to the issue of attributes? So what has occurred that was not before? And how did the issue of speech include impossibilities that the issue of knowledge and power did not include according to you? The Mu'tazila said: We agree with you that the Creator is a speaker, but the essence of the speaker is one who performs speech, so He is the agent of speech in a locus, such that it is heard and it is known that it is His speech necessarily; because if the speaker were one in whom speech subsists, as you have held, then His speech must be either eternal or originated. If it is eternal, then there is affirmation of two eternals, and the matter returns to the issue of attributes; and what is specific to this issue of impossibility is that if it were eternal and it is a command and prohibition, then it would necessarily be speech with Himself without any commanded or prohibited person. And it is an impossibility about which there is no doubt that the statement 'We sent Noah to his people' when there is no Noah and no his people is an informing of what is not as it is; so it is, along with its impossibility, a lie, and with its lie, impossible. And His statement 'Take off your sandals' to Moses when there is no Moses, no mountain, and no holy valley of Tuwa is an address to the non-existent, and how can the non-existent be addressed? And likewise all the commands, prohibitions, and reports in the Quran. So it is necessary that speech be originated at the occurrence of the addressee in the time when the address reaches him, so speech is originated. Then either it originates in His essence, as the Karramiyya held, so it would be a locus for originated events, and that is false; or it originates not in a locus or in a locus, and a locus is necessary, for it is a letter, and a letter is a cutting of sound, and sound is conceivable in a body, so it is determined that it is in a body.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents an initial challenge and the Mu'tazila response regarding the nature of divine speech. The opening lines pose a rhetorical question to the Mu'tazila: if they accept that God is rational, powerful, and willing based on certain aspects, why do they not accept that He is a commanding speaker based on His giving spiritual and psychic speech? The challenge urges consistency in treating speech like other attributes. The Mu'tazila then respond directly. They agree that God is a speaker (mutakallim), but they define 'speaker' as one who creates speech in a locus (mahall), so that the speech is heard and known to be His. They argue that if speech subsisted in God's essence (as the Ash'ari hold), it would have to be either eternal or originated. If eternal, it would entail two eternals (the essence and the speech) and absurdities: eternal commands and prohibitions with no one to command or prohibit, and eternal reports about events that have not occurred, which would be false and impossible. They cite Quranic verses: 'We sent Noah to his people' when neither Noah nor his people existed yet, and 'Take off your sandals' to Moses before Moses existed. Such eternal speech would be an address to the non-existent, which is impossible. Therefore, speech must be originated (hadith) at the time of addressing the creature. Then they consider the locus of this originated speech: it cannot originate in God's essence (as the Karramiyya claim) because that would make Him a locus of originated events, which is false. It must originate either not in a locus or in a locus; but since speech consists of letters and sounds, which require a body, it must be in a body (jism). This conclusion supports the Mu'tazila view that God creates speech in a physical substrate, such as the angel or the prophet.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
أمر
نهي
خطاب
مأمور
منهي
محل

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila in this passage, why is it impossible for God's speech to be eternal?
A) Because God cannot have any eternal attributes.
B) Because it would require two eternal beings.
C) Because it would include commands to non-existent people and false reports.
D) Because it would make God a body.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that eternal speech is impossible because it would include absurd commands to non-existent beings and false reports, so speech must be created in a physical body at the time of revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran's commandments, do you experience them as a timeless address from God to you, or as historical commands given to others? How does your belief about the nature of God's speech affect your obedience today?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that eternal commands to non-existent people are absurd. Do you ever feel that some Quranic commands are irrelevant because they were revealed in a different time and place? If so, how can you reconcile that with your faith in the Quran's eternal relevance?
?
If God's speech is created and brought into existence at specific moments, as the Mu'tazila claim, then the Quran you recite is a temporal event. Does this diminish its authority in your life, or does it make each recitation a fresh encounter with God's address to you now? Be honest with yourself.

The Ash'ari Critique of the Mu'tazila on Speech
Juwayni presents the Ash'ari critique of the Mu'tazila definition of mutakallim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari critique of the Mu'tazila definition of mutakallim and why the Mu'tazila cannot prove that God is a speaker in a distinct sense without an attribute of speech.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila cannot prove God is a speaker without admitting a real attribute of speech.

Imam al-Juwayni presents a sharp Ash'ari critique: if the Mu'tazila define mutakallim as one who creates speech, they cannot distinguish God from any other creator. This passage forces the Mu'tazila to confront the need for a real attribute of speech, beyond mere creation of acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, paying attention to the structure of the argument. Identify the two foundations the Ash'ari attribute to the Mu'tazila.
The passage is a critique, so focus on the challenge.
2
Identify the two Mu'tazila principles
The Ash'ari state that the Mu'tazila built their doctrine on two principles. Find these two principles in the text.
The first principle is that God is mutakallim; the second defines mutakallim as one who performs speech.
3
Understand the challenge to the first principle
The Ash'ari ask: if a Sabean or philosopher denies God is a speaker, what proof can the Mu'tazila provide? Since they define mutakallim as one who creates speech, they cannot offer a distinct proof.
This is the core of the critique.
4
Reflect on the metaphorical speech argument
The Ash'ari argue that the Mu'tazila position reduces to metaphorical attribution, as shown by the Quranic verse about heaven and earth. Consider why this is a problem for the Mu'tazila.
The verse does not imply real speech in the sense of command and obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية : بنيتم مذهبكم على قاعدتين ؛ إحديهما تسليمكم كون الباري تعالى متكلما والثانية أن حد المتكلم وحقيقته من فعل الكلام، فأما الأولى فلو نازعكم الصابي والفيلسوف في كونه متكلما ، فما دليلكم في ذلك عليهما، وعندكم الكلام فعل الباري تعالى كسائر الأفعال ولا يرجع إليه حكم الكلام إلا أنه فاعل صانع ؟ فما الدليل على أنه متكلم؟ أعني يوصف بمعنى يقوم بمحل آخر، فما الفرق بين مذهبكم وبين مذهب من قال : إنه يخلق فعلا يفهم عند ذلك أنه تعالى يريد من العبد فعلا أو يكرهه ، وإن أضيف إليه الكلام كان مجازا ، كما قال تعالى خطابًا للسماء والأرض : ( انْتِيَا طَوْعاً أَوْ كَرْهًا قَالَتَا أَتَيْنَا طَابِعِينَ ) فلم يجز من حيث الحقيقة كلام هو اقتضاء وأمر وإيجاب من تعالى، ولا تسلم وائتمار واستيجاب من السماء والأرض من حيث القول.
The Ash'ari said: You have built your doctrine on two principles: one of them is your acceptance that God is a speaker, and the second is that the definition and essence of the speaker is from performing speech. As for the first, if the Sabean and the philosopher were to dispute with you about His being a speaker, what is your proof against them? And according to you, speech is an act of God like other acts, and no ruling of speech is referred back to Him except that He is a doer, a maker? So what is the proof that He is a speaker? I mean described by a meaning that subsists in another locus? So what is the difference between your doctrine and the doctrine of one who says: He creates an act by which it is understood that He intends from the servant an act or dislikes it, and if speech is attributed to Him, it is metaphorical, as He said addressing the heaven and the earth: 'Come willingly or unwillingly; they said, We come obedient.' So it is not valid, in terms of reality, that speech is a requirement, command, and obligation from God, and there is no acceptance of command and obligation from the heaven and the earth in terms of speech.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari critique of the Mu'tazila position. The Ash'ari begin by stating that the Mu'tazila have built their doctrine on two foundations: (1) their acceptance that God is a speaker (mutakallim), and (2) their definition that the essence of a speaker is one who performs speech (i.e., creates it in a locus). The Ash'ari then challenge the first foundation: if a Sabean or a philosopher were to dispute that God is a speaker, what proof would the Mu'tazila provide? Since the Mu'tazila consider speech simply as an act of God like any other act, and do not attribute any special quality of speech to God beyond being a creator, they cannot claim any specific evidence that God is a 'speaker' in a sense distinct from being a 'maker'. The Ash'ari argue that the Mu'tazila definition reduces to the idea that God creates an act that conveys His will, and if speech is attributed to Him, it is merely metaphorical. They cite the Quranic verse where God addresses the heaven and earth saying 'Come willingly or unwillingly' and they respond obediently. This cannot be real speech involving actual command and obligation from God and actual compliance from the heavens and earth in terms of literal speech. Thus, the Mu'tazila position is indistinguishable from metaphorical attribution, and they lack a proof for a distinct attribute of speech.

متكلم
حد
فعل
فاعل
مجاز
حقيقة
اقتضاء
أمر
إيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari are denying that God is a speaker.
Correction: The Ash'ari are not denying that God is a speaker; they are critiquing the Mu'tazila's lack of proof for their specific definition of mutakallim.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the Ash'ari critique against the Mu'tazila in this passage?
A) The Mu'tazila wrongly assume that God's speech is eternal.
B) The Mu'tazila cannot prove from their own premises that God is a speaker in a distinct sense, as opposed to a mere creator of acts.
C) The Mu'tazila fail to address the philosophical objection about God's essence.
D) The Mu'tazila conflate God's speech with His will.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari demonstrate that the Mu'tazila definition of mutakallim fails to provide a distinct proof for God's attribute of speech, reducing it to metaphorical attribution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals a flaw in the Mu'tazila's proof for God's speech. Are there any beliefs about Allah that you hold simply because you were taught them, without ever examining the evidence? Will you take time this week to verify one such belief from the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
The Ash'ari argue that the Mu'tazila cannot prove God is a speaker except as a metaphor. When you recite the Qur'an, do you truly believe it is Allah's actual speech, or do you sometimes act as if it were just a metaphor? How does that affect your reverence and obedience to its commands?
?
Imagine someone challenged your belief that the Qur'an is the literal word of Allah. Could you provide a rational proof, or would you realize your foundation is weak? What steps can you take today to strengthen your conviction so that you can defend it?

Ash'ari Objection on Divine Speech and the Proof from Miracles
Ash'aris challenge two bases; opponent responds via miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari objection to the claim that God is mutakallim and how the opponent defends this doctrine by appealing to miracles as proof of prophetic truthfulness.

Core Idea
Miracles confirm God's speech through prophetic testimony.

This passage presents a theological debate: Ash'aris challenge the claim that God is mutakallim (speaking) by questioning the rational proof and the definition of 'speaker'. The opponent responds by grounding God's speech in the miracles that validate the prophets, who report God's commands. This shifts the argument from direct rational demonstration to the authority of prophetic testimony supported by miracles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two bases
Read the opening sentence to see how the Ash'aris frame the opponent's doctrine: affirmation that God is mutakallim and the definition of 'speaker' as one who performs speech. This sets up their challenge.
The second base is crucial because it defines what it means to be a speaker in this debate.
2
Examine the Ash'ari challenge
Notice how the Ash'aris demand a proof for the first base, especially against a Sabian or philosopher who denies God is speaking. They also point out the similarity to metaphorical speech, citing the Quranic verse about the heavens and earth.
The verse shows that attributing speech to God could be interpreted metaphorically.
3
Understand the opponent's response
The opponent replies by invoking miracles (mu'jizat) as evidence that the prophets are truthful when they report God's speech. This moves the debate from rational proof to prophetic authority.
Miracles are seen as divine confirmation of the prophet's message.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية : بنيتم مذهبكم على قاعدتين ؛ إحديهما تسليمكم كون الباري تعالى متكلما والثانية أن حد المتكلم وحقيقته من فعل الكلام، فأما الأولى فلو نازعكم الصابي والفيلسوف في كونه متكلما ، فما دليلكم في ذلك عليهما، وعندكم الكلام فعل الباري تعالى كسائر الأفعال ولا يرجع إليه حكم الكلام إلا أنه فاعل صانع ؟ فما الدليل على أنه متكلم؟ أعني يوصف بمعنى يقوم بمحل آخر، فما الفرق بين مذهبكم وبين مذهب من قال : إنه يخلق فعلا يفهم عند ذلك أنه تعالى يريد من العبد فعلا أو يكرهه ، وإن أضيف إليه الكلام كان مجازا ، كما قال تعالى خطابًا للسماء والأرض : ( انْتِيَا طَوْعاً أَوْ كَرْهًا قَالَتَا أَتَيْنَا طَابِعِينَ ) [فصلت : 11]، فلم يجز من حيث الحقيقة كلام هو اقتضاء وأمر وإيجاب من تعالى، ولا تسلم وائتمار واستيجاب من السماء والأرض من حيث القول. قالوا : طريقنا في إثبات كونه متكلما هو المعجزات الدالة على صدق قول الأنبياء عليهم السلام، وهم الصادقون المخبرون عن الله تعالى أنه قال كذا، وأمر بكذا، ونهى عن كذا.
The Ash'aris said: You have built your doctrine on two bases: one of them is your affirmation that God the Exalted is speaking (mutakallim), and the second is that the definition and reality of 'speaker' is from performing speech. As for the first, if the Sabian and the philosopher were to dispute with you about His being speaking, what is your proof against them? According to you, speech is an action of God like other actions, and no ruling of speech refers back to Him except that He is an agent and maker. So what is the proof that He is speaking? I mean, that He is described by a meaning that subsists in another locus. What is the difference between your doctrine and the doctrine of those who say that He creates an action by which it is understood that He wills or dislikes an action from the servant, and if speech is attributed to Him, it is metaphorical, as God said addressing the heavens and the earth: 'Come both of you willingly or unwillingly; they said we come obediently' [41:11]. So it is not permissible in reality that speech is a demand, command, and obligation from God, nor does He affirm compliance and obedience from the heavens and the earth in terms of speech. They said: Our method in establishing that He is speaking is the miracles that indicate the truthfulness of the prophets, peace be upon them, who are truthful, informing about God that He said such-and-such, commanded such-and-such, and forbade such-and-such.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: first, the Ash'ari objection to the opponent's doctrine, and second, the opponent's response. The Ash'aris begin by stating that the opponent's doctrine rests on two bases: affirmation that God is mutakallim (speaking) and the definition that a 'speaker' is one who performs speech. They then challenge the first base: if a Sabian or philosopher denies that God is speaking, what proof does the opponent have? Since the opponent regards speech as an action like any other, with no special attribute in God beyond being an agent, the proof for His being mutakallim is unclear. The Ash'aris further demand the difference between their view and those who say God merely creates an action conveying His will or dislike, and that attributing speech to Him is metaphorical. They cite the Quranic verse about the heavens and earth responding to God's command, arguing it is not literal speech. In response, the opponent says their proof is miracles (mu'jizat) that confirm the truthfulness of the prophets, who report that God spoke, commanded, and forbade. The argument therefore shifts to the reliability of prophetic testimony supported by miracles.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'aris are being challenged in this passage.
Correction: The Ash'aris are the challengers; they object to the opponent's doctrine, and the opponent responds.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris challenge the rational proof of God's speech, but the opponent relies on miracles to validate prophetic testimony.

?
The opponent in this passage grounds their belief in the truthfulness of the prophets proven by miracles. In your daily life, do you live as if God has truly spoken, or do you treat His commands as mere suggestions? How can you change to take His speech more seriously?

Objections to Proving Divine Speech Through Miracles
The author recounts the opponents' method and then presents three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three objections raised against proving divine speech through miracles, and why this method alone is insufficient.

Core Idea
Miracles alone cannot prove God's speech without a consistent framework for divine attributes.

This passage presents a debate about how to prove that God is a speaker. The opponents claim that miracles confirm the prophets, who then report God's speech. The author counters with three logical objections, showing that this method leads to inconsistencies. Let's explore each objection and see why the opponents' approach fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' claim
Start by reading the first sentence: the opponents say that their method to prove God's speech is through miracles that confirm the prophets' truthfulness. Understand this as their starting position.
2
Analyze the first objection
The first objection argues that if God had not sent a prophet, the opponents would have no way to prove His speech, even though they believe knowledge of God is obligatory. This exposes a dependence on prophets.
This points out a gap in their reasoning.
3
Examine the second and third objections
The second objection questions why speech specifically is attributed to God if it is just an act among acts. The third objection highlights inconsistency in how they treat will (created not in a locus) vs speech (created in a locus).
Notice the symmetry: both attribute created things to God, yet they treat them differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : طريقنا في إثبات كونه متكلما هو المعجزات الدالة على صدق قول الأنبياء عليهم السلام، وهم الصادقون المخبرون عن الله تعالى أنه قال كذا، وأمر بكذا، ونهى عن كذا). قيل لهم : قد سددتم على أنفسكم هذه الطريقة بوجوه ؛ أحدها: أنكم زعمتم أنه لو لم يبعث الله رسولا كان على العاقل البالغ في عقله وجوب معرفة الله تعالى، وعلى الباري تعالى وجوب ثوابه، فلو لم يبعث الله رسولا فيم كنتم تثبتون كونه متكلما ؟ والثاني : مصيركم إلى أن الكلام فعل من الأفعال، فما دليلكم على أنه فعل ذلك الفعل الخاص، إذ ليس كل مقدور فهو واقع بفعل الله تعالى؟ والثالث : زعمتم في الإرادة أنها مخلوقة لا في محل، وفي الكلام أنه مخلوق في محل، وفي الأمرين جميعا يرجع الحكم الأخص إلى الباري تعالى، فما الفرق بين البابين؟
They said: 'Our method in establishing that He is a speaker (mutakallim) is the miracles that indicate the truth of the statements of the prophets (peace be upon them), who are the truthful ones informing about Allah Almighty that He said such-and-such, commanded such-and-such, and prohibited such-and-such.' It was said to them: 'You have blocked this method against yourselves in several ways: First, you claimed that if Allah had not sent a messenger, it would be obligatory upon the rational adult in his intellect to know Allah Almighty, and upon the Exalted Creator to reward him. So if Allah had not sent a messenger, by what would you prove that He is a speaker? Second, your conclusion that speech is an act among acts – what is your proof that He performed that specific act, since not every possible thing occurs by the act of Allah Almighty? Third, you claimed concerning will (al-irada) that it is created not in a locus, and concerning speech that it is created in a locus, and in both matters the specific judgment refers back to the Exalted Creator; what is the difference between the two cases?'
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and counter-objection. It begins by quoting the opponents' claim: their method for proving that God is a speaker (mutakallim) is through miracles, which confirm the veracity of the prophets who report God's commands and prohibitions. Then the author addresses them directly, stating that they have closed off this method for themselves in three ways. The first objection: the opponents hold that even without a prophet, a rational adult must know God and God must reward him. Therefore, if no prophet were sent, they would have no way to prove God's speech, contradicting their reliance on miracles. The second: they consider speech an act; but not every act is performed by God, so they need to prove God specifically performed that act. The third: they claim that will is created not in a locus while speech is created in a locus, yet both are attributed to God; the author asks why speech and will are treated differently if both are created.

Key Terms
متكلّم (mutakallim)
معجزات (miracles)
كلام (speech)
إرادة (will)
فعل (act)
محل (locus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles alone suffice to prove all divine attributes, including speech.
Correction: The objections show that relying on miracles without a consistent theological framework leads to logical gaps, especially regarding the necessity of a prophet and the nature of speech as an act.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, what is the difference between how the opponents treat will (al-irada) and speech (al-kalam)?
A) Will is eternal and speech is created
B) Will is created in a locus and speech is not
C) Will is created not in a locus and speech is created in a locus
D) Both are created in a locus

Key Takeaway
The three objections expose contradictions in using miracles to prove divine speech without addressing deeper theological assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your entire understanding of God's speech depended solely on reports from messengers, what would be left of your faith in a scenario where no messenger came? This lesson forces you to ask: Is your conviction rooted in evidence or in mere tradition? What would you do if that foundation were removed?
?
The third objection exposes inconsistency in holding different standards for different attributes. Where in your own beliefs about God do you accept contradictions without noticing them? This lesson demands internal consistency. What is one inconsistency you need to resolve today?
?
The second objection asks: how do you know God performed that specific act? When you attribute actions to God, do you have proof, or do you assume? This lesson calls you to examine every claim you make about God. Will you investigate your assumptions, or remain comfortable in contradiction?

Speech as Meaning: The Two Aspects of Attribution
The author defines speech as a meaning and distinguishes its two relational aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that speech is a meaning subsisting in a locus, and why it has two distinct attributions to the agent and to the locus.

Core Idea
Speech is a meaning; its creation relates to the agent, its subsistence to the locus.

In this passage, the author lays the conceptual foundation for understanding divine speech. He asserts that speech is a meaning (ma'na) that inheres in a locus, and that every meaning has two aspects: its origination (huduth) traces to the agent, while its realization (tahaqquq) traces to the locus. Confusing these leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the definition of speech as meaning.
2
Identify the two aspects
The agent is described by the aspect of creation (huduth); the locus by the aspect of subsistence (tahaqquq).
These are two distinct rational aspects.
3
Reflect on the claim
Why does the author insist these are two separate aspects? What would conflating them imply?
It would lead to making the agent identical to the locus, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : ليس يشك العاقل أن الكلام معنى من المعاني، سواء كان ذلك المعنى عبارة منظومة من حروف منظومة وأصوات مقطعة، أو كان صفة نفسية ونطقاً عقلية من غير حرف وصوت وكل معنى قائم بمحل وصف المحل به لا محالة، وذلك المعنى من حيث هو مخلوق مفعول ينسب إلى الفاعل، ومن حيث هو معنى قام بمحل إلى المحل، فمحل المعنى موصوف به لا محالة، فالذي وصف الفاعل به هو وجه حدوثه منتسبا إلى قدرته حتى يقال : محدث ، والذي وصف المحل به هو وجه تحققه منتسباً إلى ذاته القابلة له الموصوفة به وهذان وجهان معقولان ليس يتمارى فيهما عاقل، فجعلهما وجها واحدًا حتى يكون معنى كونه فاعلا هو معنى كونه موصوفا به خروج عن المعقول ومكابرة العقل، ولا يرتفع المعنى المعقول بالاصطلاح على أن معنى المتكلم هو الفاعل للكلام. ومما يدل على ذلك دلالة واضحة أن الكلام عند الخصم عبارة عن حروف منظومة وأصوات مقطعة، ومن المعلوم الذي لا مراء فيه أن الصوت أعم من الكلام، فإن كل كلام عنده صوت وليس كل صوت كلاماً، ثم الصوت إذا قام به سمي المحل مصوتًا، ولا يرجع حكم الصوت إلى فاعل الصوت حتى يقال : الباري تعالى إذا خلق أصواتا في محل هو مصوت والكلام الذي هو أخص كيف يرجع حكمه إليه حتى يقال : هو متكلم بكلام يخلقه في محل، بل يرجع حكم الصوت من حيث هو معنى من المعاني إلى المحل، ويرجع حكم الفعل من حيث هو فعل من الأفعال إلى الفاعل، كذلك الكلام الذي هو أخص منه، فيا عجبًا بأن صار أخص صار حكمه أعم، أو ليست الحركة إذا قامت بمحل سمي بها متحركا، سواء كانت الحركة ضرورية أو اختيارية، ثم إذا خصصت الحركة بأن كانت أصواتا تسمع أو حروفًا تفهم أو كلمات تعقل انقطع حكمها عن المحال، وعاد ما يجب اختصاصه بالمحل إلى الفاعل الذي لا ينسب إليه إلا الأعم، فعرف من هذه الوجوه أن اختصاص الكلام بالمحل الذي قام به لم يبطل. ومن الدليل على ذلك أن من سمع كلاما من الغير علم على القطع والبتات أنه المتكلم به، ولا يقف معرفة ذلك على معرفة كونه فاعلا، بل ربما لا يخطر بباله
Then we say: The rational person does not doubt that speech is a meaning (ma'na) among meanings, whether that meaning is a composed expression of ordered letters and segmented sounds, or it is a psychic attribute and intellectual utterance without letter and sound. And every meaning subsisting in a locus (mahall) necessitates that the locus is described by it. That meaning, insofar as it is created and effected, is attributed to the agent (fa'il), and insofar as it is a meaning subsisting in a locus, to the locus. So the locus of the meaning is described by it necessarily. Therefore, that by which the agent is described is the aspect of its occurrence (huduth) as related to His power, so that it is called 'originated' (muhdath); and that by which the locus is described is the aspect of its realization (tahaqquq) as related to its receptive essence that is described by it. These are two rational aspects about which no rational person disputes. So making them one aspect so that the meaning of 'He is an agent' is the meaning of 'He is described by it' is a departure from reason and a contradiction of intellect. And the rational meaning is not overridden by the convention that the meaning of 'speaker' is the doer of speech. And among what indicates that clearly is that speech according to the opponent is composed letters and segmented sounds. It is known without dispute that sound is more general than speech, for every speech according to him is sound but not every sound is speech. Then when sound subsists in a locus, the locus is called 'sounding' (musawwit), and the judgment of sound does not refer to the agent of the sound such that it is said: The Exalted Creator, when He creates sounds in a locus, He is sounding. Then how can the judgment of speech, which is more specific, refer to Him so that He is called 'speaker' (mutakallim) by speech He creates in a locus? Rather, the judgment of sound insofar as it is a meaning among meanings refers to the locus, and the judgment of the act insofar as it is an act among acts refers to the agent. Likewise, speech which is more specific [should also refer to the locus]. So it is astonishing that when something becomes more specific, its judgment becomes more general. Or is it not that motion, when it subsists in a locus, the locus is called 'moving' (mutaharrik), whether the motion is necessary or voluntary? Then when motion is specified as sounds heard, letters understood, or words conceived, its judgment is cut off from the locus and returns to the agent, to whom only the general is attributed? So it is known from these points that the specificity of speech to the locus in which it subsists is not invalidated. And among the evidence for that is that one who hears speech from another knows with certainty and definitively that he is the speaker of it, and his knowledge of that does not depend on knowing that he is the agent; rather, perhaps it does not even occur to his mind.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument defending the Ash'ari position that speech is a meaning subsisting in a locus, and that God is called 'speaker' as the creator of speech in a locus, not as the one in whom speech subsists. The author begins by asserting that speech is a meaning (ma'na), whether composed of letters and sounds or a psychic attribute. Every meaning inheres in a locus and describes it. The meaning has two aspects: as created, it is attributed to the agent; as subsisting, to the locus. Conflating these two aspects is irrational. The author then provides a clear proof: according to the opponent, speech is composed of letters and sounds. Sound is more general than speech; when sound inheres in a locus, the locus is called 'sounding', not the agent. Similarly speech, being more specific, should also attribute to the locus. He criticizes the opponent for making the specific judgment more general. He uses the analogy of motion: if motion inheres, the locus is called moving; if motion is specified as speech, the judgment should remain with the locus, not shift to the agent. Finally, he gives a direct experiential proof: when one hears speech from another, one immediately knows the speaker without considering agency. This shows that the attribute of speech pertains to the locus, not the agent. The passage ends mid-sentence, suggesting a continuation.

Key Terms
معنى (meaning)
محل (locus)
فاعل (agent)
كلام (speech)
حدث (origination)
متكلم (speaker)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling God 'speaker' implies speech inheres in Him.
Correction: The author insists speech is created in a locus external to God; God is 'speaker' as the creator of speech, not as its locus.

Checkpoint
According to the author, when speech is considered as created, to whom is it attributed?
A) The locus
B) The agent
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Speech, as a meaning, has two distinct relational aspects: its creation links to the agent, its subsistence links to the locus; conflating them is irrational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you consider it as created speech subsisting in your tongue, while the Creator is the agent? How does this distinction shape your understanding of God's transcendence?
?
This passage warns against conflating the two aspects of speech. In your own concept of divine attributes, have you ever blurred the line between God's eternal essence and His temporal acts? What step can you take today to sharpen that distinction?
?
If the rational meaning of 'speaker' cannot be overridden by convention, then how does this challenge your assumptions about who truly 'speaks' when you hear the Quran recited? Will you now reflect on the reality of God's creative speech every time you listen?

The Logical Proof: Sound, Speech, and the Locus of Attribute
The author provides a logical proof using the general-specific relation of sound and speech, and a motion analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that speech, being more specific than sound, must be attributed to the locus just as sound is, and the analogy of motion reinforces this.

Core Idea
Specificity does not change the locus of attribution; speech follows sound's rule.

The author now provides a clear logical proof. He takes the opponent's own definition of speech as composed letters and sounds, and shows that sound is attributed to the locus where it occurs, not to the agent. Since speech is a specific type of sound, the same rule applies. He also uses the analogy of motion: if motion is specified as speech, its judgment should not shift from the locus to the agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
The author starts by stating the opponent's definition of speech as letters and sounds.
2
Understand the general-specific relationship
Sound is more general than speech; every speech is sound but not every sound is speech.
3
Apply the rule of sound
When sound subsists in a locus, the locus is called 'sounding', not the agent. So speech, being a type of sound, should also be attributed to the locus.
The author criticizes the opponent for making the specific judgment more general.
4
Consider the motion analogy
Motion in a locus makes the locus 'moving'; specifying motion as speech should not change the locus's description.
This analogy reinforces the point.

Misconception: Because speech is created by God, it should be attributed exclusively to Him.

Checkpoint
What does the motion analogy demonstrate?
A) Motion is not like speech
B) Specifying motion as speech changes its locus
C) Specifying motion as speech should not change the locus's description
D) Motion is always voluntary

Key Takeaway
The specificity of speech among sounds does not transfer its attribution from the locus to the agent; the attribute remains with the locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you listen to the Qur'an being recited, do you unconsciously attribute the speech to the reciter's voice and effort? This lesson forces you to recognize that the attribute of speech truly resides in the Qur'an itself, not in the human agent. How will you train your heart to focus on the divine origin of every recited verse, starting with the next recitation you hear?
?
In your daily life, you often say 'I spoke' or 'I moved'—but are you ever aware that these actions are attributes subsisting in you, with Allah as the ultimate agent? This lesson distinguishes between the locus (you) and the agent (Allah). Will you begin to say 'Allah enabled me to speak' instead of 'I spoke'? What is one concrete situation today where you will practice this shift?
?
The opponent in this lesson made an inconsistent exception by attributing speech to the agent despite the general rule. Consider your own life: where do you make exceptions for yourself that you would not accept for others? This lesson demands intellectual honesty. What is one area where you have been inconsistent in applying Islamic principles, and what step will you take today to correct it?

The Experiential Proof: Hearing Speech Directly Identifies the Speaker
The author gives an experiential proof that speech is attributed to the speaker directly, not to the agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that direct human experience of hearing speech attributes it to the speaker (the locus) without needing to consider agency.

Core Idea
When you hear speech, you know the speaker immediately, not the creator.

The author presents a final experiential proof. He observes that when someone hears speech from another person, they immediately and certainly know that the person is the speaker, without any thought of whether that person is the agent (the creator) of the speech. This shows that the attribute of speech belongs to the locus (the speaker), not to the agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the experiential proof
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the intuitive knowledge.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.
2
Identify the key observation
The hearer knows the speaker with certainty without considering agency.
3
Reflect on the implication
How does this support the Ash'ari position that speech is a meaning subsisting in the speaker?
It shows that the natural attribution of speech is to the locus (the speaker).

He uses the analogy of motion: if motion inheres, the locus is called moving; if motion is specified as speech, the judgment should remain with the locus, not shift to the agent. Finally, he gives a direct experiential proof: when one hears speech from another, one immediately knows the speaker without considering agency. This shows that the attribute of speech pertains to the locus, not the agent. The passage ends mid-sentence, suggesting a continuation.

Key Terms
كلام (speech)
متكلم (speaker)
فاعل (agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: We attribute speech to the agent because he created it.
Correction: Experience shows we attribute speech to the speaker (the locus) without any thought of creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does a person know immediately upon hearing speech?
A) Who created the speech
B) Who the speaker is
C) Both A and B
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Direct human experience confirms that speech is attributed to the speaker, not to the creator.

Speech Belongs to the Substrate, Not the Creator
A refutation arguing that speech is attributed to its substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why speech, as a type of sound, must be attributed to the substrate in which it subsists, not to the agent who creates it.

Core Idea
Speech, being a species of sound, is attributed to the substrate, not the creator.

This lesson examines a key argument from Nihayat al-Aqdam that challenges a common view on divine speech. The author argues that if speech is composed of sounds and letters, then like sound, its ruling (attribution) belongs to the substrate where it occurs, not to the agent. This distinction is crucial for understanding the Islamic theological debate on the nature of the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: the author starts by rejecting the opponent's view, then builds a proof using the relationship between sound and speech.
The passage uses dense argumentation; read slowly.
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify the terms 'sound' (الصوت) and 'speech' (الكلام), and note how the author uses the fact that sound is more general than speech.
This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the analogy with motion
The author compares speech to motion. When motion occurs in a body, the body is called 'moving'. Similarly, when speech occurs in a substrate, the substrate should be called 'speaking'.
This analogy strengthens the conclusion.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that the attribution of speech to the substrate cannot be invalidated, despite the opponent's attempts to assign it to the agent.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موصوفا به خروج عن المعقول ومكابرة العقل، ولا يرتفع المعنى المعقول بالاصطلاح على أن معنى المتكلم هو الفاعل للكلام. ومما يدل على ذلك دلالة واضحة أن الكلام عند الخصم عبارة عن حروف منظومة وأصوات مقطعة، ومن المعلوم الذي لا مراء فيه أن الصوت أعم من الكلام، فإن كل كلام عنده صوت وليس كل صوت كلاماً، ثم الصوت إذا قام به سمي المحل مصوتًا، ولا يرجع حكم الصوت إلى فاعل الصوت حتى يقال: الباري تعالى إذا خلق أصواتا في محل هو مصوت والكلام الذي هو أخص كيف يرجع حكمه إليه حتى يقال: هو متكلم بكلام يخلقه في محل، بل يرجع حكم الصوت من حيث هو معنى من المعاني إلى المحل، ويرجع حكم الفعل من حيث هو فعل من الأفعال إلى الفاعل، كذلك الكلام الذي هو أخص منه، فيا عجبًا بأن صار أخص صار حكمه أعم، أو ليست الحركة إذا قامت بمحل سمي بها متحركا، سواء كانت الحركة ضرورية أو اختيارية، ثم إذا خصصت الحركة بأن كانت أصواتا تسمع أو حروفًا تفهم أو كلمات تعقل انقطع حكمها عن المحال، وعاد ما يجب اختصاصه بالمحل إلى الفاعل الذي لا ينسب إليه إلا الأعم، فعرف من هذه الوجوه أن اختصاص الكلام بالمحل الذي قام به لم يبطل.
Being described by it is contrary to reason and an absurdity against the intellect, and the rational meaning is not negated by convention that the meaning of 'speaker' is the doer of speech. And among the proofs indicating that is a clear indication that speech, according to the opponent, consists of arranged letters and segmented sounds, and it is known without dispute that sound is more general than speech, for every speech according to him is sound but not every sound is speech. Then when sound subsists in a substrate, the substrate is called 'sounding', and the ruling of sound does not return to the doer of sound, so that it could be said: the Exalted Creator, when He creates sounds in a substrate, is sounding, and speech, which is more specific, how could its ruling return to Him so that He is called 'speaker' by a speech He creates in a substrate? Rather, the ruling of sound, insofar as it is a meaning among meanings, returns to the substrate, and the ruling of the act, insofar as it is an act among acts, returns to the agent. Similarly, speech, which is more specific than it. So how astonishing that when it became more specific, its ruling became more general! Or does not motion, when it subsists in a substrate, the substrate is called 'moving' by it, whether the motion is necessary or voluntary? Then when motion is specified into sounds that are heard, or letters that are understood, or words that are comprehended, its ruling is cut off from the substrate and returns what must be specific to the substrate to the agent who is only attributed with the more general? So it is known from these considerations that the specific attribution of speech to the substrate in which it subsists has not been invalidated.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that the speaker is the one who creates speech. The author first states that attributing speech to the creator disregards reason. Then he presents a proof: speech is composed of letters and sounds, and sound is more general than speech. When sound subsists in a substrate, that substrate is called 'sounding', not the creator of the sound. Similarly, speech must be attributed to the substrate. The author marvels at the inconsistency: why would making the attribute more specific shift its ruling to the agent? He uses the analogy of motion: if motion subsists in a body, the body is called 'moving'; if that motion is specified to sounds, etc., the ruling should not transfer to the agent. Therefore, speech remains attributed to the substrate.

Key Terms
الكلام (speech)
الصوت (sound)
المحل (substrate)
الفاعل (agent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The one who creates speech is the speaker.
Correction: The author argues that speech, being sound, is attributed to the substrate where it occurs, not to the creator.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between sound and speech?
A) They are identical.
B) Sound is more general than speech.
C) Speech is more general than sound.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Speech, as a type of sound, must be attributed to the substrate in which it subsists, not to the agent who creates it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Immediate Knowledge of the Speaker
The author argues from common linguistic usage that speech is immediately attributed to the speaker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof from everyday experience that speech is directly attributed to the speaker, not to the creator of the sound.

Core Idea
When you hear speech, you know the speaker directly, without thinking about who created the sound.

In this passage, the author presents a second argument for the attribute of speech. He draws on everyday human experience: when we hear someone speak, we immediately attribute that speech to the person we hear, not to some unseen creator. This natural linguistic usage shows that speech is an attribute of the speaker himself, not merely an act of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the key terms: المتكلم (speaker) and الفاعل (doer).
2
Identify the key claim
The author states that the hearer knows the speaker with certainty without considering whether the speaker is also the doer (creator). Find that statement in the text.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Examine the linguistic examples
The author uses commands like 'say' and 'do' to show that they are both attributed to the speaker in the same way. Reflect on how this supports the claim.
Language usage is the evidence.
4
Understand the analogy
The author draws an analogy with motion: the most specific description of motion returns to the substrate it subsists in. Similarly, speech's most specific description should return to its substrate—the speaker. See how this analogy strengthens the argument.
This analogy makes the argument conclusive.
5
Reflect on the decisiveness
Consider why the author calls this proof 'conclusive, with no answer to it'. This underscores the strength of the argument from direct experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الدليل على ذلك أن من سمع كلاما من الغير علم على القطع والبتات أنه المتكلم به، ولا يقف معرفة ذلك على معرفة كونه فاعلا، بل ربما لا يخطر بباله كونه فاعلا أصلا، وعن هذا انتسب إليه القول، فيقال: قال ويقول وهو قائل، ويخطاب الأمر والنهي يخاطب قل ولا تقل، ويفرق الفارق ضرورة فرقًا معقولا، بين قولهم: قل وبين قولهم افعل فإن مساق قولهم: قل هو بعينه مساق قولهم: تحرك واسكن وقم واقعد، وإن كان المعنى في الموضعين مخلوقا متكسباً بالوجهين كما عرفت ثم رجع أخص وصف الحركة إلى المحل الذي قامت به الحركة، كذلك القول ينبغي أن يرجع أخص وصفه إلى المحل الذي قام به، وهذا قاطع لا جواب عنه ونحرر هذا المعنى.

And because of this, speech is ascribed to him, so it is said: 'he said' and 'he says', and he is a 'speaker'. And the address of command and prohibition addresses 'say' and 'do not say', and the distinguisher distinguishes necessarily a rational distinction between their saying 'say' and their saying 'do', for the usage of their saying 'say' is exactly the same as the usage of their saying 'move, be still, stand, sit', although the meaning in both cases is created and acquired in two ways as you have learned. Then the most specific description of motion returned to the substrate in which motion subsisted. Likewise, speech should have its most specific description return to the substrate in which it subsisted. And this is conclusive, with no answer to it, and we refine this meaning.

Commentary
This passage is a second proof for the same thesis. The author argues from common human experience: when we hear speech, we directly attribute it to the person from whom we hear it, without needing to consider whether that person created it or not. For example, if someone says 'qul' (say), we immediately know the speaker is the one uttering the command. The structure of commands like 'say' and 'do' is the same; both are attributed to the speaker, not to the creator of the sound. Therefore, the most specific description of speech must return to the substrate where the speech subsists. This proof is presented as decisive.

الفاعل (doer)
