The Hierarchy of Accidents: Proving God's Speech
The author uses a hierarchy of accidents to argue that speech must be attributed to the substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hierarchy of accidental attributes logically entails that the substrate (God) must be described as a speaker.

Core Idea
Speech is not a separate accident; it is inherent in the chain of attributes.

The author presents a chain of attributes from the most general (accidentalness) to the most specific (speech). By showing that each attribute is a subset of the previous, he argues that if we affirm the substrate has motion, we must also affirm it has speech. This is a key argument in Ash'ari theology for God's attribute of speech being eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the chain: accidentalness > modality > motion > sound > letters > speech.
2
Identify the logical structure
The author uses a 'greater general to less general' hierarchy. Each term is more specific. He then argues that if the substrate is described by the general, it must be described by the specific.
This is an argument from containment.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the substrate (body, and by extension God) must be described as speaking (having speech). This refutes those who deny God's speech.
The final sentence dismisses alternative views as irrational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : العرضية أعم من الكونية، والكونية أعم من الحركية، والحركة أعم من الصوت والصوت أعم من الحروف والحروف أعم من الكلام، ثم يجب وصف المحل بكونه موصوفا بعرض، وذلك العرض بعينه كون، والكون بعينه حركة، والحركة بعينها صوت والصوت حرف، والحرف كلام، فيجب أن يوصف المحل بكونه ذا كلام ومتكلما، كما وجب وصفه بكونه ذا حركة ومتحركا، ومن جعل المعنيين معنى واحدا، وسمى النسبتين نسبة واحدة كان عن المعقول خارجًا.
And we say: accidentalness is more general than modality, and modality is more general than motion, and motion is more general than sound, and sound is more general than letters, and letters are more general than speech. Then it is necessary to describe the substrate as being qualified by an accident, and that accident is precisely modality, and modality is precisely motion, and motion is precisely sound, and sound is a letter, and the letter is speech. So it is necessary to describe the substrate as having speech and being a speaker, just as it was necessary to describe it as having motion and being moving. And whoever makes the two meanings one meaning and calls the two relations one relation is departing from reason.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument from the hierarchy of accidental attributes. The author posits a chain: 'aradiyya (accident in general) > kawniyya (modality) > harakiyya (motion) > sawt (sound) > huruf (letters) > kalam (speech). Each more specific attribute is a subset of the more general. Since the substrate is described by the general accident, it must also be described by the specific ones. Therefore, the substrate must be described as having speech and being a speaker. To separate speech from the substrate while allowing motion to remain is irrational.

Key Terms
العرضية (accidentalness)
الكونية (modality)
الحركية (motion)
الصوت (sound)
الحروف (letters)
الكلام (speech)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speech is a kind of attribute that is entirely different and separate from other accidents like motion.
Correction: The author shows that speech is actually continuous with other accidents, as it is a further specification of sound, which is a specification of motion, and so on.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which is more general: sound or speech?
A) Speech is more general.
B) Sound is more general.
C) They are equal.
D) Neither is general.

Key Takeaway
The substrate must be described with speech because speech is the endpoint of the chain of accidental attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every word you speak is part of a hierarchy that culminates in God's own speech, how does that change the way you use your tongue today? Will you commit to removing one idle or harmful word from your speech this week?

?

The Delirious Person Argument
The author argues from a hypothetical case of delirium.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hypothetical of a delirious person proves that the speaker is the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who creates it.

Core Idea
A delirious person's speech shows the speaker is the substrate of speech.

The author presents a clever thought experiment: If God creates speech in a delirious person, who is the speaker? By examining the only two logical possibilities, the author demonstrates that the speaker must be the one in whom speech resides, thereby refuting the opposing view. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical scenario
Imagine God creates speech in a delirious person who utters the words 'I stood and I sat.' This is the starting point of the argument.
The specific words are chosen to show falsehood if attributed to God.
2
Examine the two possibilities
The author presents two options: either God is the speaker of those words, or the delirious person is. Consider the consequences of each.
Option 1 leads to absurdity (God saying a false claim). Option 2 is evident.
3
Conclude the refutation
Since the second option is unavoidable, it proves that the speaker is the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who creates it. This invalidates the opponents' definition.
Thus, the speaker is the one with whom the speech attribute resides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتمسك به في دفع قولهم المتكلم من فعل الكلام : أن الله تعالى لو خلق في المبرسم وبعض الممرورين أن قال بلسانه : قمت وقعدت لم يخل الحال من أحد أمرين؛ إما أن يقال : يكون المتكلم بهذه الحروف المنظومة والأصوات المقطعة خالقها وفاعلها، فيلزم أن يكون الباري قائلا : قمت وقعدت، وإما أن يقال : المتكلم بهذه الكلمات هو صاحب البرسام دون غيره، فقد بطل قولهم : إن المتكلم ليس من قام به الكلام، بل من فعل الكلام دون غيره.
And among the things to hold to in refuting their statement that the speaker is the one who does the speech: that if the Exalted God created in the delirious (al-mubarsam) and some of the melancholic that he says with his tongue: 'I stood and I sat' (qumtu wa qa'adtu), the situation would not be free from one of two matters: either it is said that the speaker of these arranged letters and segmented sounds is their Creator and Doer, so it would be necessary that the Creator said 'I stood and I sat'; or it is said that the speaker of these words is the owner of the delirium (sahib al-birsam) and no other. Then their statement is invalidated: that the speaker is not the one in whom speech subsists, but rather the one who does the speech without other.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a final proof and refutation. The author presents a hypothetical: if God creates speech in a delirious person who says 'I stood and I sat' (qumtu wa qa'adtu), then either God is the speaker (which is absurd because it would attribute a false statement to God) or the delirious person is the speaker. Since the latter is evident, it proves that the speaker is the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who creates it. Thus, the opponents' claim that the speaker is the doer of speech is invalid.

Key Terms
المبرسم (delirious)
المتكلم (speaker)
الفاعل (doer)
الكلام (speech)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God can create speech in a person without that person being the speaker.
Correction: The argument shows that if speech occurs in a person, that person is the speaker, regardless of who created the speech.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the example of the delirious person?
A) God is the speaker of all speech.
B) The person is the speaker of that speech.
C) The speech is not real.
D) The delirious person is not a speaker.

Key Takeaway
The delirious person argument proves that the speaker is the one in whom speech exists, not the one who creates it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The delirious person speaks without awareness. When you speak without thinking, are you still the speaker? How does this truth affect the way you see the weight of every word that leaves your tongue?

If God creates the sound of your words, but you are the speaker—then what is your excuse for every harmful word you have ever said? Will you hold yourself accountable, or blame the Creator?
?
The argument shows that the speaker is the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who creates it. Does this not place full responsibility on you for your speech? How will you guard your tongue from this moment onward?

Speech Inheres in a Subject Like Motion
Argument that speech's specific description returns to its subject as with motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that speech must inhere in a subject like motion, and why the subject is truly described as a speaker.

Core Idea
Speech inheres in a subject just as motion inheres in a body.

The author argues that speech is not merely a created action external to God, but must be an inherent attribute. Drawing an analogy with motion, he shows that the most specific description of speech returns to its subject. The passage then refines this with a hierarchy of generality from accidentality down to speech, proving that the subject must be described as a speaker.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and initial attribution
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author begins by attributing 'saying' (al-qawl) to God because He is an agent.
The term 'qa'il' (speaker) is derived from this attribution.
2
Distinguish between 'say' and 'do'
Observe the rational distinction between the command 'say' and 'do'. Both are commands, but 'say' specifically concerns speech, while 'do' concerns action.
3
Follow the analogy with motion
Understand the analogy: just as the most specific description of motion (e.g., 'moving') returns to the subject in which motion inheres, so the most specific description of speech must return to its subject.
The author calls this decisive and unanswerable.
4
Study the hierarchy of generality
Trace the hierarchy from the most general (accidentality) to the most specific (speech). Note how each level entails the next, culminating in the subject being described as a speaker.
The hierarchy: accidentality → spatial mode → motion → sound → letters → speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كونه فاعلا أصلا، وعن هذا انتسب إليه القول، فيقال: قال ويقول وهو قائل، ويخطاب الأمر والنهي يخاطب قل ولا تقل، ويفرق الفارق ضرورة فرقا معقولا بين قولهم: قل، وبين قولهم: افعل، فإن مساق قولهم: قل هو بعينه مساق قولهم: تحرك واسكن وقم واقعد، وإن كان المعنى في الموضعين مخلوقا متكسبا بالوجهين كما عرفت. ثم رجع أخص وصف الحركة إلى المحل الذي قامت به الحركة، كذلك القول ينبغي أن يرجع أخص وصفه إلى المحل الذي قام به، وهذا قاطع لا جواب عنه. ونحرر هذا المعنى. ونقول: العرضية أعم من الكونية، والكونية أعم من الحركية، والحركة أعم من الصوت، والصوت أعم من الحروف، والحروف أعم من الكلام، ثم يجب وصف المحل بكونه موصوفا بعرض، وذلك العرض بعينه كون، والكون بعينه حركة، والحركة بعينها صوت، والصوت حرف، والحرف كلام، فيجب أن يوصف المحل بكونه ذا كلام ومتكلما، كما وجب وصفه بكونه ذا حركة ومتحركا، ومن جعل المعنيين معنى واحدا، وسمى النسبتين نسبة واحدة كان عن المعقول خارجا.
That He is an agent originally, and on account of this the saying (al-qawl) is attributed to Him, so it is said: he said and he says, and he is a speaker (qa'il). And the address of command and prohibition addresses 'say' and 'do not say'. And the distinguisher distinguishes necessarily a rational distinction between their saying 'say' and their saying 'do', for the context of their saying 'say' is exactly the context of their saying 'move', 'be still', 'stand', and 'sit', even though the meaning in both places is created and acquired in both respects as you have known. Then the most specific description of motion returns to the subject (al-mahall) in which motion inheres; likewise, the most specific description of speech (al-qawl) should return to the subject in which it inheres. This is decisive and has no answer. And we refine this meaning. We say: 'accidentality' is more general than 'spatialness', and 'spatialness' is more general than 'motionality', and 'motionality' is more general than 'sound', and 'sound' is more general than 'letters', and 'letters' are more general than 'speech' (al-kalam). Then it is necessary that the subject be described as having an accident, and that accident is exactly a spatial mode (kawn), and the spatial mode is exactly a motion, and the motion is exactly a sound, and the sound is a letter, and the letter is speech. So it is necessary that the subject be described as having speech and being a speaker (mutakallim), just as it was necessary to describe it as having motion and being in motion. And whoever makes the two meanings one meaning and calls the two relations one relation has departed from reason.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument that speech must inhere in a subject and that the subject is truly described as a speaker. The author begins by noting that the term 'saying' is attributed to God because He is an agent. He then distinguishes between the command 'say' and the command 'do': both are commands, but 'say' is specifically about speech. Using an analogy with motion, he argues that just as the most specific description of motion (e.g., 'moving') returns to the subject in which motion inheres, so the most specific description of speech must return to the subject in which speech inheres. This is presented as decisive and unanswerable. He then refines the argument with a hierarchy of generality: accidentality is more general than spatial mode, spatial mode more general than motion, motion more general than sound, sound more general than letters, letters more general than speech. Therefore, the subject that is described as having an accident is the same subject that is described as having each of these specifics in turn, culminating in speech. Hence the subject must be described as a speaker (mutakallim). He concludes that anyone who conflates these distinctions—making, for example, the speaker the creator of speech rather than the one in whom speech inheres—has departed from reason.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that speech is an action external to the subject's essence, like creating speech in another.
Correction: The author shows that speech must inhere in the subject like motion, so the subject is truly a speaker, not merely an agent who creates speech.

Checkpoint
According to the hierarchy in the passage, which of the following is most specific?
A) Sound
B) Speech
C) Motion
D) Spatial mode

Key Takeaway
Speech is not a mere action but an inherent attribute that necessitates describing its subject as a speaker.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you read it as the very speech of the One in whom it inheres, or as just a message from afar? What will change in your recitation today to honor that reality?
?
The passage draws a parallel between speech and motion: just as a moving body is truly described as moving, a speaker is truly described as speaking. When you speak, do your words align with your inner reality, or are they disconnected from who you are? In what relationship will you commit to making your speech a true reflection of your heart before Allah?
?
The distinction between 'say' and 'do' shows that commands about speech are treated like commands about physical actions. Do you take the commands of Allah concerning your tongue as seriously as you take His commands concerning your deeds? What one verbal command of Allah are you neglecting, and how will you act on it starting today?

The Delirious Person Refutes the Mu'tazilite Speaker
Dilemma from a delirious person's speech refutes Mu'tazilite definition of speaker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the case of a delirious person's speech refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that the speaker is merely the creator of speech.

Core Idea
The speaker is the one in whom speech inheres, not the one who merely creates it.

The Mu'tazilites argued that a speaker is defined by creating speech. But what about cases where speech is created in someone who is not its author? The Ash'ari theologian uses the example of a delirious person to drive a dilemma that exposes the weakness of the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
Recall that the Mu'tazilites define the speaker as the one who creates speech (fa'il al-kalam), not the one in whom speech inheres.
This is the view being refuted.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the dilemma structure: two mutually exclusive possibilities.
The author uses the term 'مبرسم' (epileptic/delirious person).
3
Analyze the two horns of the dilemma
Identify the two consequences: either God is the speaker of the delirious person's words, or the delirious person is the speaker. Consider why the first is unacceptable.
Attributing trivial human utterances to God is impossible.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the first horn is impossible, the second must hold: the delirious person is the speaker. This proves that the speaker is the one in whom speech inheres, not the creator.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتمسك به في دفع قولهم المتكلم من فعل الكلام: أن الله تعالى لو خلق في المبرسم وبعض الممرورين أن قال بلسانه: قمت وقعدت لم يخل الحال من أحد أمرين؛ إما أن يقال: يكون المتكلم بهذه الحروف المنظومة والأصوات المقطعة خالقها وفاعلها، فيلزم أن يكون الباري قائلا: قمت وقعدت، وإما أن يقال: المتكلم بهذه الكلمات هو صاحب البرسام دون غيره، فقد بطل قولهم: إن المتكلم ليس من قام به الكلام، بل من فعل الكلام دون غيره.
And among what is used to refute their saying that the speaker is the one who creates speech (fa'il al-kalam): that if God Most High created in an epileptic (al-mubrassam) and some of the possessed that he should say with his tongue: 'I stood up' and 'I sat down', the situation would be free of one of two things: either it is said that the speaker of these arranged letters and segmented sounds is their creator and maker, so it would follow that the Creator is saying 'I stood up' and 'I sat down'; or it is said that the speaker of these words is the person with epilepsy and no one else. So their saying that the speaker is not the one in whom speech inheres, but rather the one who creates speech, is refuted.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents a specific refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine that the speaker (al-mutakallim) is the one who creates speech (fa'il al-kalam), not the one in whom speech inheres. The author uses the case of a delirious or epileptic person (al-mubrassam) in whom God creates speech. He frames a dilemma: either God is the speaker of that speech (since He created it), which would attribute trivial human utterances like 'I stood up' to God, an impossibility; or the epileptic person is the speaker, which supports the Ash'ari view that the speaker is the one in whom speech inheres. Since the second option is the only reasonable one, the Mu'tazilite position is thereby refuted: the speaker is not merely the creator of speech, but the one in whom speech subsists.

Correction: The passage treats the delirious person's utterance as real speech (composed of words and sounds) that must be attributed to a speaker, either God or the person.

Checkpoint

Refutation from Miracles Involving Speech
Miracles with talking animals/objects show God would be speaker if creator equals speaker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles involving speech disprove the Mu'tazilite equation of 'speaker' with 'creator of speech.'

Core Idea
Miracles with talking animals force an absurd consequence on those who equate speaker with speech-creator.

This lesson presents a powerful argument from miracles involving speech. The author lists several well-known miracles where God creates speech in inanimate objects or animals. These examples are then used to turn the Mu'tazilite definition of 'speaker' against them, revealing an impossible implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the list of miracles and the logical argument that follows.
The author uses the Mu'tazilite's own definition to refute them.
2
Identify the miracles mentioned
Find each miracle: the poisoned sheep, pebbles glorifying, conversation with a lizard, bird speech, mountains echoing. These are examples of God creating speech in non-human entities.
All are from authentic reports according to the author.
3
Understand the Mu'tazilite definition
The Mu'tazilites define a 'speaker' as the one who creates speech. Here, God creates speech in these miracles. Therefore, according to their definition, God would be the speaker of those utterances.
This includes utterances like 'I am poisoned' from the sheep, which is absurd for God to say.
4
Grasp the impossibility
If God is the speaker of such statements, it leads to an impossibility (muhal). Therefore, the Mu'tazilite definition must be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نلزمهم عليه عجائب أخر نشرحها ها هنا، فإنه قد وقع الاتفاق على أن المعجزات من فعل الله تعالى غير مكتسبة لجنس الحيوان والبشر، ثم من المعجزات ما هو نطق وقول يخلقه الله في جماد أو حيوان؛ مثل تكليم الشاة المسمومة: لا تأكل مني فإني مسمومة، ومثل تسبيح الحصى في يد الرسول عليه السلام وشهادته برسالته، ومثل مكالمته الضب، ومثل منطق الطير وتأويب الجبال: يَا جِبَالُ أَوِّبِي مَعَهُ وَالطَّيْرَ [سبأ: ١٠]، إلى غير ذلك مما قد استفاضت الأخبار الصحيحة بها، فكل ذلك فعل الله تعالى، فالمتكلم عندهم من فعل الكلام، فيجب أن يكون الباري تعالى متكلما بها إذ كان فاعلا لها، وهو محال.
Then we impose on them other wonders which we explain here. For it has been agreed upon that miracles are from God's act, not acquired by the genus of animals and humans. Then among miracles are speech and saying that God creates in an inanimate object or animal, such as the speaking of the poisoned sheep: 'Do not eat from me, for I am poisoned', and such as the glorification of the pebbles in the hand of the Messenger (peace be upon him) and their testimony to his prophethood, and such as his conversation with the dabb (lizard), and such as the speech of birds and the echoing of the mountains: 'O mountains, echo with him and the birds' [Saba: 10], and other such things that authentic reports have related. All of that is God's act. So the speaker according to them is the one who creates speech, it must be that the Creator is a speaker with them (i.e., such speech) since He is their agent, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage uses miracles involving speech to refute the Mu'tazilite view. The author notes that miracles are God's acts, not acquired by humans or animals. Examples include the poisoned sheep that spoke, pebbles that glorified and testified to prophethood, the Prophet's conversation with a lizard, birds speaking, and mountains echoing with David. These are authentic reports. According to the Mu'tazilite definition, the speaker is the creator of speech. Therefore, God would be the speaker of these utterances (e.g., the sheep saying 'I am poisoned'), which is absurd and impossible. Thus the Mu'tazilite claim is invalid.

Correction: The author asserts these are literal speech created by God in objects/animals, as proven by authentic reports.

Key Takeaway
The miraculous speech in animals and objects refutes the Mu'tazilite definition of speaker, because it would force God to be the speaker of absurd statements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazilites thought they were defending God's transcendence, yet their logic led them to deny His attribute of speech. Where in your own faith might you be over-relying on logic to 'protect' God, thereby limiting His perfection? Ask God for the humility to submit to His descriptions of Himself even when they challenge your intellect.
?
When you read that a sheep spoke to the Prophet, does your heart immediately accept it or struggle? Your reaction is a mirror of your trust in God's absolute sovereignty. Pray for the certainty that God can do anything, and that His messengers were given clear signs that demand full submission.

The Implication of Najjariyya’s Agreement with Ash‘ari
Najjariyya agreeing with Ash'ari on creation of acts leads to God speaking with servants' speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Najjariyya’s agreement with the Ash‘ari on the creation of human acts forces them to an unacceptable conclusion about God’s speech.

Core Idea
If God creates every human act, then He creates our speech—so He is the speaker of it.

This short but powerful passage exposes a logical trap for the Najjariyya. By accepting the Ash‘ari view that God creates all human acts, they must also accept the Mu‘tazili definition that the speaker is the one who creates speech. This leads to the absurdity that God speaks with every human utterance, which undermines their own position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's point come through clearly.
Notice the structure: premise, then implication.
2
Identify the two premises
The first premise: Najjariyya agrees with Ash'ari that God creates human acts. The second premise (implicit): the definition of 'speaker' is the one who creates speech. These lead to the conclusion.
The second premise is shared by Mu'tazilites and Najjariyya.
3
Understand the implication
If God creates all acts, including human speech, then according to that definition, God is the speaker of that speech. So He is 'qā'il' with every human utterance.
This makes God the speaker of both true and false speech, which is problematic.
4
Reflect on the absurdity
Consider why this conclusion is unacceptable: it equates God's speech with created human speech and leads to contradictions with other divine attributes.
This shows the Najjariyya position is inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن النجارية وافقت الأشعرية على أن الباري خالق أعمال العباد فيلزمهم أن يقولوا: هو قائل بقولهم، متكلم بكلامهم؛ إذ كان فاعلا لها.
Then the Najjariyya agreed with the Ash'ariyya that the Creator is the creator of the acts of servants. So it would be necessary for them to say: He is the one who speaks with their speech, the speaker with their speech, since He is the agent of it.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This short passage points out a consequence for the Najjariyya (a sect that held the Qur'an was created) who, like the Ash'ariyya, affirm that God creates the acts of human beings. If they also adopt the Mu'tazilite definition that the speaker is the one who creates speech, then they would be forced to say that God speaks with the speech of His servants (i.e., He is the speaker of every utterance humans make). This implication is problematic and further undermines the Mu'tazilite position.

Correction: The agreement on the creation of acts, combined with their definition of speech, forces them to say God speaks with every human utterance, which is an unacceptable conclusion.

Refutation from Power over Opposites
Ash'ari argument from power over opposites to refute Mu'tazilite definition of speaker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari argument from power over opposites refutes the Mu'tazilite definition of God as speaker by showing it leads to attributing evil and falsehood to Allah.

Core Idea
If God creates speech, He must also create its opposites, making Him author of evil and falsehood—an impossibility.

This passage presents a powerful reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazilite view that God's attribute of speech is created. The argument begins with a basic principle: true power is power over opposites. Since speech has opposites (silence, command, report, etc.), the same logic that defines the speaker must apply to the creator of those opposites. Following this leads to absurd conclusions that no Muslim can accept.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the principle of power over opposites
The author states that true power (qudra) enables its possessor to bring about both a thing and its opposite. This is a key kalam axiom.
2
Identify the opposites of speech
Speech has opposites: silence, and within speech, commands have good/evil opposites, reports have truth/falsehood opposites. List them.
3
Apply the definition consistently
If the speaker is the one who creates speech, then the creator of silence would be 'silent', the creator of a command would be 'commander', etc. This leads to attributing all actions to God.
4
Recognize the absurd conclusion
Among commands are evil and among reports false, so God would be the creator of evil and false reports, which is impossible. Therefore, the original definition must be rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما نلزمهم أن القادر على الحقيقة من يكون قادرا على الضدين، والكلام معنى له أضداد، فإذا قالوا المتكلم من فعل الكلام يلزمهم أن يقولوا: الساكت من فعل السكوت، حتى لو خلق سكوتا في محل كان ساكنا، ولو خلق أمرا في محل كان آمرا، ولو خلق خبرا في محل كان مخبرا، ثم من الأوامر ما يكون خيرا، ومنه ما يكون شرا، ومن الأخبار ما يكون صدقا ومنه ما يكون كذبا، فيلزمهم إضافة الكل إلى الله تعالى وهو محال.
And among what we impose on them: that the one who is truly powerful is the one who has power over opposites, and speech is a meaning that has opposites. So if they say that the speaker is the creator of speech, it would be necessary for them to say that the silent is the creator of silence, so that if He created silence in a subject, He would be silent; and if He created a command in a subject, He would be a commander; and if He created a report in a subject, He would be a reporter. Then among commands are good and among them are evil, and among reports are truthful and among them are false. So it would be necessary for them to attribute all to God Most High, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage argues from the nature of true power (qudra) and opposites. True power is power over opposites (e.g., to move or not move). Speech has opposites: silence, command vs. prohibition, truth vs. falsehood. If the speaker is defined as the creator of speech, then the same logic would apply to the opposites: the creator of silence is silent, the creator of a command is a commander, etc. Since God creates all actions including commands (some good, some evil) and reports (some true, some false), it would follow that God is the creator of evil commands and false reports, attributing to Him what is impossible and blasphemous. Therefore, the Mu'tazilite definition leads to an absurdity and must be rejected.

Key Terms
قادر
ضدان
ساكت
آمر
مخبر
أمر
خبر
صدق
كذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creating speech does not require creating its opposite.
Correction: If being a speaker is defined as creating speech, then consistency demands that the creator of silence is silent, and similarly for other opposites. The definition cannot be selectively applied.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the relationship between power and opposites?
A) Power does not require opposites
B) True power is power over opposites
C) Only God has power over opposites
D) Speech has no opposites

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite definition of speaker is refuted because it forces the attribution of evil and falsehood to Allah, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is the Creator of all speech, including falsehood and evil commands, how would that affect your understanding of His justice? This passage forces you to choose between two definitions of 'speaker'. Are you willing to accept the Mu'tazilite definition that leads to attributing evil to Allah? Or do you reject it, and if so, what does that imply about your own commitment to tawhid?
?
The argument shows that definitions have consequences. What areas of your own life have you defined in a way that leads to inconsistent or harmful beliefs about Allah? How can you ensure your definitions of Allah's attributes are consistent with His perfection?
?
Consider the principle that true power is power over opposites. How does this challenge your own perception of power? Do you see Allah's power as including control over both good and evil in a way that does not attribute evil to Him? How does this lesson deepen your awe and reverence for Allah?

5 min
The Impossibility of God Being the Speaker of Created Speech
Author presents an objection against defining speaker as creator of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining 'speaker' as 'creator of speech' leads to the impossible conclusion that God would be the speaker of created, contingent speech.

Core Idea
If God creates speech, He cannot be its speaker without compromising His eternal attribute.

This lesson presents a powerful objection against those who define the speaker as the creator of speech. By examining miracles that involve speech in inanimate objects, the author shows that such a definition would force us to attribute finite, created speech to God — an impossibility. The argument then extends to human actions, creating further theological difficulties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once without analyzing. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the premise
Note the agreed-upon premise that miracles are from God alone and are not acquired by creatures. Then identify the examples of miraculous speech.
Examples include the poisoned sheep, pebbles, lizard, birds.
3
Understand the definition of 'speaker'
The author attributes to the opponents the definition that 'the speaker is the one who creates speech'. Recognize how this definition is applied.
This is the key move in the objection.
4
Follow the logical consequence
Given God creates those miraculous speeches, He would be their speaker — but that is impossible. Also see how this extends to human speech if God creates actions.
The extension to human speech is a further reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا نلزمهم عليه عجائب أخر نشرحها ها هنا، فإنه قد وقع الاتفاق عليه على أن المعجزات من فعل الله تعالى غير مكتسبة لجنس الحيوان والبشر ثم من المعجزات ما هو نطق وقول يخلقه الله في جماد أو حيوان؛ مثل تكليم الشاة المسمومة: لا تأكل مني فإني مسمومة، ومثل تسبيح الحصى في يد الرسول عليه السلام وشهادته برسالته ومثل مكالمته الضب، ومثل منطق الطير وتأويب الجبال: يَا جِبَالُ أَوِّبِي مَعَهُ وَالطَّيْرَ، إلى غير ذلك مما قد استفاضت الأخبار الصحيحة بها، فكل ذلك فعل الله تعالى، فالمتكلم عندهم من فعل الكلام، فيجب أن يكون الباري تعالى متكلما بها إذ كان فاعلاً لها، وهو محال، ثم إن النجارية وافقت الأشعرية على أن الباري خالق أعمال العباد فيلزمهم أن يقولوا: هو قائل بقولهم، متكلم بكلامهم؛ إذ كان فاعلاً لها.
Then we impose upon them other wonders that we explain here. For it has been agreed that miracles are from the act of God the Exalted, not acquired by the genus of animals and humans. Then among miracles is speech and utterance that God creates in an inanimate object or animal, such as the speaking of the poisoned sheep: 'Do not eat from me, for I am poisoned,' and like the glorification of the pebbles in the hand of the Messenger (peace be upon him) and their testimony to his prophethood, and like his speaking to the lizard, and like the speech of birds and the echoing of the mountains: 'O mountains, echo with him and the birds' [Saba 10], and other things of which authentic reports have been widely transmitted. All of that is the act of God the Exalted. So the speaker (al-mutakallim) according to them is the one who creates speech, so it would be necessary that the Creator the Exalted be a speaker by them (i.e., those speeches) since He is their doer, and that is impossible. Then the Najjariyya agreed with the Ash'ariyya that the Creator is the creator of the actions of servants, so it would be necessary for them to say: He is a speaker by their speech, a mutakallim by their speech, since He is their doer.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an objection (ilzam) against the Ash'ari and Najjari position that the mutakallim (speaker) is defined as the one who creates speech (man fa‘ala al-kalam). The author begins by establishing a premise: miracles are from God alone and are not acquired by humans or animals. Among miracles are instances of speech in inanimate objects or animals, like the speaking of the poisoned sheep to the Prophet, the glorification of pebbles, etc. These are all God’s acts. Since the opponent defines the speaker as the one who creates speech, God would then be the speaker of these created utterances, because He is their creator. But this is impossible (muhal), because it would make God the speaker of finite, created, contingent speech, conflicting with the divine attribute of speech as eternal. The author then extends the argument: the Najjariyya and Ash'ariyya both hold that God creates the actions of servants. If so, they must say that God is the speaker of every human speech, since He creates it. This leads to further absurdity, such as implying that God speaks falsehood or commands evil (an implication the author will develop later).

Key Terms
المتكلم
المعجزات
النجارية
الأشعرية
فعل الله
خلق الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Defining 'speaker' as 'creator of speech' is a harmless semantic definition.
Correction: This definition leads to the absurd conclusion that God speaks through all created utterances, including falsehoods, which contradicts His perfection.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider it impossible that God be the speaker of the miraculous speeches mentioned?
A) Because those speeches are not real speech.
B) Because it would imply that God speaks in a finite, created manner, which is incompatible with His eternal attribute.
C) Because the Prophet did not attribute them to God.
D) Because the opponents do not accept that miracles are from God.

Key Takeaway
Defining 'speaker' as 'creator of speech' forces the conclusion that God speaks through all created utterances, which contradicts His eternal attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear a verse of the Quran, do you truly believe it is the eternal speech of God, not a created thing? How does that belief shape your reverence for every word?
?
Have you ever casually attributed a miracle or a good event to a human cause, forgetting that it is God who creates the action? What would it take for you to habitually see God's hand in all speech and deeds?
?
If God were the creator of your every word, as the opponents' view implies, would you be more or less careful about what you say? How does the argument in this lesson challenge you to take responsibility for your own speech?

The Necessity of Meaning in Divine Speech
Proof that divine speech necessitates a distinct meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine speech must have a meaning distinct from knowledge, will, and power.

Core Idea
Divine speech requires a meaning beyond mere letters; only a distinct attribute suffices.

This passage tackles a fundamental question: does God's speech have actual meaning, or is it just empty sounds? The author argues from the nature of speech itself that it must convey meaning. He then eliminates three divine attributes as candidates for that meaning, proving a distinct attribute is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Speech requires mental meanings attaching to verbal expressions; without this correspondence, it is not speech at all.
This principle is self-evident in human language.
2
Apply to God's speech
If God creates letters and words without meaning, that is impossible. So His speech must have meaning. What is that meaning?
3
Eliminate knowledge as meaning
Knowledge lacks command/request (اقتضاء وطلب), yet divine commands and prohibitions require that. So knowledge cannot be the meaning.
Knowledge only informs about things as they are.
4
Eliminate will and power
Will, though involving command, does not include news, inquiry, promise, threat. Power is far from requesting action from another. Both fail.
5
Conclude: there must be a distinct meaning
Since the only possible candidates are eliminated, divine speech must have a distinct meaning. Otherwise it would be futile.
This meaning is what the author will later identify as the attribute of speech (كلام نفسي).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعبر فنقول : لولا مطابقة الألفاظ اللسانية معانيها النفسانية لم يكن كلاما أصلا، بل لولا سبق تلك المعاني في النفس على العبارات في اللسان لم يمكن أن [؟] عنها، ولا أن يدل عليها ويوصل إليها، وهذا في حقنا ظاهر، فما قولكم في حق الباري سبحانه : أفيخلق حروفا وكلمات في محل ولا دلالة لها على معان وحقائق أم لها دلالة؟ فإن كان الأول فهو من أمحل المحال، وإن كان الثاني حقاً فما مدلول تلك العبارات؟ وأين ذلك المدلول؟ ولا جائز أن يقال مدلولها علم الباري تعالى وعالميته، فإن العلم لا اقتضاء فيه والمدلول ها هنا يجب أن يكون اقتضاء وطلبا، والعلم يتعلق بالمعلوم على ما هو به، وخلاف المعلوم لا يقتضيه العلم، وقد يكون من القول ما يقتضي خلاف المعلوم، والعلم من حيث هو علم يتعلق بالواجب والجائز والمستحيل او الاقتضاء والطلب لا يتعلق بالواجب والمستحيل. وكذلك الإرادة لا تصلح أن تكون مدلولة لها، فإن الإرادة وإن تخيل فيها اقتضاء وطلب فلا يتخيل فيها خبر واستخبار ووعد ووعيد، وقد تحقق في العبارات هذا المعنى، وكذلك القدرة لا تصلح أن تكون مدلولة لها فإنها بخاصيتها بعيدة عن الاقتضاء والطلب؛ أعني طلب الفعل من الغير فلا بد إذا من مدلول قطعا ويقينا وإلا خلت العبارات عن المعاني وطاحت في إدراج الأماني كسير السواني.
He expresses saying: If verbal expressions did not correspond to their mental meanings, they would not be speech at all. Rather, if those meanings did not precede the expressions in the tongue, it would not be possible to [express] them, nor to indicate them and convey them. This is evident in our case. What do you say concerning the Exalted Creator? Does He create letters and words in a locus without them indicating any meanings and realities, or do they have indication? If the first, then it is among the most absurd of impossibilities. If the second is true, then what is the meaning indicated by those expressions? And where is that meaning? It is not permissible to say that their meaning is the knowledge of the Exalted God and His omniscience, for knowledge has no command and request. The meaning here must involve command and request. Knowledge pertains to the known as it is, and what contradicts the known is not required by knowledge. Indeed, speech may require what contradicts the known. Knowledge, insofar as it is knowledge, pertains to the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, while command and request do not pertain to the necessary and the impossible. Likewise, will is not suitable to be their meaning, for although will implies command and request, it does not imply news, inquiry, promise, and threat. Yet these meanings are actualized in expressions. Likewise, power is not suitable to be their meaning, for by its specificity it is far from command and request—I mean requesting action from another. Therefore, there must necessarily and certainly be a meaning; otherwise, expressions would be empty of meanings and would fall into the realm of wishes like the grinding of millstones.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine speech must have a meaning, and it rejects three candidates for that meaning. The author begins with a general principle: verbal expressions (الألفاظ اللسانية) must correspond to internal meanings (المعاني النفسانية); otherwise they are not speech. This is self-evident in human language. He then applies this to God's speech, asking a crucial question: Does God create letters and words without any meaning, or with meaning? The first is impossible (أمحل المحال). If with meaning, then what is that meaning? The author systematically eliminates three divine attributes as candidates: knowledge (علم), will (إرادة), and power (قدرة). Each fails because the meaning of divine commands and prohibitions involves command and request (اقتضاء وطلب), which these attributes lack. Knowledge only informs about things as they are; it does not command what is contrary to the known. Will, though it involves command, does not include news, inquiry, promise, and threat which appear in speech. Power is far from requesting action from another. Therefore, there must be a distinct meaning (مدلول) that is not identical to these attributes. The conclusion is emphatic: without such a meaning, speech would be empty and futile.

Key Terms
كلام
معنى
دلالة
اقتضاء
طلب
علم
إرادة
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech could be meaningless vocal sounds or letters without any content.
Correction: The author argues that this is 'among the most absurd of impossibilities' because speech by definition conveys meaning; otherwise it is not speech.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why could knowledge not be the meaning of God's speech?
A) Knowledge does not involve command and request.
B) Knowledge is eternal.
C) Knowledge is not an attribute of God.
D) Knowledge cannot be created.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech must have a meaning that is distinct from knowledge, will, and power, otherwise it would be empty and futile.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you treat it as a collection of commands and prohibitions directed personally to you, or do you reduce it to mere information? Which verse have you been resisting because you treated it as just knowledge, not as a command?
?
If God's speech is a distinct attribute demanding a response, what does that mean for your daily recitation? Are you listening for His command, or just repeating sounds?
?
The author says without meaning, speech is like grinding millstones—futile. When you recite the Quran without reflecting on its meaning, do you risk falling into that futility? What will you change to ensure your recitation is meaningful?

The Two Dilemmas of Divine Speech
The author defines the conditions for the meaning of divine speech and poses two key dilemmas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key questions about the meaning of divine speech: whether it is one or many, and whether it is eternal or created, and why these questions are central to debates on divine attributes.

Core Idea
The meaning of divine speech is an eternal attribute, not the created words we hear.

In this passage, the author presents a foundational problem for the doctrine of divine speech. He insists that the meaning must be an attribute of God, not a physical sound. Then he poses two pairs of questions: Is it one or many? Is it eternal or created? These questions define the battlefield between the Ash'aris and their opponents. Let's dive into the author's precise wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage slowly
Read the Arabic passage once without rushing. Note the key terms: مدلول (meaning), اختصاص (specificity), and صفة (attribute).
Pay attention to the parallel structure of the two questions.
2
Identify the two pairs of questions
The author asks two pairs: one vs many (واحد/كثير) and eternal vs created (قديم/محدث). See how each pair relates to the described (الموصوف) or the expressions (العبارات).
These are the core dilemmas about divine speech.
3
Reflect on the author's description
The author calls this the 'clashing of two seas' (ملتطم الأمواج عند التقاء البحرين). Think about why this metaphor is used—two opposing views colliding.
The phrase highlights the intensity of the theological dispute.
4
Consider the implications
If the meaning is many, does that compromise God's unity? If created, does that imply change in God? These questions challenge fundamental doctrines.
Ash'aris resolve them by positing one eternal meaning (الكلام النفسي).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك المدلول يجب أن يختص بالقائل اختصاص وصف وصفة حتى تستقيم الدلالة وتتضح الإمارة، ثم ذلك المدلول أهو واحد وحدة الموصوف، أو كثير كثرة العبارات، أو هو قديم قدم الموصوف، أو محدث حدوث العبارات؟ فذلك محل الاشتباه في الموضعين ومجال النظر في الطرفين وملتطم الأمواج عند التقاء البحرين.
And that meaning must be specific to the speaker as an attribute and description, so that the indication is sound and the sign is clear. Then, is that meaning one with the oneness of the described, or many with the multiplicity of expressions? Or is it eternal with the eternity of the described, or temporally originated with the temporally originated expressions? For that is the place of confusion in both positions, and the field of examination on both sides, and the clashing of waves at the meeting of the two seas.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage classifies the possible metaphysical status of the meaning (المدلول) of divine speech. The author first sets a condition: the meaning must be an attribute (صفة) specific to the speaker, ensuring that the indication is sound. He then poses two key questions that frame the theological debate. First, is the meaning one (واحد) corresponding to the oneness of God, or many (كثير) corresponding to the multiplicity of verbal expressions? Second, is the meaning eternal (قديم) like God's essence, or temporally originated (محدث) like the created words? The author describes these questions as the point of confusion (محل الاشتباه) and the field of examination (مجال النظر), comparing it to the clashing of two seas. This reflects the classic Ash'ari position that the internal meaning (الكلام النفسي) is one eternal attribute, while the external expressions are many and created. The Mu'tazilites held that all speech is created. The author presents the issue as a recognized problem for further investigation.

اختصاص (specificity)
صفة (attribute)
واحد (one)
كثير (many)
قديم (eternal)
محدث (temporally originated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The meaning of divine speech is identical to the vocal sounds or letters.
Correction: The author clarifies that the meaning must be an attribute distinct from the expressions, and raises questions about its unity and temporality, implying it is not the same as physical words.

Checkpoint
What are the two pairs of questions the author raises about the meaning of divine speech?
A) Whether it is one or many, and whether it is eternal or created.
B) Whether it is knowledge or will.
C) Whether it is seen or heard.
D) Whether it is internal or external.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of divine speech is an eternal attribute of God, distinct from the created expressions; its oneness and eternity are central to the Ash'ari position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What does it mean for your relationship with the Quran if it is not just created words but part of God's essence?
?
You are confronted with a choice: is the meaning of divine speech one or many? Does this affect how you view the unity of the Quran message despite its many verses? How does this lesson challenge you to think about the Quran's consistency and divine origin?

The Oneness of Divine Speech: Ash'ari Doctrine and Sectarian Views
Al-Shahrastani presents the Ash'ari principle and contrasts it with Karramiyya and Mu'tazila views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that divine speech is a single eternal meaning, and how it differs from the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila positions.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one eternal meaning, not many created sounds.

This lesson presents the thirteenth principle from al-Shahrastani's work, focusing on the nature of divine speech. Al-Shahrastani first outlines the Ash'ari view that God's speech is one single meaning encompassing all forms of address, then contrasts it with the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila positions. The passage then begins the Ash'ari proof against multiplicity, arguing that if speech were many, it would be either infinite or finite—both problematic. This sets the stage for a fuller argument continued in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the shift from doxography to proof.
2
Identify the three sectarian positions
The passage presents the views of Ash'aris, Karramiyya, and Mu'tazila on divine speech. Note the key differences: Ash'aris say it's one meaning; Karramiyya say power to speak is one but utterances are many; Mu'tazila say it's created letters and sounds.
Also note the sub-disagreement between Abu Ali and Abu Hashim.
3
Understand the Ash'ari proof structure
The Ash'ari proof against multiplicity is presented as a dilemma: if speech were many, it would be either infinite or finite. Both options are argued to be impossible for the eternal attribute.
The argument is incomplete; it cuts off at 'and the eternal does not...'
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
Notice that the proof is not finished. Consider what the next step might be: if finite numbers require a specifier, what does that imply?
This prepares you for the continuation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثالثة عشر في أن كلام الباري واحد. ذهبت الأشعرية إلى أن كلام الباري تعالى واحد وهو مع وحدته أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار ووعد ووعيد، وذهبت الكرامية إلى أن الكلام بمعنى القدرة على القول معنى واحد وبمعنى القول معان كثيرة قائمة بذات الباري تعالى، وهي أقوال مسموعة وكلمات محفوظة تحدث في ذاته عند قوله وتكلمه ولا يجوز عليها الفناء ولا العدم عندهم، وذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الكلام حروف منظومة وأصوات مقطعة شاهداً وغائبًا لا حقيقة للكلام سوى ذلك، وهي مخلوقة قائمة بمحل حادث إذا أوجدها الباري تعالى سمعت من المحل، وكما وجدت فنيت، وشرط أبو علي الجبائي البنية المخصوصة التي يتأتى منها مخارج الحروف شاهدا وغائبا، ولم يشترط ذلك ابنه أبو هاشم في الغائب. قالت الأشعرية : إذا قام الدليل على أن الكلام معنى قائم بذات الباري تعالى، وكل معنى أو صفة له فهي واحدة، وكل ما دل على أن علمه وقدرته وإرادته واحدة فذلك يدل على أن كلامه واحد وذلك أنه لو كان كثيرًا لم يخل إما أن يكون أعدادًا لا تتناهى وإما أن يكون أعدادًا متناهية، فإن كان أعدادًا لا تتناهى فهو محال ؛ لأن ما حصره الوجود من العدد فهو متناه وإن اقتصر على عدد دون عدد فاختصاصه بالبعض دون البعض يستدعي مخصصا، والقديم لا
The thirteenth principle: That the speech of the Exalted is one. The Ash'aris held that the speech of the Exalted is one, and despite its oneness it is command, prohibition, inform, interrogation, promise, and threat. The Karramiyya held that speech in the sense of the power to speak is one meaning, and in the sense of the uttered speech is many meanings subsisting in the essence of the Exalted, which are audible utterances and preserved words that occur in His essence at the time of His saying and speaking, and they consider annihilation and non-existence impermissible for them. The Mu'tazila held that speech is structured letters and discrete sounds, witnessed and absent, and there is no reality of speech other than that; they are created subsisting in a temporal locus; when the Exalted brings them into existence, they are heard from the locus, and just as they come into existence, they perish. Abu Ali al-Jubba'i stipulated a specific structure from which the points of articulation of letters emerge, witnessed and absent, while his son Abu Hashim did not stipulate that in the absent. The Ash'aris said: When the proof establishes that speech is a meaning subsisting in the essence of the Exalted, and every meaning or attribute of His is one, then whatever indicates that His knowledge, power, and will are one, that indicates that His speech is one. That is because if it were many, it would not be free from being either infinite numbers or finite numbers. If it is infinite numbers, it is impossible, because what the existence of number encompasses is finite. And if it is limited to a certain number without another, then its specification to some without others necessitates a specifier, and the eternal does not...
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth principle concerning the oneness of divine speech. It begins by stating the Ash'ari position: divine speech is one eternal meaning that encompasses all forms of speech (command, prohibition, etc.) while remaining single. Then it summarizes two opposing views: the Karramiyya say that the power to speak is one meaning, but the actual utterances are many accident-like events subsisting in God, never perishing; the Mu'tazila hold that speech is merely created letters and sounds, brought into existence in a temporal locus and then perishing. A sub-point notes a disagreement between Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim about whether a specific physical structure is necessary for speech in the unseen. Finally, the Ash'ari proof begins: if divine speech were multiple, its multiplicity would be either infinite or finite. Infinity is impossible for what is encompassed by existence; finitude would require a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ), which is inappropriate for the eternal. The argument is cut off at 'and the eternal does not…' indicating it continues on the next page. The passage serves as a doxography and the start of a systematic proof for the Ash'ari doctrine.

Key Terms
كلام الباري
حروف منظومة
أصوات مقطعة
معنى قائم
القدرة على القول
البنية المخصوصة
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari position denies that divine speech includes commands, prohibitions, etc.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm these as aspects of a single eternal meaning, not multiple separate entities.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari argument, why can divine speech not be many finite numbers?
A) Because finite numbers would require a specifier, which is inappropriate for the eternal.
B) Because finite numbers would imply creation in time.
C) Because finite numbers contradict divine simplicity.
D) Because finite numbers would make speech divisible.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'aris argue that divine speech must be one eternal meaning because multiplicity leads to logical impossibility, whether infinite or finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If divine speech is one eternal meaning that encompasses all commands and prohibitions, do you approach the Quran with the reverence that it is a direct, timeless address from God to you? How can you ensure you do not treat it as mere words from the past?
?
The Ash'aris argue that multiplicity in God's attributes would lead to logical absurdities. In your own life, do you try to 'multiply' God's attributes by picking and choosing which aspects to emphasize based on your convenience? What would it mean to accept His speech as whole and undivided?
?
The proof against infinite multiplicity says that what exists is finite. If God's speech is infinite in meaning, how does that challenge your limited understanding? Are you content with the mystery, or do you try to reduce His speech to fit your own categories?

Ash'ari Proof That God's Speech Is One
Ash'ari proof from disjunction of infinite/finite to oneness of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof that God's speech is one, including the disjunctive argument about infinite and finite multiplicity and the principle of specification.

Core Idea
God's speech is one because infinite speeches are impossible and finite ones need a specifier.

The Ash'aris argue that divine speech, like all divine attributes, is one. They use a logical disjunction: if speech were many, it would be either infinite or finite. Both possibilities are rationally impossible. This proof establishes the oneness of God's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: premise, disjunction, two branches, conclusion.
2
Understand the disjunction
The key move is the either-or: if speech were many, it must be either infinite or finite. Identify why each option is rejected. The commentary will explain.
Infinite is impossible because existence encompasses finite things; finite requires a cause of specification.
3
Reflect on the specification argument
Ask yourself why the eternal cannot have a specifier. This ties into the nature of eternal attributes and their relation to all possible objects.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari principle that eternal attributes are not subject to external causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية : إذا قام الدليل على أن الكلام معنى قائم بذات الباري تعالى، وكل معنى أو صفة له فهي واحدة، وكل ما دل على أن علمه وقدرته وإرادته واحدة فذلك يدل على أن كلامه واحد وذلك أنه لو كان كثيرًا لم يخل إما أن يكون أعدادًا لا تتناهى وإما أن يكون أعدادًا متناهية، فإن كان أعدادًا لا تتناهى فهو محال؛ لأن ما حصره الوجود من العدد فهو متناه وإن اقتصر على عدد دون عدد فاختصاصه بالبعض دون البعض يستدعي مخصصا، والقديم لا اختصاص له والصفة الأزلية إذا كانت متعلقة وجب عموم تعلقها بجميع المتعلقات؛ لأن نسبتها إلى الكل وإلى كل واحد نسبة واحدة فلئن تعلقت بواحدة تعلقت بالكل، وإن تخلفت عن واحدة تخلفت عن الكل.
The Ash'aris said: When the proof establishes that speech is a meaning subsisting in the essence of the Exalted Creator, and every meaning or attribute of His is one, and everything that indicates that His knowledge, power, and will are one indicates that His speech is one. That is because if it were many, it could not escape being either infinite in number or finite in number. If it were infinite in number, that is impossible because whatever existence encompasses in number is finite. And if it were limited to a certain number and not another, then its specification with some to the exclusion of others would require a specifier, and the eternal has no specification. And if the eternal attribute is related (to something), its relation must be universal to all related things, because its relation to the whole and to each individual is one relation. So if it relates to one, it relates to all, and if it fails to relate to one, it fails to relate to all.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari proof that God's speech is one. It begins by stating the premise that speech is an attribute subsisting in God's essence. Then it invokes a principle: all of God's attributes are one (i.e., they are not multiple distinct entities but aspects of a single divine essence). Since knowledge, power, and will are one, speech must also be one. The proof then considers the alternative: if speech were multiple (many distinct speeches), then the number of speeches would be either infinite or finite. An infinite number is impossible because anything that exists is finite. If finite, then there would be a specific number, but then why that number? It would require a cause of specification (mukhassis), but God is eternal and has no cause. Therefore, speech must be one. Furthermore, an eternal attribute, if it relates to anything, must relate equally to all possible objects of that relation; otherwise it would be inconsistent. Since speech relates to commands, prohibitions, etc., if it were many, each would relate to its specific object, but then they would not be universal. This reinforces the oneness of the attribute.

أعداد لا تتناهى
أعداد متناهية
اختصاص
مخصص
صفة أزلية
تعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari proof merely assumes oneness without argument.
Correction: The proof actually uses a logical disjunction about infinity and specification to demonstrate that multiple speeches are impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari argument, why can God's speech not be finite in number?
A) Because it is impossible for God to have many attributes
B) Because a finite number would require a specifier, which is impossible for the eternal
C) Because speech is essentially infinite
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny it

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof that God's speech is one is based on the impossibility of infinite multiplicity and the need for a specifier for finite multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech is one unified attribute, how does that challenge your habit of treating some Quranic commands as more important than others? Will you now strive to obey each command with equal commitment?
?
The proof shows that an eternal attribute must relate universally to all its objects. Does your faith in God's speech reflect that universality, or do you selectively accept parts of the Quran while ignoring others? What one area of obedience have you been neglecting?
?
The argument rejects multiplicity in God's attributes because it leads to absurdity. Have you ever doubted God's consistency or thought He might have conflicting intentions? How does this passage strengthen your conviction in His absolute unity and coherence?

The Homonymy of Speech: Ash'ari Response to Opponents
Ash'ari explanation of terminological equivocation in the debate on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari and their opponents talk past each other on the nature of divine speech due to the homonymy of the term 'kalam'.

Core Idea
Agreement on definition does not guarantee agreement on referent.

This passage reveals a subtle but crucial point in theological debate: even when two parties agree on the meaning of a term in one domain, they may still disagree in another because the term is used homonymously. The Ash'ari author explains that his opponents, by taking 'speech' to refer only to created utterances, cannot validly argue against the Ash'ari position of an eternal attribute of speech. Let's unpack this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical agreement
Notice the conditional opening: 'if our opponents agreed with us...' What would they have to agree on for the dispute to dissolve?
2
Identify the reason for continued disagreement
The author then gives the reason: the term 'speech' is homonymous (mushtarak). Each side uses it to refer to different things.
Homonyms have multiple distinct meanings.
3
Recognize the asymmetry
The Ash'aris accept both senses (created utterances and eternal attribute), while the opponents only accept the created sense. So when the opponent argues against oneness, he is arguing about the created utterances, not the attribute.
4
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, the opponent's discourse is not really about the same subject. The author notes they only speak by way of ilzam and irad al-ishkal (necessity and raising problems).
This implies their argument is not a genuine engagement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخصومنا لو وافقونا على أن الكلام في الشاهد معنى في النفس سوى العبارات القائمة باللسان، وأن الكلام في الغائب معنى قائم بذات الباري تعالى سوى العبارات التي نقرؤها باللسان ونكتبها في المصاحف، لوافقونا على اتحاد المعنى، لكن لما كان لفظاً مشتركاً في الإطلاق لم يتوارد على محل واحد فإن ما يثبته كلاماً فالأشعرية تثبته وتوافقه على أنه كثير وأنه محدث مخلوق، وما يثبته الأشعري كلاماً فالخصم ينكره أصلاً، فكيف يصح منه كلامه في وحدته، وكونه أزلياً قديماً، ولكن ليس يتكلم الخصم في هذه المسألة إلا على سبيل الإلزام وإيراد الإشكال.
And if our opponents agreed with us that speech in the witnessed is a meaning in the soul aside from the expressions that subsist on the tongue, and that speech in the absent is a meaning subsisting in the essence of the Exalted Creator aside from the expressions that we read on the tongue and write in the copies (of the Quran), they would agree with us on the oneness of the meaning. But because it is a homonymous word in usage, it does not refer to a single locus, for what the opponent confirms as speech, the Ash'aris confirm it and agree with him that it is many and that it is originated and created, but what the Ash'ari confirms as speech, the opponent denies it entirely. So how can his discourse about its oneness and its being eternal and pre-eternal be valid? But the opponent only speaks about this issue by way of necessity and raising a problem.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is an Ash'ari explanation of why their opponents (likely the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya) disagree with them on the oneness of God's speech despite some common ground. The author states that if the opponents agreed that speech in the witnessed (human speech) is a meaning in the soul distinct from uttered expressions, and that speech in the absent (divine speech) is a meaning subsisting in God's essence distinct from the recited and written words, then they would agree on the oneness of that meaning. However, the term 'speech' (kalam) is homonymous: it is used for both the created, multiple utterances and the single eternal attribute. The opponents take 'speech' to refer to the created, multiple expressions, and the Ash'aris accept that those are indeed many and created. But the Ash'aris also affirm another sense: the eternal attribute of speech. The opponents deny that entirely. Thus, when the opponents argue against the oneness of divine speech, they are really arguing about the created expressions, not the eternal meaning. The author notes that the opponents only engage in this debate by way of necessity (ilzam) and raising problems (irad al-ishkal), implying they do not accept the premise of an eternal attribute but still try to refute it.

معنى في النفس
لفظ مشترك
إطلاق
محدث مخلوق
إلزام
إشكال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ash'aris deny that the recited Quran is created.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm that the recited expressions are created and multiple, but they also affirm an eternal meaning in God's essence.

Checkpoint
Why does the Ash'ari author say the opponents' discourse about the oneness of divine speech is not valid?
A) Because the opponents deny the existence of any divine speech
B) Because the opponents use the term 'speech' equivocally, referring only to created expressions
C) Because the opponents are not allowed to speak about divine speech
D) Because the opponents agree with the Ash'aris on the oneness of the meaning

Key Takeaway
The dispute over divine speech persists not because the parties disagree on the definition of speech in principle, but because the term itself is homonymous and each side latches onto a different referent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever pause to consider whether you are reciting the eternal attribute of Allah or a created expression? This passage says the term 'speech' covers both. How does that affect your reverence—or your carelessness—in each letter you utter?
?
Think of a heated argument you've had about religion where both sides used the same words but meant different things. After reading this passage, are you willing to be the one who first asks, 'What do you mean by that word?' even if it feels like losing face?
?
The opponents argue 'by way of necessity'—they engage even without accepting the premise. Have you ever joined a debate to score a point rather than to seek truth? What is one step you can take this week to ensure your theological discussions are about genuine inquiry, not just winning?

The Eternal Attribute Must Be Universal
Argument that an eternal attribute relates universally to all objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an eternal attribute must relate universally to all its objects, based on the unity of its relation.

Core Idea
An eternal attribute's relation is uniform, so it must relate to all or none.

This lesson presents a concise logical argument from classical kalam about the nature of eternal divine attributes. The author demonstrates that if an attribute like divine speech is eternal, it cannot be selective—it must relate to all possible objects equally. This reasoning helps clarify why God's attributes are all-encompassing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overall sense of the argument.
Notice the compact style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the key premise
Focus on the phrase 'its relation to the whole and to each individual is one relation'. This is the core reason for the universal relation.
3
Understand the logical consequence
See how the unity of relation leads to two conditional conclusions: if it relates to one, it relates to all; if it fails for one, it fails for all.
This shows the attribute cannot be selective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختصاص ،له والصفة الأزلية إذا كانت متعلقة وجب عموم تعلقها بجميع المتعلقات ؛ لأن نسبتها إلى الكل وإلى كل واحد نسبة واحدة فلئن تعلقت بواحدة تعلقت بالكل ، وإن تخلفت عن واحدة تخلفت عن الكل.
The eternal attribute: if it is related, its universal relation to all related things is necessary; because its relation to the whole and to each individual is one relation; if it relates to one, it relates to all; and if it fails for one, it fails for all.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents a concise argument about the nature of an eternal attribute (al-ṣifa al-azaliyya). It states that if such an attribute is related (mutaʿalliqa) to any object, it must be related universally to all possible objects of that relation. The reasoning is that the attribute's relation (nisba) to the whole (al-kull) and to each individual (kull wāḥid) is identical (nisba wāḥida). Therefore, if it relates to one, it necessarily relates to all; conversely, if it fails to relate to one, it fails to relate to all. This argument is foundational for discussions of divine attributes like speech, which must be all-comprehensive.

Key Terms
الصفة الأزلية
عموم التعلق
نسبة واحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal attribute could selectively relate to some objects but not others.
Correction: The passage proves that because the relation is one, it must be universal—if it relates to one, it relates to all; if it fails for one, it fails for all.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required if an eternal attribute is related to something?
A) It may relate to some things but not others.
B) It must relate to all related things universally.
C) Its relation is unrelated to its object.
D) It cannot be related to any object.

Key Takeaway
An eternal attribute's relation is uniform, so it must relate to all or none.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's eternal attributes are uniform and universal, how does that challenge your assumption that His mercy or guidance might be for others but not for you? In light of this lesson, will you fully trust that God's commands and promises are meant for you personally, or will you continue to live as if they are selective?
?
The passage argues that an eternal attribute either relates to all or to none. How does this truth expose any selective obedience in your life? Is there a command of God that you have deliberately ignored while claiming to follow others? What will you do today to align your obedience with the universality of His commands?
?
Consider the attribute of God's knowledge: it is eternal and universal, knowing all things equally. How does this confront your tendency to think that some of your sins are hidden or that God does not see certain areas of your life? Will you now live with the certainty that His knowledge encompasses every thought and action, and respond with repentance and sincerity?
