Mu'tazila Objection to Unity of Divine Speech and Ash'ari Response
Mu'tazila objection to one divine speech and Ash'ari five-attribute solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that divine speech cannot be one if it includes diverse types, and the early Ash'ari response proposing five attributes for divine speech.

Core Idea
One reality cannot be both command and news without contradiction.

The Mu'tazila raise a powerful objection: if God's speech is one eternal reality, how can it contain commands, prohibitions, promises, and threats? These are distinct kinds of speech. The Ash'ari reply by proposing that divine speech actually consists of five distinct attributes. This lesson examines the logic of both positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Read the first part where the Mu'tazila argue that one speech cannot be command, prohibition, etc. because they are different realities.
The objection uses the principle that one thing cannot have opposing properties.
2
Examine the genus analogy
The Mu'tazila consider the possibility that speech is a genus like animality, but they reject it because genera exist only mentally, not externally.
This shows their commitment to extramental reality.
3
Understand the conclusion
The Mu'tazila conclude that each type of speech must have its own attribute, and that the Ash'ari view leads to absurdity.
They equate speech to a single essence encompassing multiple realities.
4
See the Ash'ari response
The early Ash'ari solution posits five attributes: khabar, istikhbar, amr, nahy, nida. This would remove the problem.
The passage notes that this is not the well-known view of al-Ash'ari himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : لو كان كلامه تعالى واحدًا لاستحال أن يكون مع وحدته أمرًا ونهيًا وخبرًا واستخبار ووعداً ووعيدا، فإن هذه حقائق مختلفة وخصائص متباينة، ومن المحال اشتمال شيء واحد له حقيقة واحدة على خواص مختلفة، نعم يجوز أن يكون معنى واحد يشمل معاني مختلفة ؛ كالجنس والنوع، فإن الحيوانية معنى واحد يشمل معاني مختلفة، وكذلك الإنسانية تشمل أشخاصاً مختلفة، لكن لا يتصور وجود المعنى الجنسي إلا في الذهن، ويستحيل أن يتحقق في الوجود شيء واحد هو أشياء مختلفة وحقيقة واحدة هي بعينها حقائق مختلفة حتى تكون حقيقة واحدة علمًا وقدرة وإرادة وسوادًا وحركة فإن ذلك يرفع الحقائق ويؤدي إلى السفسطة، فنسبة الأمر والنهي والخبر والاستخبار والوعد والوعيد وهي حقائق مختلفة إلى الكلام كنسبة العلم والقدرة والإرادة والسواد والحركة وهي حقائق مختلفة إلى شيء واحد وذلك محال. وإذا كان لكل واحد من أقسام الكلام حقيقة خاصة فليكن لكل واحد منهما صفة خاصة، وإن قلتم : إن الكلام اسم جنس يشمل أنواعا صحيح غير أن الجنس لا يتحقق له وجود ما لم يتنوع بفصل يميز نوعًا عن نوع، والنوع لا يتحقق له وجود ما لم يتشخص بعارض يميز شخصاً عن شخص، وذلك كالعرض المطلق من حيث هو عرض لا يتحقق له وجود ما لم يتنوع بكونه لونا، واللون لا يتحقق له وجود ما لم يتعين بكونه سوادًا معينًا، وإلا فالعرض ليس له ذات على انفراده قائم بجوهر، ثم يكون هو بعينه علما وقدرة ولونا وسوادًا وطعما، وأنتم أثبتم الكلام قائماً بذات الباري تعالى على هذا المنهاج أنه حقيقة واحدة هي بعينها أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار وذلك محال. قالت الأشعرية حكي عن بعض متقدمي أصحابنا أنه أثبت الله خمس هي خمس صفات ؛ الخبر والاستخبار والأمر والنهي والنداء، فإن سلكنا هذا المسلك اندفع السؤال وارتفع الإشكال، لكن المشهور من مذهب أبي الحسن أن
The Muʿtazila said: If His speech were one, it would be impossible that with its unity it be command, prohibition, news, interrogation, promise, and threat. For these are different realities (ḥaqāʾiq mukhtalifa) and distinct properties. It is impossible for one thing having one reality to encompass different properties. However, it is possible that one meaning includes different meanings, like genus and species; for animality is one meaning that includes different meanings, and similarly humanity includes different individuals. But the existence of the generic meaning is only conceived in the mind; it is impossible that in external existence one thing be different things and one reality be essentially different realities such that one reality be knowledge, power, will, blackness, and motion. For that would nullify realities and lead to sophistry (safsata). So the relation of command, prohibition, news, interrogation, promise, and threat—which are different realities—to speech is like the relation of knowledge, power, will, blackness, and motion—which are different realities—to one thing, and that is impossible. And if each of the divisions of speech has a specific reality, then each should have a specific attribute. If you say that speech is a generic name encompassing types, that is correct, but the genus does not have existence unless it becomes diversified by a differentia distinguishing one type from another, and the type does not have existence unless it becomes individuated by an accident distinguishing one individual from another. This is like absolute accident (al-ʿarḍ al-muṭlaq) insofar as it is an accident does not have existence unless it becomes diversified by being a color, and color does not have existence unless it becomes determined as a specific blackness. Otherwise, the accident does not have a self by itself subsisting in a substance, then it would be in itself knowledge, power, color, blackness, and taste. And you have affirmed that speech subsists in the essence of God (qāʾiman bi-dhāt al-Bārī) in this way: that it is one reality that is in itself command, prohibition, news, and interrogation, and that is impossible. The Ashʿariyya said: It is related from some of our early companions that he affirmed that God has five attributes: khabar (news), istikhbār (interrogation), amr (command), nahy (prohibition), and nidāʾ (call). If we follow this path, the question is repelled and the problem is removed. However, the well-known view of Abū al-Ḥasan (al-Ashʿarī) is that...
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage contains a detailed objection from the Muʿtazila against the Ashʿari doctrine of a single eternal divine speech, followed by the beginning of the Ashʿari response. The Muʿtazila argue: if God's speech is one, it cannot simultaneously be command, prohibition, news, etc., because these are distinct realities (ḥaqāʾiq mukhtalifa). They illustrate with an analogy: one thing cannot be both knowledge and power. They consider the possibility that speech is a genus that includes species (like animality includes humans and horses), but note that a genus exists only mentally, not externally. In external existence it is impossible for one reality to be multiple distinct realities. Therefore, the relation of these different kinds of speech to one speech is impossible. They conclude that each division of speech must have its own specific attribute. Then they criticize the Ashʿari position that speech subsisting in God's essence is one reality that is all these things, calling it absurd. The Ashʿari response (beginning with 'qālat al-Ashʿariyya') reports that some early Ashʿari scholars held that God's speech consists of five distinct attributes: khabar, istikhbār, amr, nahy, and nidāʾ. Adopting this view would resolve the problem. However, the text notes that the well-known view of al-Ashʿari himself is different, but the passage cuts off before stating it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila completely deny that divine speech has any reality.
Correction: They instead argue that divine speech is multiple real attributes, each distinct, not a single essence.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila objection, what would be the consequence if divine speech were one reality?
A) It could still encompass different properties like command and news.
B) It would be impossible for it to be both command and prohibition.
C) It would be a genus existing externally.
D) It would be like animality including species.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argued that divine speech cannot be one if it includes distinct types, prompting early Ash'aris to propose five attributes for speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that one speech cannot be both command and promise because they are different realities. In your own life, do you sometimes treat God's commands as burdens while ignoring His promises? What would change if you saw both as inseparable aspects of His single word?
?
The early Ash'ari solution of five attributes shows a struggle to preserve the multiplicity in God's speech. How does this remind you that God's speech is not a simple monolith? Will you today approach the Quran with the awareness that every verse—command, prohibition, news, promise—is part of one divine message that demands a unified response from you?
?
The Mu'tazila used the analogy that one thing cannot be both knowledge and power. How does this challenge you to not reduce God's attributes to human categories? In your worship, are you acknowledging the full complexity of God's nature, or are you simplifying Him? Commit to learning one more attribute this week.

5 min
The Impossibility of Multiple Realities in One Essence
Objection against unitary divine speech via impossibility of multiple realities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection against the Ash'ari claim that divine speech is one attribute encompassing multiple distinct realities like command, prohibition, news, and inquiry.

Core Idea
One essence cannot be multiple distinct realities without destroying reality itself.

Before we delve into the Ash'ari response, we must first understand the force of this objection. The critic argues that if one thing were command, prohibition, news, and inquiry all at once, that would collapse the distinctions between different realities, leading to sophistry. This passage lays out the argument in detail, using analogies from genus and species.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Get a feel for the objection's flow.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage begins by stating that one reality cannot be multiple different realities. Find this statement and understand its implication.
The phrase 'واحد هو أشياء مختلفة' is key.
3
Examine the analogy with attributes
The objector draws an analogy: if speech relates to command/prohibition/etc. as one thing relates to knowledge/power/will, that is impossible. Understand why this analogy is used.
The objector equates the multiplicity in speech to the multiplicity in other attributes.
4
Understand the genus-species argument
The objector then argues that even if speech is a genus, it must be differentiated by differentiae and accidents to produce actual instances, like an accident becoming a color then blackness. This proves multiplicity requires distinction.
This part uses logical categories from Aristotelian philosophy.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The objector concludes that the Ash'ari position (one reality being command, prohibition, news, inquiry) is impossible, as it would lead to sophistry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحد هو أشياء مختلفة وحقيقة واحدة هي بعينها حقائق مختلفة حتى تكون حقيقة واحدة علمًا وقدرة وإرادة وسوادًا وحركة فإن ذلك يرفع الحقائق ويؤدي إلى السفسطة، فنسبة الأمر والنهي والخبر والاستخبار والوعد والوعيد وهي حقائق مختلفة إلى الكلام كنسبة العلم والقدرة والإرادة والسواد والحركة وهي حقائق مختلفة إلى شيء واحد وذلك محال. وإذا كان لكل واحد من أقسام الكلام حقيقة خاصة فليكن لكل واحد منهما صفة خاصة، وإن قلتم : إن الكلام اسم جنس يشمل أنواعا صحيح غير أن الجنس لا يتحقق له وجود ما لم يتنوع بفصل يميز نوعًا عن نوع، والنوع لا يتحقق له وجود ما لم يتشخص بعارض يميز شخصاً عن شخص، وذلك كالعرض المطلق من حيث هو عرض لا يتحقق له وجود ما لم يتنوع بكونه لونا، واللون لا يتحقق له وجود ما لم يتعين بكونه سوادًا معينًا، وإلا فالعرض ليس له ذات على انفراده قائم بجوهر، ثم يكون هو بعينه علما وقدرة ولونا وسوادا وطعما، وأنتم أثبتم الكلام قائما بذات الباري تعالى على هذا المنهاج أنه حقيقة واحدة هي بعينها أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار وذلك محال.
One thing is different things, and one reality is in itself different realities, such that one reality is knowledge, power, will, blackness, and movement — that would annihilate realities and lead to sophistry. So the relation of command, prohibition, news, inquiry, promise, and threat (which are different realities) to speech is like the relation of knowledge, power, will, blackness, and movement (which are different realities) to one thing, and that is impossible. And if each of the divisions of speech has a specific reality, then each should have a specific attribute. And if you say: speech is a genus name encompassing types — correct, but a genus does not achieve existence unless it is diversified by a differentia distinguishing one type from another, and a type does not achieve existence unless it is individuated by an accident distinguishing one individual from another. For example, absolute accident insofar as it is an accident does not achieve existence unless it is diversified by being a color, and color does not achieve existence unless it is specified by being a particular blackness. Otherwise, the accident does not have an essence on its own subsisting in a substance, and then it would be in itself knowledge, power, color, blackness, and taste. And you (Ash'aris) have established speech subsisting in the essence of Allah Almighty on this basis: that it is one reality which is in itself command, prohibition, news, and inquiry — that is impossible.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is an objection against the Ash'ari doctrine that divine speech is a single attribute, which at the same time is command, prohibition, news, and inquiry. The objector argues that if one thing were multiple distinct realities, it would destroy the distinctions between realities and lead to sophistry. He draws an analogy: if speech (kalam) relates to command, prohibition, etc., as a single essence relates to multiple distinct realities like knowledge, power, will, etc., that is impossible. He further argues that if each division of speech has its own reality, each should have its own attribute. If speech is a genus name, then it must be specified by differentiae and accidents to produce actual instances, just as an absolute accident must become a color and then a specific blackness to exist. Otherwise, an accident cannot be multiple things at once. The objector concludes that the Ash'ari claim that speech is one reality which is command, prohibition, news, and inquiry is impossible.

Misconception: That one reality can be multiple different realities without contradiction.
Correction: The objector argues that this would annihilate the distinctions between realities and lead to sophistry.

C) It would prove Ash'ari doctrine.

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that divine speech cannot be one reality that is simultaneously command, prohibition, news, and inquiry because that would collapse the distinctions between different realities and lead to sophistry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read this passage, it warns that collapsing distinct realities into one leads to sophistry. In your own understanding of Allah's attributes, have you ever settled for vague, undifferentiated concepts (like 'God is just loving' without distinguishing justice from mercy) to avoid the difficulty of specificity? What would it cost you to press into the precise distinctions this passage demands?
?
The objector says that one thing cannot be many different realities without destroying reality itself. Where in your spiritual life do you treat contradictory obligations (like a command to speak truth and a prohibition against causing harm) as if they are the same thing, avoiding the tension? What would it mean to honor each as a distinct reality from Allah?
?
This passage teaches that a genus must be differentiated to exist. Do you treat Allah's commands as generic suggestions rather than specific, individuated demands on your life today? Write down one command you have been evading by saying 'it's all the same' and commit to obeying it in its particularity this week.

Ash'ari Reply: Speech as One Attribute with Multiple Aspects
Ash'ari reply distinguishing between divisions and attributes of speech, with two positions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between divisions (aqsam) and attributes (awsaf) of speech, and the two positions within the school regarding the multiplicity of divine speech.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one attribute with multiple aspects, not multiple attributes.

In this passage, the author presents the Ash'ari response to the objection that unitary speech cannot account for commands, prohibitions, etc. He outlines two positions within the school, then provides analogies to show that a single attribute can have multiple properties. The key is distinguishing between divisions and attributes of a thing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage and find the two views: some early Ash'aris affirming five distinct attributes (news, inquiry, etc.), and the well-known view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari that speech is one attribute with properties.
The first position is attributed to 'some of our early associates'.
2
Examine the knowledge analogy
Notice how the author compares speech to divine knowledge: one knowledge relates to many known things without multiplication. This supports the idea that one attribute can have multiple objects.
Knowledge of eternal and temporal, existence and non-existence, are all one knowledge.
3
Understand the aqsam vs awsaf distinction
Focus on the substance analogy and the statement 'whatever in the witnessed realm is divisions for speech, in the unseen it is attributes'. This clarifies that the categories we apply to God differ from those in creation.
Divisions imply separation into species; attributes describe one essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الأشعرية حكي عن بعض متقدمي أصحابنا أنه أثبت الله خمس هي خمس صفات : الخبر والاستخبار والأمر والنهي والنداء، فإن سلكنا هذا المسلك اندفع السؤال وارتفع الإشكال، لكن المشهور من مذهب أبي الحسن أن الكلام صفة واحدة لها خاصية واحدة والخصوص وصفها بخص، وكونه أمرًا ونهيًا وخبرًا واستخبارًا خصائص أو لوازم تلك الصفة، كما أن علمه تعالى صفة واحدة تختلف معلوماتها، وهي غير مختلفة في أنفسها فيكون علما بالقديم والحادث والوجود والعدم وأجناس المحدثات وكما لا يجب تعدد العلم بعدد المعلومات، كذلك لا يجب تعدد الكلام بعدد المتعلقات وكون الكلام أمرًا ونهيا أوصاف الكلام لا أقسام الكلام. كما أن كون الجوهر قائما بذاته قابلا للعرض متحيزا ذا مساحة وحجم أوصاف نفسية للجوهر، وإن كانت معانيها مختلفة، كذلك كون الكلام أمرًا ونهيًا وخبرا واستخبارًا أوصاف نفسية للكلام، وإن كانت معانيها مختلفة، وليس اشتمال معنى الكلام على هذه المعاني كانقسام العرض إلى أصنافه المختلفة، وانقسام الحيوان إلى أنواعه المتمايزة، فأقسام الشيء غير وأوصاف الشيء غير، وكل ما في الشاهد للكلام من الأقسام فهو في الغائب للكلام أوصاف، والذي يحقق ذلك أن المعنى قد يكون واحدًا في ذاته، ويكون له أوصاف هي اعتبارات عقلية، ثم الاعتبارات العقلية قد تكون من جهة النسب والإضافات، وقد تكون من جهة
The Ash'aris said: It is narrated from some of our early associates that he affirmed for God five attributes: news, inquiry, command, prohibition, and call. If we take this path, the question is repelled and the difficulty is removed. But the well-known doctrine of Abu al-Hasan (al-Ash'ari) is that speech is one attribute having one specific property, and its being specific is described as specific, and its being command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are properties or concomitants of that attribute. Just as His knowledge, exalted be He, is one attribute while its objects of knowledge differ, and they do not differ in themselves, so it is knowledge of the eternal and the temporal, existence and non-existence, and the genera of originated things. And just as multiplicity of knowledge is not necessitated by the multiplicity of known things, likewise multiplicity of speech is not necessitated by the multiplicity of objects. And speech being command and prohibition are attributes (awsaf) of speech, not divisions (aqsam) of speech. Just as a substance being subsistent by itself, receptive to accidents, occupying space, having extension and volume are essential attributes of a substance, even though their meanings differ. Similarly, speech being command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are essential attributes of speech, even though their meanings differ. And the inclusion of these meanings in the meaning of speech is not like the division of accident into its various kinds, or the division of animal into its distinct species, because divisions of a thing are different from attributes of a thing. And whatever in the witnessed realm (al-shahid) is divisions for speech, in the unseen (al-gha'ib) it is attributes for speech. That which verifies this is that a meaning may be one in itself, and it has attributes which are mental considerations. Then mental considerations may be in terms of relations and additions, and may be in terms of...
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is the Ash'ari response to the objection against unitary divine speech. It presents two positions within the Ash'ari school. The first, attributed to some early Ash'aris, affirms five distinct attributes for God: news, inquiry, command, prohibition, and call. If this position is adopted, the objection is resolved. However, the more famous position of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari himself is that speech is a single attribute with a single specific property, and its being command, prohibition, etc., are properties (khawass) or concomitants (lawazim) of that attribute, not separate entities. The author then provides two analogies to support this. First, divine knowledge is one attribute that relates to many objects (known things) without being multiplied. Similarly, divine speech is one attribute with many objects (commands, prohibitions, etc.) without being divided. Second, a substance has multiple intrinsic attributes (e.g., subsisting by itself, occupying space, having extension) that are not divisions of the substance but attributes. Likewise, speech has intrinsic attributes (being command, prohibition, etc.) that are not divisions. The author emphasizes that this is different from how an accident is divided into species (e.g., color into black, white) or an animal into species (e.g., human, horse). Divisions (aqsam) are not the same as attributes (awsaf). What is considered a division in the witnessed realm (al-shahid) is considered an attribute in the unseen realm (al-gha'ib) when speaking of God. The passage concludes by explaining that a single meaning can have attributes that are mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya), which may be based on relations (nisab) and additions (idafat), though the text cuts off.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech must be multiple attributes because commands differ from prohibitions.
Correction: Commands, prohibitions, etc., are aspects (awsaf) of a single speech attribute, not separate attributes. The Ash'ari view maintains unity while accounting for diversity.

Checkpoint
According to the well-known Ash'ari position, how should we understand speech being command, prohibition, news, and inquiry?

B) They are divisions (aqsam) of speech.
C) They are properties or attributes (awsaf) of a single speech attribute.
D) They are independent realities.

Key Takeaway
Speech is one attribute with multiple properties; divisions in the witnessed realm are attributes in the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you receive it as a single unified message from God, or do you mentally break it into separate commands, prohibitions, and stories? How might viewing it as one cohesive address change your obedience and submission to every part of it?
?
If God's speech is a single attribute with multiple aspects—like a diamond with many facets—are you content to let it be one complete truth that governs your entire life, or do you pick and choose which aspects to obey? Which facet have you been ignoring?
?
The author warns against treating God's attributes like divisions in the created world. In your own understanding of God, do you unconsciously imagine Him as composed of parts or multiple beings? What does that reveal about your heart's pure tawhid?

The Unity of Divine Speech
Enunciation of the thesis and knowledge analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech is one attribute and that its different names (command, prohibition, etc.) are descriptions of that one attribute, not multiple speech acts.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one; its names are descriptions, not divisions.

The author begins by asserting the unity of divine speech, using the analogy of God's knowledge to show that one attribute can relate to many objects without multiplying. This lesson unpacks that foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from thesis to analogy to conclusion.
Pay special attention to the phrase 'أوصاف الكلام لا أقسام الكلام'.
2
Identify the main thesis
The passage begins by stating that speech is one attribute with one specificity. Find this statement and understand it as the core claim.
3
Understand the knowledge analogy
The author compares speech to God's knowledge: just as knowledge is one but covers many known things, speech is one but covers many objects. Ask yourself how this analogy supports the unity of speech.
The key point: multiplicity of objects does not require multiplicity of the attribute.
4
Note the conclusion
The passage ends this section with the explicit statement that being command and prohibition are descriptions of speech, not divisions. This is the main takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام صفة واحدة لها خاصية واحدة والخصوص وصفها حد خاص، وكونه أمرًا ونهيًا وخبرًا واستخبارًا خصائص أو لوازم تلك الصفة، كما أن علمه تعالى صفة واحدة تختلف معلوماتها، وهي غير مختلفة في أنفسها فيكون علما بالقديم والحادث والوجود والعدم وأجناس المحدثات وكما لا يجب تعدد العلم بعدد المعلومات، كذلك لا يجب تعدد الكلام بعدد المتعلقات وكون الكلام أمرا ونهيا أوصاف الكلام لا أقسام الكلام. كما أن كون الجوهر قائما بذاته قابلا للعرض متحيزا ذا مساحة وحجم أوصاف نفسية للجوهر، وإن كانت معانيها مختلفة، كذلك كون الكلام أمرًا ونهيًا وخبرا واستخبارًا أوصاف نفسية للكلام، وإن كانت معانيها مختلفة، وليس اشتمال معنى الكلام على هذه المعاني كانقسام العرض إلى أصنافه المختلفة، وانقسام الحيوان إلى أنواعه المتمايزة، فأقسام الشيء غير وأوصاف الشيء غير، وكل ما في الشاهد للكلام من الأقسام فهو في الغائب للكلام أوصاف، والذي يحقق ذلك أن المعنى قد يكون واحدًا في ذاته، ويكون له أوصاف هي اعتبارات عقلية، ثم الاعتبارات العقلية قد تكون من جهة النسب والإضافات، وقد تكون من جهة الموانع واللواحق، أليست الإرادة قد تسمى رضا إذا كان فعل الغير واقعا على نهج الصواب، وقد تسمى هي بعينها سخطًا إذا كان الفعل على غير الصواب؟ كذلك أمرًا إذا تعلق بالمأمور به ويسمى نهيًا إذا تعلق بالمنهي عنه، وهو في ذاته واحد، وتختلف أساميه من جهة متعلقاته حتى قيل : إن الكلام بحقيقته خبر عن المعلوم، وكل عالم يجد من نفسه خبرًا عن معلومه ضرورة، فإن تعلق بالشيء الذي وجب فعله فعله أمرًا، وإذا تعلق بالشيء الذي حرم سمي نهيًا، وإن تعلق بشيء ليس فيه اقتضاء وطلب سمي خبرًا واستخبارًا، فهذه أسامي الكلام من جهة متعلقاته كأسامي الرب تعالى من جهة أفعاله.
Speech is one attribute possessing one specificity, and the specification is its specific description. Its being command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are properties or necessary concomitants of that attribute, just as His knowledge – exalted is He – is one attribute, and its objects of knowledge differ while they are not different in themselves, so it is knowledge of the eternal, the originated, existence, non-existence, and the genera of originated things. And just as it is not necessary that knowledge be multiple according to the number of known things, likewise it is not necessary that speech be multiple according to the number of objects. And the fact that speech is command and prohibition are descriptions of speech, not divisions of speech. Just as the fact that the substance is self-subsistent, receptive of accidents, occupying space, possessing area and volume are essential descriptions of the substance, even if their meanings are different; likewise the fact that speech is command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are essential descriptions of speech, even if their meanings are different. And the inclusion of these meanings in the meaning of speech is not like the division of accident into its various species, nor the division of animal into its distinct types; for divisions of a thing are other than descriptions of a thing. And as for whatever divisions exist for speech in the physical world, these are descriptions for speech in the unseen. What verifies this is that a meaning may be one in its essence, and it may have descriptions which are mental considerations. Then mental considerations may be from the standpoint of relations and additions, and may be from the standpoint of impediments and consequent properties. Does not will sometimes be called approval when the act of another occurs on the path of correctness, and it may be called, the very same [will], disapproval when the act is not on the correct path? Similarly, [speech] is called command when it relates to the commanded thing, and is called prohibition when it relates to the prohibited thing, while it is one in essence, and its names vary according to its objects. Hence it is said: Speech, in its reality, is information about the known. And every knower finds in himself information about his known thing necessarily. If it relates to a thing whose commission is obligatory, it is called command; if it relates to a thing that is forbidden, it is called prohibition; if it relates to a thing that involves neither requirement nor demand, it is called news and inquiry. So these are the names of speech according to its objects, like the names of the Lord – exalted is He – according to His actions.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation. The author argues that divine speech is one eternal attribute, and its being command, prohibition, etc. are descriptions (awsaf) not divisions (aqsam). He begins by stating that speech is one attribute with one specificity. Then he gives an analogy: God's knowledge is one but covers all known things; similarly, speech is one but relates to various objects. Next, he distinguishes between descriptions and divisions: a substance has multiple essential descriptions but is one substance; likewise speech. He then explains that the inclusion of these meanings in speech is not like the division of accident or animal into species. He further explains that a meaning can be one in essence but have multiple mental considerations, which may be relational or based on impediments. He gives the example of will (irada) which is called approval or disapproval based on the action's correctness. Similarly, speech is called command, prohibition, etc. based on its objects. He concludes that these are names of speech according to its objects, like God's names according to His actions.

كلام
صفة
أوصاف
أقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different names like 'command' and 'prohibition' imply different speech acts or multiple attributes.
Correction: They are descriptions (awsaf) of one attribute, not separate entities.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the analogy with God's knowledge show?
A) Knowledge and speech are the same attribute.
B) Many objects of knowledge require many knowledges.
C) One attribute can have many objects without multiplying.
D) Speech is divided into command, prohibition, etc.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is one eternal attribute; its names indicate relational descriptions, not separate divisions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you experience it as a fragmented collection of commands and stories, or as the unified, eternal speech of God? How would your reverence change if you truly believed the latter?
?
The passage teaches that God's command and prohibition are not separate but descriptions of one attribute. Are there areas of your life where you pick and choose which commands to follow, as if they were independent? How can you start seeing them as a single, consistent call from God?
?
If God's speech is one and eternal, then every word He speaks carries the same authority. Does your heart tremble at His promise as much as at His threat? What does your reaction reveal about your understanding of His unified speech?

Descriptions vs. Divisions
Distinction between descriptions and divisions with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between descriptions (awsaf) and divisions (aqsam) of an attribute, using the analogy of a substance and the contrast with the division of accidents and animals.

Core Idea
Descriptions characterize one reality; divisions imply multiplicity.

Having established the unity of speech, the author now clarifies that its different aspects are like the essential descriptions of a substance, not like the division of an accident into species. This lesson examines that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the analogy of the substance (جوهر) and its descriptions.
Substance here means a self-subsistent entity in philosophy.
2
Understand the substance analogy
The substance has multiple essential descriptions (self-subsistent, receptive of accidents, etc.) yet remains one substance. Similarly, speech has multiple descriptions (command, etc.) yet remains one speech.
The key is that descriptions do not fragment the essence.
3
Contrast with divisions of accident and animal
The author contrasts with the division of accident into species (like color into red, blue) and animal into types (human, horse). Those are real divisions, not just descriptions. Learn why speech's aspects are not like that.
Divisions imply different kinds; descriptions imply different facets of one kind.
4
Absorb the rule about physical vs. unseen
The author states: whatever divisions exist for speech in the physical world (i.e., created speech) are descriptions for speech in the unseen (divine speech). This is a key principle.
What appears as separate in creation is a unified description in the divine.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام صفة واحدة لها خاصية واحدة والخصوص وصفها حد خاص، وكونه أمرًا ونهيًا وخبرًا واستخبارًا خصائص أو لوازم تلك الصفة، كما أن علمه تعالى صفة واحدة تختلف معلوماتها، وهي غير مختلفة في أنفسها فيكون علما بالقديم والحادث والوجود والعدم وأجناس المحدثات وكما لا يجب تعدد العلم بعدد المعلومات، كذلك لا يجب تعدد الكلام بعدد المتعلقات وكون الكلام أمرا ونهيا أوصاف الكلام لا أقسام الكلام. كما أن كون الجوهر قائما بذاته قابلا للعرض متحيزا ذا مساحة وحجم أوصاف نفسية للجوهر، وإن كانت معانيها مختلفة، كذلك كون الكلام أمرًا ونهيًا وخبرا واستخبارًا أوصاف نفسية للكلام، وإن كانت معانيها مختلفة، وليس اشتمال معنى الكلام على هذه المعاني كانقسام العرض إلى أصنافه المختلفة، وانقسام الحيوان إلى أنواعه المتمايزة، فأقسام الشيء غير وأوصاف الشيء غير، وكل ما في الشاهد للكلام من الأقسام فهو في الغائب للكلام أوصاف، والذي يحقق ذلك أن المعنى قد يكون واحدًا في ذاته، ويكون له أوصاف هي اعتبارات عقلية، ثم الاعتبارات العقلية قد تكون من جهة النسب والإضافات، وقد تكون من جهة الموانع واللواحق، أليست الإرادة قد تسمى رضا إذا كان فعل الغير واقعا على نهج الصواب، وقد تسمى هي بعينها سخطًا إذا كان الفعل على غير الصواب؟ كذلك أمرًا إذا تعلق بالمأمور به ويسمى نهيًا إذا تعلق بالمنهي عنه، وهو في ذاته واحد، وتختلف أساميه من جهة متعلقاته حتى قيل : إن الكلام بحقيقته خبر عن المعلوم، وكل عالم يجد من نفسه خبرًا عن معلومه ضرورة، فإن تعلق بالشيء الذي وجب فعله فعله أمرًا، وإذا تعلق بالشيء الذي حرم سمي نهيًا، وإن تعلق بشيء ليس فيه اقتضاء وطلب سمي خبرًا واستخبارًا، فهذه أسامي الكلام من جهة متعلقاته كأسامي الرب تعالى من جهة أفعاله.
Speech is one attribute possessing one specificity, and the specification is its specific description. Its being command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are properties or necessary concomitants of that attribute, just as His knowledge – exalted is He – is one attribute, and its objects of knowledge differ while they are not different in themselves, so it is knowledge of the eternal, the originated, existence, non-existence, and the genera of originated things. And just as it is not necessary that knowledge be multiple according to the number of known things, likewise it is not necessary that speech be multiple according to the number of objects. And the fact that speech is command and prohibition are descriptions of speech, not divisions of speech. Just as the fact that the substance is self-subsistent, receptive of accidents, occupying space, possessing area and volume are essential descriptions of the substance, even if their meanings are different; likewise the fact that speech is command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are essential descriptions of speech, even if their meanings are different. And the inclusion of these meanings in the meaning of speech is not like the division of accident into its various species, nor the division of animal into its distinct types; for divisions of a thing are other than descriptions of a thing. And as for whatever divisions exist for speech in the physical world, these are descriptions for speech in the unseen. What verifies this is that a meaning may be one in its essence, and it may have descriptions which are mental considerations. Then mental considerations may be from the standpoint of relations and additions, and may be from the standpoint of impediments and consequent properties. Does not will sometimes be called approval when the act of another occurs on the path of correctness, and it may be called, the very same [will], disapproval when the act is not on the correct path? Similarly, [speech] is called command when it relates to the commanded thing, and is called prohibition when it relates to the prohibited thing, while it is one in essence, and its names vary according to its objects. Hence it is said: Speech, in its reality, is information about the known. And every knower finds in himself information about his known thing necessarily. If it relates to a thing whose commission is obligatory, it is called command; if it relates to a thing that is forbidden, it is called prohibition; if it relates to a thing that involves neither requirement nor demand, it is called news and inquiry. So these are the names of speech according to its objects, like the names of the Lord – exalted is He – according to His actions.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation. The author argues that divine speech is one eternal attribute, and its being command, prohibition, etc. are descriptions (awsaf) not divisions (aqsam). He begins by stating that speech is one attribute with one specificity. Then he gives an analogy: God's knowledge is one but covers all known things; similarly, speech is one but relates to various objects. Next, he distinguishes between descriptions and divisions: a substance has multiple essential descriptions but is one substance; likewise speech. He then explains that the inclusion of these meanings in speech is not like the division of accident or animal into species. He further explains that a meaning can be one in essence but have multiple mental considerations, which may be relational or based on impediments. He gives the example of will (irada) which is called approval or disapproval based on the action's correctness. Similarly, speech is called command, prohibition, etc. based on its objects. He concludes that these are names of speech according to its objects, like God's names according to His actions.

Key Terms
جوهر
عرض
أوصاف نفسية
أقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The different names of speech are like different species or types, implying multiple speech attributes.
Correction: They are descriptions (awsaf) of one attribute, not divisions (aqsam) into different kinds.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how is the inclusion of meanings (command, prohibition, etc.) in speech different from the division of accident into species?
A) It is exactly the same; speech has multiple kinds.
B) It is like the division of animal into types.
C) It is not like division; it is description of one thing.
D) Speech has no meanings.

Key Takeaway
Descriptions (awsaf) characterize one essence; divisions (aqsam) create separate kinds – divine speech's names are descriptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite a verse of command in the Quran, do you feel you are encountering a separate 'piece' of God or an expression of His single, living attribute of speech? How would seeing it as a description of the One Speaker change your awe?
?
If God's command and prohibition are both descriptions of one speech, then every 'do' and 'don't' comes from the same Lord. Which of His commands have you been treating as optional, as if they come from a different source? What will you change today?
?
The analogy of substance shows that many descriptions can apply to one thing without dividing it. In your worship, is your understanding of God's attributes fragmented? Ask Him to unify your heart's vision of His oneness.

Mental Considerations and Relational Names
Explanation of mental considerations and relational naming

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how one essence can have multiple names based on mental considerations and relations, using the examples of will (irada) and speech, and how this applies to divine speech's names according to its objects.

Core Idea
One essence, many names based on relations.

The author deepens the argument by explaining that a single meaning can be described differently based on mental considerations. He illustrates with the will being called approval or disapproval, and speech being called command, prohibition, etc., depending on its objects. This lesson completes the picture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the concept of mental considerations (اعتبارات عقلية) and how they give rise to names.
Mental considerations are conceptual distinctions, not real separations.
2
Understand the will analogy
The author gives an example: the will (irada) is called approval when the action is correct, and disapproval when the action is incorrect. Yet it is the same will. This shows how one thing gets different names based on relation.
The names do not indicate different wills, but different relations.
3
Apply to speech
Similarly, speech is called command when related to what is commanded, prohibition when related to what is prohibited, etc. The essence of speech remains one; only the relation changes.
4
Note the conclusion about names
The author concludes that these names of speech are like the names of Allah based on His actions – they indicate diverse relations, not multiplicity in the essence.
This reinforces the unity of divine attributes.

According to the author, why is speech sometimes called 'command' and sometimes 'prohibition'?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call on Allah as ar-Rahman and al-Quddus, do you habitually slip into imagining two separate 'parts' of God, or do you truly feel that you are addressing one undivided Being? What would change in your worship if you lived this unity moment by moment?
?
The Qur'an contains both stern warnings and gentle promises; do you treat them as coming from two different attitudes in Allah, or do you recognize them as the very same divine speech arising from one will? How might that recognition humble your fear and elevate your hope?
?
Consider your own inner state: you have one will, yet you label actions as 'good' or 'bad' based on your perspective. Are you willing to admit that your mental considerations might distort reality, while Allah's names are perfectly true? What would it take for you to align your 'names' for things with His judgment?

Refutation of the Objection Against the Unity of Divine Speech
The author refutes an objection against the unity of divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes an objection against the unity of divine speech by exposing the opponent's own problematic concept of 'haal'.

Core Idea
The opponent's own position is more self-defeating than the one he attacks.

The author responds to an objection that claims the unity of divine speech is impossible. He points out that the opponent's only argument is to force a consequence on the Ash'ari view. More importantly, he turns the tables by showing that the opponent's own doctrine of 'states' (ahwal) leads to an even more absurd unity. This passage illustrates a classic dialectical strategy of counter-refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the rhetorical flow: the author first dismisses the opponent's argument, then launches a counter-attack.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the opponent's primary argument
The opponent uses ilzam (forcing a consequence) against those who affirm the unity of speech. The author notes that if the opponent denies kalam nafsi entirely, he cannot even discuss unity.
The opponent's argument is based on an assumption that is not shared.
3
Observe the author's counter-attack
The author points out that the opponent's own concept of 'haal' (state) is more absurd. The opponent affirms that God's knowledge and power are a single state that makes Him knowing and powerful, a unity even stranger than the unity of speech.
This is a tu quoque argument: you do the same thing you criticize.
4
Understand the analogy
The author argues: if the opponent can accept a unitary state for attributes, then he cannot object to a unitary speech that manifests as command, prohibition, and news by different considerations.
The key is that unity does not preclude multiplicity of aspects.
5
Reflect on the philosophers mentioned
The author alludes to philosophers who deny eternal speech even more strongly. This hints at the opponent's potential inconsistency if he aligns with them, but the main point is that the opponent's objection collapses.
The last phrase is incomplete but suggests a further critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : ليس بيد الخصم في هذه المسألة إلا مجرد الإلزام على مذهب من قال بوحدة الكلام، وإلا فمن أنكر أصل الكلام النفسي كيف يسمع منه القول في الوحدة، ولكن العجب من هذا الملزم أنه التزم ما هو أمحل المحال في وحدة الحال التي هي مصححة الأحوال، فإنه قال : عالمية الباري سبحانه وقادريته حال وله حال توجب كونه عالمًا قادرًا، فقد أثبت حالا لها خصوصية العلم والقدرة وهي واحدة في نفسها وراء الذات، فكيف يستبعد ممن يثبت كلامًا هو أمر ونهي وخبر وهو في نفسه واحد مختلف الاعتبار والفلاسفة الذين هم أشد إنكارا للكلام الأزلي
Then we say: The opponent has in this issue nothing but mere forcing of a consequence upon the doctrine of those who say the unity of speech. Otherwise, whoever denies the very principle of internal speech (kalam nafsi), how can a statement about unity be heard from him? But the wonder is that this imposer [of the consequence] has committed himself to what is more absurd in the unity of the 'state' (haal) which is the corrector of states. For he said: The omniscience and omnipotence of the Exalted are a state, and He has a state which necessitates His being Knowing and Powerful. So he affirmed a state having the specificity of knowledge and power, which is one in itself and beyond the essence. So how can he deem it improbable from one who affirms speech that is command, prohibition, and news, while it is one in itself, differing in consideration? And the philosophers, who are more severe in denying eternal speech...
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is an objection and refutation. The author responds to an opponent who argues against the unity of divine speech. He states that the opponent's only recourse is to force a consequence (ilzam) on those who affirm the unity of speech. He points out that if the opponent denies internal speech altogether, he cannot even discuss unity. Then he turns to the opponent's own position: the opponent affirms that God's attributes of knowledge and power are 'states' (ahwal) that are distinct from the essence but are one in themselves. This is a more absurd position. So how can the opponent object to someone who affirms speech as one attribute with different considerations? The author ends by mentioning the philosophers who deny eternal speech even more strongly, implying inconsistency. This shows the author's defense of the Ash'ari doctrine of kalam nafsi.

Key Terms
كلام نفسي
إلزام
حال
فلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's objection against unity of speech is logically sound.
Correction: The author shows that the opponent's own position on divine states ('ahwal') involves a similarly problematic unity, and is actually more absurd, thus undermining the objection.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the opponent's concept of 'haal' (state)?
A) It is the same as kalam nafsi
B) It is more absurd than the unity of speech
C) It is a correct doctrine
D) It is rejected by philosophers

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the opponent by showing that his own doctrine of 'ahwal' is a self-defeating position that undermines his objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disagree with someone about a matter of faith, do you ever use arguments that could just as easily be turned against you? What does this passage teach you about the danger of inconsistency in defending your beliefs?
?
This lesson shows how the opponent's own position was more absurd than the one he criticized. Have you ever criticized others for a failing that you yourself possess in a subtler form? How can you guard against hypocrisy in your own theological convictions?
?
The author points out that if the opponent denies internal speech, he cannot even coherently discuss unity. Are there areas in your life where you reject a truth but then rely on that truth to argue against it? What would it take for you to humbly accept the full implications of divine speech?

Refuting the Opponent Using the Concept of State and the Philosophers' View
Refutes opponent by showing his acceptance of a unified state, then uses philosophers' single intellect to argue for unified speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the opponent's denial of unified divine speech by pointing out the opponent's own acceptance of a unified state, and how he uses the philosophers' doctrine of the single intellect and first principle to argue for an eternal speech.

Core Idea
The opponent's own commitment to a unified state refutes his denial of unified speech.

In this passage, the author turns the opponent's own concepts against him. Having earlier defended the doctrine of a single, eternal divine speech that encompasses command, prohibition, and news, he now shows that the opponent's objection is self-defeating. By examining the philosophers' analogous doctrine of the first intellect, the author strengthens his case for an eternal speech that serves as the source of all determination and obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the strong rhetorical style – the author is refuting an opponent.
2
Identify the opponent's position
Find where the author describes the opponent's objection and the opponent's own acceptance of a unified state (hal) that grounds multiple attributes.
The opponent says God's omniscience and omnipotence are a state, one in itself beyond the essence.
3
Understand the analogy with philosophers
See how the author uses the philosophers' concept of a single intellect from which infinite forms emanate, analogous to speech having many aspects from one essence.
The philosophers deny eternal speech but posit a single intellect; the author argues they are inconsistent.
4
Follow the final argument from the first principle
The author presents a clearer argument: the philosophers' first principle does not become multiple by attributes, so why not apply the same logic to speech and posit an eternal speech as the beginning and end?
The passage ends with Quranic verses emphasizing that all command returns to Allah.

ثم نقول : ليس بيد الخصم في هذه المسألة إلا مجرد الإلزام على مذهب من قال بوحدة الكلام، وإلا فمن أنكر أصل الكلام النفسي كيف يسمع منه القول في الوحدة، ولكن العجب من هذا الملزم أنه التزم ما هو أمحل المحال في وحدة الحال التي هي مصححة الأحوال، فإنه قال : عالمية الباري سبحانه وقادريته حال وله حال توجب كونه عاما قادرًا، فقد أثبت حالا لها خصوصية العلم والقدرة وهي واحدة في نفسها وراء الذات، فكيف يستبعد ممن يثبت كلامًا هو أمر ونهي وخبر وهو في نفسه واحد مختلف الاعتبار والفلاسفة الذين هم أشد إنكارا للكلام الأزلي ووحدته أثبتوا عقلا واحدا في ذاته ووجوده وتفيض منه صور لا تتناهى مختلفة ربما تختلف أساميه باختلاف الصور والفيض عندهم كالتعلق عند المتكلم، والعقل الأول كالكلام في وحدته واختلاف الكلام بالأمر والنهي كاختلاف الفيض بالصور. وأوضح من ذلك مذهبهم في المبدأ الأول لا يتكثر بتكثر الموجودات اللازمة والصفات والأسماء ؛ إما إضافة وإما سلب وإما مركبة من إضافة وسلب فهلا قالوا في الكلام كذلك، وهلا أثبتوا كلامًا أزلياً على منهاج إثباتهم له عناية أزلية حتى يكون هو المبدأ وإليه المنتهى تقديراً في أفعاله الخاصة ويكون أمره المبدأ وإليه الرجعي تكليفا على أفعال عباده فيكون له الخلق في الأول والأمر في الثاني، ويرجع الكل إليه ( لِلَّهِ الأَمرُ مِن قَبْلُ وَمِنْ بَعْدُ ) ( وَإِلَيْهِ يُرْجَعُ الْأَمْرُ كُلُّهُ ).
Then we say: The opponent has nothing in this issue except mere objection based on the doctrine of the unity of speech; otherwise, if one denies the reality of internal speech (kalam nafsi), how can he hear the discussion about unity? But the wonder is that this objector has committed what is the most impossible of impossibilities regarding the unity of the state (hal) which is the ground of states. For he said: The omniscience and omnipotence of the Exalted are a state, and He has a state that necessitates His being knowing and powerful. So he posited a state with the specificity of knowledge and power, one in itself beyond the essence. So why does he consider it far-fetched for one who posits a speech that is command, prohibition, and news, being one in itself, differing by consideration? And the philosophers, who are more severe in denying eternal speech and its unity, posited a single intellect (aql) one in its essence and existence, and from it emanate infinite different forms, perhaps its names differ by the difference of forms. And emanation (fayd) in their view is like relation (ta'alluq) in the view of the theologian. The first intellect is like speech in its unity, and the difference of speech by command and prohibition is like the difference of emanation by forms. And clearer than that is their doctrine regarding the first principle: it does not become multiple by the multiplicity of necessary existents and attributes and names; either as addition (idafa), negation (salb), or a combination of addition and negation. So why do they not say the same about speech? And why did they not posit eternal speech in the manner of their positing of eternal care (inaya), so that it is the beginning and to it is the end, as determination of His specific actions, and His command is the beginning and to it is the return as obligation upon the actions of His servants, so that creation is His in the first and command in the second, and all returns to Him. 'To Allah is the command before and after' and 'To Him is the return of all command.'
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage refutes the opponent (khasm) who objects to the unity of divine speech by accusing him of a greater absurdity: he himself posits a unified state (hal) that grounds the attributes of omniscience and omnipotence, a state that is one in itself yet has the specific properties of knowledge and power. If he accepts such a state, he should not reject a speech that is one in essence but has multiple aspects (command, prohibition, news). Then the author cites the philosophers who deny eternal speech but posit a single intellect (aql) from which emanate infinite forms, and the names of that intellect differ by the forms, analogous to speech names differing by command and prohibition. The author then presents an even clearer argument: the philosophers' doctrine of the first principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) does not become multiple by the multiplicity of necessary existents and attributes; they are either additions (idafa), negations (salb), or combinations. So why do the philosophers not apply the same logic to speech and posit an eternal speech analogous to their eternal care (inaya)? Such an eternal speech would be the beginning and end of determination (taqdir) for God's actions and His command would be the source of obligation for servants' actions. Thus, creation is His in the first (al-khalq fi al-awwal) and command in the second (al-amr fi al-thani), and all returns to Him, citing Quranic verses.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's objection that unified speech is absurd is valid.
Correction: The opponent himself accepts a unified state (hal) that grounds multiple attributes, so he cannot consistently deny a unified speech that appears as multiple commands.

Checkpoint
What do the philosophers posit that the author uses as an analogy for the unity of speech?
A) A single state (hal)
B) A single intellect (aql)
C) A single will (irada)
D) A single command (amr)

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the opponent by showing his own acceptance of a unified state, and uses the philosophers' doctrine of a single intellect and the first principle to argue for an eternal, unified divine speech that encompasses all commands and determinations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that God's single command encompasses all prohibitions and obligations, does it change how you view your daily choices? Are you obeying as if each command is a fragment or as part of a unified divine purpose?
?
The opponent accepted a unified state for God's attributes but denied unified speech. Where in your own life do you accept a truth in one area but refuse to apply it consistently in another? Will you ask Allah to grant you consistency in faith and action?
?
If God's speech is one yet includes command, prohibition, and news, then every verse of the Quran addresses you with the same divine authority. Are you approaching the Quran as a single message from the One, or do you pick and choose? What would it take for you to submit to its unity today?

The Philosophers' Own Principle Demands Eternal Speech
Argues for eternal speech from philosophers' own principles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the philosophers' own principles to argue for the necessity of eternal divine speech.

Core Idea
If philosophers affirm eternal care, they must also affirm eternal speech.

The author engages with the philosophers' framework to argue for a key Islamic doctrine: that Allah's speech is eternal. He points out that the philosophers already posit eternal care as the principle of determination, so consistency demands they also affirm eternal speech as the principle of command. This argument shows how Islamic theologians could use philosophical principles to defend revealed truths.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophical framework of emanation
The passage begins by describing how philosophers view the first intellect as a source from which infinite forms emanate. Note the analogy they draw: emanation is like relation for the mutakallim, and the first intellect is like speech in its unity.
This sets the stage for the author's critique.
2
See the analogy between the first intellect and speech
The author points out that just as the philosophers say the first intellect is one yet its emanations differ, similarly speech is one yet its commands and prohibitions differ. This parity suggests speech could be eternal.
3
Grasp the author's stronger argument from the First Principle
The author then offers a clearer argument: the philosophers already say the First Principle does not become multiple through attributes (relational or negative). They also affirm an eternal care. So why not affirm an eternal speech as the principle of determination and obligation?
This is the core of the argument: consistency requires extending the same reasoning to speech.
4
Connect to the Quranic conclusion
The author concludes with Quranic verses emphasizing that all command returns to Allah. This links the rational argument to revelation, showing that the philosophers' own principles, if followed consistently, lead to a Quranic truth.
The verses are: 'To Allah belongs the command before and after' and 'To Him is returned the whole command.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووحدته أثبتوا عقلا واحدا في ذاته ووجوده وتفيض منه صور لا تتناهى مختلفة ربما تختلف أساميه باختلاف الصور والفيض عندهم كالتعلق عند المتكلم، والعقل الأول كالكلام في وحدته واختلاف الكلام بالأمر والنهي كاختلاف الفيض بالصور. وأوضح من ذلك مذهبهم في المبدأ الأول لا يتكثر بتكثر الموجودات اللازمة والصفات والأسماء؛ إما إضافة وإما سلب وإما مركبة من إضافة وسلب، فهلا قالوا في الكلام كذلك، وهلا أثبتوا كلاما أزليا على منهاج إثباتهم له عناية أزلية حتى يكون هو المبدأ وإليه المنتهى تقديرا في أفعاله الخاصة، ويكون أمره المبدأ وإليه الرجعي تكليفا على أفعال عباده، فيكون له الخلق في الأول والأمر في الثاني، ويرجع الكل إليه: (لِلَّهِ الْأَمْرُ مِن قَبْلُ وَمِن بَعْدُ) (وَإِلَيْهِ يُرْجَعُ الْأَمْرُ كُلُّهُ).
And they affirmed a single intellect in His essence and existence, from which emanate infinite different forms, whose names perhaps differ by difference of forms, and emanation according to them is like relation according to the mutakallim. And the first intellect is like speech in its unity, and the difference of speech by command and prohibition is like the difference of emanation by forms. And clearer than that is their doctrine regarding the First Principle: He does not become multiple by the multiplicity of necessary existents, attributes, and names—either by addition or negation or combination of addition and negation. So why did they not say similarly about speech, and why did they not affirm an eternal speech in the manner of their affirmation of an eternal care for Him, so that it (speech) would be the principle and to it is the end—determination in His particular acts—and His command would be the principle and to it is the return—obligation upon the acts of His servants. So He has creation in the first and command in the second, and all returns to Him: 'To Allah belongs the command before and after' and 'To Him is returned the whole command.'
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is an argument directed at the philosophers (falasifa) who posit a First Principle that does not become multiple by multiplicity of names and attributes, but they also posit an eternal care ('inayah azaliyyah) as the principle of determination of particular acts. The author argues that they should similarly affirm an eternal speech (kalam azali) as the principle of command and obligation. The passage begins by describing the philosophers' view of the first intellect as a source of emanation, analogous to the mutakallim's view of speech. The author then makes a stronger point: since the philosophers admit that the First Principle does not multiply through attributes that are relational or negative, they should extend this to speech. They already posit an eternal care; why not an eternal command that serves as the principle of taklif? The passage concludes with Quranic verses emphasizing that all command returns to God.

الكلام الأزلي
المبدأ الأول

التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is arguing that eternal speech is identical to eternal care.
Correction: The author argues by analogy: if eternal care is accepted as the principle of determination, then a similar eternal speech should be accepted as the principle of command. They are distinct, but the same reasoning applies.

Checkpoint

B) Eternal speech

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If you truly believed that Allah's speech is eternal and that His command is the ultimate principle of your life, would you still treat the Quran as a mere book of guidance rather than the living, authoritative voice of your Lord? What one change in your daily relationship with the Quran would prove you believe in its eternal nature?

The Mu'tazila Objection Against Contradictory Attributes in One Speech
Mu'tazila argue that command and prohibition cannot be attributes of one speech due to contradictory properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that contradictory attributes like command and prohibition cannot inhere in a single divine speech, and how they distinguish between essential attributes and conflicting properties.

Core Idea
A single speech cannot be both command and prohibition—their properties clash.

The Mu'tazila accept that a single essence can have multiple essential attributes, but they draw the line at attributes that are mutually exclusive. They argue that command and prohibition have distinct properties that cannot coexist in one speech, even if we consider different aspects. This passage presents their key objection against the Ash'ari view of one divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the Mu'tazila's concession first, then their main objection.
Pay attention to the distinction between essential attributes and contradictory properties.
2
Identify the two types of attributes
The Mu'tazila distinguish between essential attributes (sifat nafsiyyah) and contradictory properties. Identify which category command and prohibition fall into according to them.
They claim command and prohibition are not relational but intrinsic.
3
Understand the non-relational argument
The Mu'tazila argue that command and prohibition are not relational attributes, because even without considering an object, speech remains command or prohibition. This is key to their objection.
Contrast with relational attributes that vanish when the relation is removed.
4
Reflect on the implication for divine speech
Consider why this objection challenges the Ash'ari view of one eternal speech. If speech is one, how can it be both command and prohibition at once?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة: إثبات صفات نفسية لذات واحدة غير مستحيل، لكن إثبات خواص مختلفة وصفات متضادة لشيء واحد هو المستحيل، ومن المعلوم أن الأمر والنهي يتضادان ولهما خاصيتان مختلفتان فإثبات ذلك لكلام واحد محال، ونحن لا تنكر اختلاف الأسامي لشيء واحد لاختلاف الوجوه والاعتبارات، لكنا ننكر اختلاف الخواص المتباينة لشيء واحد، ولا نشك أن كون الكلام أمرا ونهيا ليس من جملة النسب والإضافات فإن الصفات الإضافية تتحقق عند الإضافة ولا يتحقق عند رفع الإضافة، ويتطرق إليها التبدل والتغير، وليس كون الكلام أمرا ونهيا مما يتحقق عند الإضافة، بل هما من أخص أوصاف الكلام، سواء لاحظنا جانب المتعلق أو لم نلاحظ، وكذلك لو رفعنا المتعلق عن الوهم لم يخرج الكلام عن كونه أمرا ونهيا وخبرا واستخبارا.
The Mu'tazila said: Affirmation of essential attributes for a single essence is not impossible, but affirmation of different properties and contradictory attributes for one thing is impossible. And it is known that command and prohibition are opposites and have two different properties, so affirming that for one speech is impossible. We do not deny difference of names for one thing due to difference of aspects and considerations, but we deny difference of mutually exclusive properties for one thing. And we do not doubt that the being of speech as command and prohibition is not among relations and additions, for relational attributes are realized upon relation and are not realized when the relation is removed, and they are subject to change and alteration. And being of speech as command and prohibition is not something realized upon relation; rather, they are among the most specific descriptions of speech, whether we consider the aspect of the object or not. Likewise, if we remove the object from imagination, speech does not cease to be command, prohibition, news, and inquiry.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila objection against the Ash'ari view that divine speech is one eternal attribute that encompasses command, prohibition, etc. The Mu'tazila begin by conceding that essential attributes (sifat nafsiyyah) for one essence are possible, but they draw a line at contradictory attributes like command and prohibition, which have mutually exclusive properties. They acknowledge that names can differ due to aspects, but the properties themselves are different. They argue that command and prohibition are not relational attributes that depend on an object; rather, they are intrinsic descriptions of speech. Even if we ignore the object, speech remains command or prohibition. Therefore, a single speech cannot be both.

Key Terms
الصفات النفسية
الأمر والنهي
الصفات الإضافية
الخواص المتباينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny all attributes of God.
Correction: They affirm essential attributes but deny contradictory ones in a single essence.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila claim that command and prohibition cannot belong to one speech?
A) Because speech is not an attribute
B) Because they are relational and depend on the object
C) Because they are opposite properties that cannot coexist in one thing
D) Because command and prohibition are not real descriptions

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila object to multiple contradictory attributes in one speech by arguing that command and prohibition are intrinsic, opposite properties that cannot coexist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech could be contradictory, would you still trust His commands and prohibitions absolutely? How does this lesson deepen your certainty that the Quran is consistent and reliable?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that a single speech cannot be both command and prohibition because their properties clash. Reflect on your own speech: when you say one thing and do another, or when you issue mixed signals, what does that say about your integrity? How can you align your words to be as consistent as divine speech?
?
Consider that command and prohibition are intrinsic, not dependent on the object. In your life, do you obey God's commands and avoid His prohibitions as unchanging truths, or do you make them conditional on circumstances? What would it look like to treat them as intrinsic demands?
